North Hempstead v. North Hills


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


38 N.Y.2d 334; 342 N.E.2d 566; 379 N.Y.S.2d 792; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2357; 80 A.L.R.3d 87


November 20, 1975, Argued  
December 29, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, town and individuals, in consolidated appeals, sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's decision to grant defendant village's motion to dismiss separate actions that challenged the constitutionality of zoning ordinance and amendment, and application of the ordinance to a parcel of property.


OVERVIEW: The village had adopted a zoning ordinance and amendment to the ordinance that created a cluster residential district with floating boundaries that was later established by zoning amendments. The town had sought a judgment to declare the ordinance and amendment unconstitutional and enjoining the village from authorizing or permitting construction on any parcel benefited by the ordinance and amendment. The court affirmed the judgment as to the town, finding that it lacked standing to challenge the ordinance because the village had complied with the notice provisions in N.Y. Village Law § 7-706(1). The court affirmed the judgment as to the individuals, finding that limiting the floating zoning to parcels with four or more acres was not exclusionary or unconstitutionally discriminatory. The four-acre minimum made the ordinance available to more of the property owners and only affected seven owners. The court found that the record showed that the ordinance coupled with N.Y. Village Law § 7-704 was nothing but comprehensive planning rather than special interest, irrational ad hocery.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed dismissal of town and individual's action against village zoning ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- right to court review -- Town of North Hempstead lacks standing to challenge zoning ordinance and amendment adopted by Village of North Hills (Village Law, § 7-706, subd 1) -- "floating zone" provision of zoning ordinance not unconstitutionally discriminatory.

1. The Town of North Hempstead lacks standing to challenge a zoning ordinance and amendment adopted by the Village of North Hills. Section 7-706 (subd 1) of the Village Law provides that written notice of any proposed zoning change or amendment affecting property within 500 feet of the boundaries of any town shall be given to the clerk of such town at least 10 days prior to the date of such public hearing and "Such * * * town * * * shall have the right to appear and to be heard at such public hearing with respect to any such proposed change or amendment, but shall not have the right of review by a court as hereinafter provided." The town is prohibited from seeking judicial review by the express terms of the statute.

2. The Board of Trustees of the Village of North Hills, in 1970, established, by ordinance, a "Cluster Residence, R-CL District", a "floating zone" whose boundaries were not fixed in the original ordinance, but were to be established by later amendments to the zoning map. Any property in an R-2 district could potentially be rezoned to R-CL if, among other requirements, a minimum plot size of four acres was provided. That part of the zoning ordinance authorizing the R-CL district as a floating zone is not unconstitutionally discriminatory (cf. Rodgers v Village of Tarrytown, 302 NY 115).

3. The claim that the challenged amendment was not in accord with a comprehensive plan as required by Village Law ( § 7-704) is rejected.

4. There is no merit to petitioners' contention that the individual members of the board acted in an apparent conflict of interest.

 

2 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Leyva


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


38 N.Y.2d 160; 341 N.E.2d 546; 379 N.Y.S.2d 30; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2302


October 23, 1975, Argued  
December 4, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant inmates appealed from the orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed judgments upon verdicts convicting each defendant of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the first degree, and convicting two defendants of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree.


OVERVIEW: All three defendants appeal from separate orders affirming their convictions for criminal possession of dangerous drugs rendered after a joint jury trial. All three were apprehended while they were together inside an automobile that also contained a large quantity of cocaine, stored in a manila envelope underneath the front seat. Their arrest stemmed from information received by police from an informer. The court held that once the prosecution proved defendants were inside a car containing drugs, it was entitled to rely on the presumption as a part of its prima facie case. The court further ruled that given the presence of both defendant and drugs in the car, it was appropriate for the court to permit the jury to consider whether the statutory presumption of possession or defendants' explanation was the more credible and, if the former, whether it was so beyond a reasonable doubt. The court concluded that the jury had a right to find that the inconsistencies in the officers' testimony relied on by defendants were trivial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions finding no error in the use of a statutory presumption regarding drug possession.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- dangerous drugs -- statutory presumption of possession -- where prosecution utilized statutory presumption of knowing fpossession of dangerous drugs by every person in automobile at time drug is found (Penal Law, § 220.25), defendants were properly convicted of criminal possession of dangerous drugs; although sufficient rebuttal evidence may not allow jury to consider statutory inference of possession, defendant's story directed to rebutting any knowing possession was insufficient to achieve such result -- jury had right to find that inconsistencies in police officers' testimony were trivial and therefore insufficient to rebut presumption -- jury charge conveyed correct permissiveness with respect to use of presumption -- no error in withholding identity of police informant.

1. Acting upon a tip from an informer, police officers apprehended defendants while they were together inside an automobile which contained a large quantity of cocaine, found in a container protruding visibly from beneath the front seat. At the trial, the prosecution utilized the statutory presumption of knowing possession of dangerous drugs by every person in an automobile at the time such drug is found, to complete its prima facie case (Penal Law, § 220.25, since amended to conform to the 1973 controlled drug legislation), and defendants were properly convicted of criminal possession of dangerous drugs. Although sufficient rebuttal evidence may not allow the jury to consider the statutory inference of possession, defendant Low's story directed to rebutting any knowing possession was insufficient to achieve such a result. Reasonable persons could differ about whether his explanation for his presence in the car negated possession. Accordingly, given the presence of both defendant and drugs in the car, it was appropriate for the court to permit the jury to consider whether the statutory presumption of possession or Low's explanation was the more credible and, if the former, whether it was so beyond a reasonable doubt.

2. The same conclusion, that it was proper for the jury to consider the statutory presumption of possession, applies with even greater force to the jury's consideration of whether the police officers' testimony was so lacking in credibility as to undermine the factual support their testimony provided for the presumption. On the record here, the jury had a right to find that the inconsistencies in the officers' testimony relied on by the defendants were trivial.

3. The charge of the trial court conveyed the correct permissiveness with respect to the use of a presumption. After detailing the statutory provisions, the court advised the jury that the presumption was not a conclusion, that after considering all the facts, the jury had a right to find that the presumption fails, and above all, that the burden of proving guilt beyond a reasonable doubt stays with the People. Nor is the charge erroneous for failing to state explicitly that the jury could consider the credibility of the prosecution's police witnesses in deciding whether the presumption was rebutted or not. The charge correctly stated that the jury could consider all the evidence in the case in evaluating the presumption.

4. On the facts, there is no reason to overrule the determination made at the suppression hearing that there was a sufficient showing of probable cause without disclosure of the informer. Nor was the trial court incorrect in denying disclosure of the informer's identity since he was in no position to give testimony bearing on the guilt or innocence of the defendants, and therefore the informer privilege does not give way. Defendant Low was not entitled to the identity of the informer so as to use his testimony that Low was neither identified nor described as one of the persons who would be in the car that day, since that proof was already before the jury and conceded by the prosecution.

 

3 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schulman v. New York City Health & Hospitals Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


38 N.Y.2d 234; 342 N.E.2d 501; 379 N.Y.S.2d 702; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2347


October 16, 1975, Argued  
December 4, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a doctor and a patient under an assumed name, challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) that reversed a trial court's decision that declared unconstitutional New York, N.Y., Health Code § 204, which required that a certificate of termination of pregnancy include the name and address of the patient obtaining the abortion.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged the constitutionality of § 204, which required that a certificate of termination of pregnancy, which was required to be filed by abortion patients, include the name and address of the patient obtaining the abortion. The trial court determined that the statute's disclosure requirement was unconstitutional. The appellate court reversed that determination. On appeal, the court ruled: (1) there was no evidence that the statute violated or otherwise interfered with a woman's qualified right to an abortion in that it was intended only to monitor legal abortions and to provide statistical data for the medical community; (2) the disclosure requirement did not impinge a woman's First Amendment rights in that it was narrowly tailored to collect personal information for legitimate state purposes, and; (3) the state's interests in assuring compliance second-trimester abortion procedures, providing medical assistance to indigent patients, and enhancing research were rationally related to the disclosure requirement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order. The statute did not violate any woman's qualified right to an abortion or interfere with her decision to have an abortion, did not violate a woman's right to privacy, was not overbroad, and was rationally related to compelling state interest. The statute was properly upheld as constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Health -- identification of abortion patient -- section 204 of New York City Health Code, which requires disclosure to Department of Health of abortion recipients' names, does not violate qualified right to abortion, since requirement provides for distribution of services and for safety without dissuading women from having abortions or breaching confidentiality of disclosures; physician-patient relationship is not interfered with, but such relationship is provided to indigents through health counseling programs; doctrine of facial overbreadth should not be extended beyond application to ordinances governing freedom of expression -- right to privacy is not violated, since such extends only to freedom of expression and guarantee against self incrimination; regulation is narrowly tailored for legitimate purposes and avoids transgression into decision making in one's personal life -- statute is not overbroad and rationally relates to compelling State interest and, absent interference with right to privacy, availability of more effective means to secure same objectives does not result in invalidation.

1. Section 204 of the New York City Health Code, which requires that hospitals report to the New York City Department of Health terminations of pregnancy according to Board of Health forms which require the inclusion of abortion patients' names, does not violate a woman's qualified right to an abortion or interfere with her decision to have an abortion. The reporting requirement was designed to monitor the safety and effectiveness of legal abortions and to provide for planning of service and to facilitate distribution and availability by providing a broad statistical sampling of data which is unavailable to a private physician or with respect to indigent women who do not have private physicians. There is no showing that the name requirement dissuades potential abortion recipients from having an abortion and confidentiality of the information is adequately insured. Health counseling programs facilitated by the reports are intended not to interfere with the physician-patient relationship but to provide such a relationship for indigent women. There is no authority for extending the doctrine of facial overbreadth beyond its application to ordinances governing freedom of expression.

2. Disclosure of the names of abortion recipients does not violate the right to privacy, which extends only to areas involving freedom of association and the guarantee against self incrimination. The city's motives in gathering the information are not suspect. The regulation is narrowly tailored to collect personal information for legitimate purposes and avoids unconstitutional governmental transgression into decision making in one's personal life and those places where one's personal life is conducted.

3. The statute is not overbroad and is rationally related to the compelling State interest in assuring compliance with second trimester abortion procedures, providing medical assistance to abortion patients and advancing medical research. Absent interference with the right to privacy, the availability of other, more effective means to secure the same objectives does not invalidate the statute. Accordingly, the statute is constitutional.

 

4 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re WALTEMADE


STATE OF NEW YORK COURT ON THE JUDICIARY


1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2421



December 1, 1975 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court was convened to hear the charges and investigate the allegations of judicial misconduct levied against respondent N.Y. Supreme Court Justice (judge). The judge's motion to strike the charges as unconstitutional and his motion that the court vacate its order designating a referee was denied. The judge's motion that the court hear the evidence en banc in the first instance was also denied.


OVERVIEW: The charges against the judge alleged that he was so lacking in judicial temperament and understanding of proper judicial conduct that he was unfit to continue to serve as a Supreme Court justice. The judge's defense was that even if the charges were true, they did not constitute grounds for his removal. After an extensive investigation, a hearing was held before a referee. The referee filed a report with the court, sustaining certain charges against the judge. The court heard arguments by counsel for the court and for the judge. The court held that the record of the judge's repeated and unrelenting display of unjudicial temperament compelled the court to conclude that he grossly violated reasonable concepts of judicial behavior. The court found the judge to be in violation of several canons of judicial ethics and determined him to be guilty of the misconduct charges. The court further held that N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 22 empowered the court to make its own rules and procedures for the investigation of misconduct, the judge was given a full opportunity to present his evidence and arguments in opposition to the charges, and the record before the court was adequate.


OUTCOME: The court determined the judge to be guilty of misconduct and censured him in light of the fact that he was at the end of his term of office.

 

5 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Lalli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


38 N.Y.2d 77; 340 N.E.2d 721; 378 N.Y.S.2d 351; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2244


October 14, 1975, Argued  
November 25, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant illegitimate son sought review of a decree of the Westchester County Surrogate's Court (New York), which granted respondent administratrix's motion to dismiss an application by the illegitimate son for a compulsory accounting of the deceased father's estate.


OVERVIEW: In response to the illegitimate son's (son) application for a compulsory accounting, the administratrix filed a motion to dismiss and argued that the son was not a distributee and hence had no standing to compel an accounting. The surrogate court granted the administratrix's motion to dismiss on the ground that the son was appellant was not a distributee under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts § 4-1.2 (a)(2). In so doing, the surrogate court rejected the son's contention that § 4-1.2(a)(2) was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed the decree and found that § 4-1.2 (a)(2) was not unconstitutional to the extent that it prescribed the entry, during the father's lifetime, of an order of filiation declaring paternity as a condition precedent for inheritance by son from his father. The court reasoned that, given the practical problems and difficulties associated with proof of fatherhood, the legislature had acted rationally when it established a different standard for proving fatherhood for purposes of intestacy than that required to establish motherhood. Accordingly, the decree of the surrogate court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The decree of the surrogate court, which granted the administratrix's motion to dismiss the legitimate son's application for a compulsory accounting of the deceased father's estate, was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Descent and distribution -- illegitimate child -- EPTL 4-1.2 (subd [a], par [2]), which provides that illegitimate child is legitimate child of his father for inheritance if court, during lifetime of father, has made order of filiation, is not unconstitutional.

1. EPTL 4-1.2 (subd [a], par [2]), insofar as it provides that "An illegitimate child is the legitimate child of his father so that he and his issue inherit from his father if a court of competent jurisdiction has, during the lifetime of the father, made an order of filiation declaring paternity", is not unconstitutional.

2. The test applicable to the kind of expectancy here involved is whether there is a rational basis for the means chosen by the Legislature for the accomplishment of a permissible State objective.

3. The fact that the statute ( EPTL 4-1.2, subd [a], par [1]) provides that an illegitimate child is the legitimate child of its mother for purpose of inheritance is not unconstitutionally discriminatory. Since the identification of a natural mother is both easier and more conclusive than the identification of a natural father, the Legislature acted rationally in prescribing for sound and understandable reasons a specially defined procedure for establishing the fact of fatherhood.

4. It is not irrational to require the formality of a court order adjudicating the fact of paternity.

5. It is not irrational or unreasonable to require that the order of filiation must be made during the lifetime of the natural father -- to require that he should have personal opportunity to participate, if he chooses, in the procedure by which the fact of his fatherhood is established.

 

6 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Montgomery v. Daniels


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


38 N.Y.2d 41; 340 N.E.2d 444; 378 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2242


September 3, 1975, Argued  
November 25, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, without costs, summary judgment granted to appellants and case remitted to Supreme Court, Kings County, for entry of judgment declaring the rights of the parties in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurers and their insureds challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, entered in Kings County (New York), which granted plaintiffs' summary judgment and which declared that article 18 of the N.Y. Insurance Law was unconstitutional and invalid. The court also denied defendants' and intervenors' motions to dismiss plaintiffs' personal injury complaint.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were injured in separate automobile accidents, which occurred after the enactment of article 18 of the New York insurance law, the Comprehensive Automobile Insurance Reparations Act. Plaintiffs joined together against their respective tort-feasors and insurers and sought a declaration that the Act was unconstitutional. Plaintiffs claimed that because the reasonable and customary charges for medical treatment for their injuries would not exceed $ 500, the Act impermissibly barred them from commencing an action for their injuries. The lower court held that the Act was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that the classifications created by the legislature were reasonably related to the end of establishing a rational line of distinction. The court concluded that the action involved economic legislation and that modern substantive due process principles required the judiciary to give deference to the legislature in that area. Accordingly, the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decision and determined that the statute was valid and constitutional. Accordingly, the court granted defendants' summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- no-fault automobile insurance -- no-fault automobile accident compensation law (Insurance Law, art 18) does not violate due process or equal protection clauses of State or Federal Constitution, since it is reasonably related to accomplishment of permissible State objectives -- article 18 does not unconstitutionally abrogate common-law right to sue in tort, since Legislature may alter common law, and any requirement of adequate substitute for rights abrogated is met -- term "significant disfigurement" is not unconstitutionally vague -- reasonable basis standard, rather than strict scrutiny test, should be used, since no fundamental right is denied -- there is rational basis for exclusion of noncovered persons from no-fault coverage -- threshold monetary requirements and list of injuries constituting serious injury are reasonably related to establishment of rational line of distinction -- threshold monetary requirement is properly based upon "reasonable and customary" charges for medical services since mathematical exactitude is not required and, under such general standard, actual payments are not determinative -- right to trial by jury is not abrogated, since article 18 modifies substantive law, such that there is nothing to which right may attach -- accordingly, article 18 of Insurance Law is not unconstitutional and summary judgment should be granted to defendants.

1. The partial abolition under article 18 of the Insurance Law of an automobile accident victim's right to sue for damages for another's negligent action does not deprive the victim of a right or interest protected under the due process clauses of either the State or Federal Constitution. The New York no-fault automobile accident compensation law is reasonably related to the accomplishment of the permissible State objective under the police power of regulating motor vehicle use and liability for the public welfare. Article 18 has the objectives of reducing economic pressure on seriously injured persons to compromise down their claims, while reducing pressure on insurers to compromise upward minor claims to avoid investigative and defense expenses, eliminating expenditures extraneous to treatment of injuries, guaranteeing prompt payment for economic losses and lessening the burden on the courts. There is presumptively no deprivation of due process under a statute challenged on nonprocedural grounds until the court is satisfied to the contrary, and, where the factual basis for the exercise of police power is fairly debatable and there has been extensive legislative investigation, the opinion of the Legislature regarding a legitimate and established area of police power will be accepted and the wisdom of its methods reasonably related thereto will not be questioned.

2. Article 18 of the Insurance Law does not unconstitutionally abrogate the common-law right to sue in tort. The New York State Constitution (art I, § 4) recognizes the power of the Legislature to alter the common law and, if any adequate substitute for abrogated rights were required, whereby the new system would be measured against the common-law system, without regard to the relative treatment of any particular claimant under the two systems, no issue would be presented in relation thereto, since a more than adequate substitute is provided.

3. The term "significant disfigurement" (Insurance Law, § 671, subd 4, par [a]), as used to define "serious injury", is not unconstitutionally vague, since a sufficiently accurate concept is conveyed to any interested person and prior statutory use of the term has caused no difficulty.

4. The strict scrutiny standard of review should not be applied to the plaintiff's equal protection arguments challenging article 18 of the Insurance Law, since such test should be invoked only when a challenged law creates suspect classifications or impairs a fundamental constitutional right. No right of "security of the person" is interfered with, since the economic remedy for injury to the person has simply been altered. No fundamental constitutional right of "access to the courts" has been denied, since that right does not exist independent of an underlying fundamental right sought to be vindicated and for which there is no alternative forum. Rather than denying access to the courts, article 18 merely eliminates partially the judicial recovery for injuries suffered. Accordingly, the rational basis standard of review should be applied.

5. There is a rational basis for the exclusion of noncovered persons from the operation of article 18. The exclusion of motorcycles is rationally based upon evidence that the premium cost for first-party no-fault coverage would have been prohibitively high, and the exclusion, accordingly, relates to the legislative purpose of reducing insurance costs. Exclusion of operators of uninsured vehicles is not unreasonable, since such persons do not contribute to the compensation system. The exclusion of out-of-State vehicles without New York no-fault coverage is in recognition of the inability of the Legislature realistically to exercise power over such persons.

6. Although certain injuries not listed in the definition of serious injury (Insurance Law, § 671, subd 4) might have been included and the threshold monetary requirement might have been different, the classifications created are reasonably related to the legislative purpose of establishing a rational line of distinction.

7. The computation of the threshold requirement for serious injury is rationally based on treatment expenses rather than basic economic loss, since use of the latter would favor those with higher earned income.

8. The threshold amount is properly based upon "reasonable and customary" charges for medical services, although such may vary among localities, since the legislative standard is not required to be mathematically exact. The standard is general and the actual payments made are not determinative.

9. The plaintiffs are not denied the right to trial by jury, since article 18 modifies the substantive law and the rights of automobile accident victims, rather than replacing the jury as the trier of fact, and to the extent of the modification nothing remains to which the right to trial by jury may attach. It is the basic function of the Legislature to remedy defects in the common law and, if a cause of action triable by jury could not be limited or abolished, that function would be seriously impaired. Partial abrogation produces no different result, since, pro tanto, there is nothing for the jury to try.

10. Accordingly, article 18 of the Insurance Law is not unconstitutional and summary judgment should be granted to the defendants.

 

7 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Daniels


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 624; 339 N.E.2d 139; 376 N.Y.S.2d 436; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2198


September 17, 1975, Argued  
October 30, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) affirming judgments convicting them of criminal possession of a dangerous drug and criminally using drug paraphernalia. Defendants contended that the testimony of an accomplice was unsupported by corroborating evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted after a jury trial of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the third and fourth degrees and of two counts of criminally using drug paraphernalia in the second degree. The appellate division affirmed. Defendants appealed, contending that the testimony of an accomplice was uncorroborated. In affirming, the court concluded that the trial court did not err in instructing the jurors that if they believed police testimony that there was contraband on the kitchen table when they entered the apartment, then the testimony of the accomplice had been sufficiently corroborated. In light of the evidence, this was ample corroboration. The contraband was found in the apartment of one defendant, and the other defendants were inside the apartment. The narcotics were found in open view in a room on private premises, and those persons in close proximity to the drugs at the time of discovery were statutorily presumed to have knowing possession. While this presumption was rebuttable, the jury was authorized, after hearing all the evidence, to draw from the presence of defendants at the place of discovery the logical inference that they were guilty of criminal possession.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate division affirming defendants' convictions was affirmed as defendants' presence at the location where the narcotics were discovered by police raised a presumption that they were in knowing possession of the drugs and sufficiently corroborated the accomplice testimony.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- corroboration of accomplice testimony -- evidence that large supply of drugs was found on kitchen table in apartment of one of three defendants, that he was observed leaving apartment just prior to arrest, and that other two defendants were found inside apartment, was sufficient to supply required corroboration of testimony given by codefendant, who had previously pleaded guilty, that one of three defendants was his partner in drug trade and that they employed other two to distribute drugs and handle telephone calls regarding drugs.

Three defendants were convicted by a jury of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the third and fourth degrees and criminally using drug paraphernalia. At their trial, a codefendant, who had previously pleaded guilty, testified that one of them was his partner in the drug trade and that they employed the other two to distribute the drugs and to handle telephone calls regarding the drugs. In addition to the testimony of this witness, who was an accomplice as a matter of law, there was other evidence that a large supply of drugs was found on the kitchen table in the apartment of the accomplice's alleged partner; that the latter was observed leaving the apartment just prior to his arrest, and that the other two convicted defendants were found inside the apartment. This additional proof tended to connect the convicted defendants with the commission of the offense, and, therefore, was sufficient to supply the required corroboration of the accomplice's testimony.

 

8 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Slavin v. Ingraham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 653; 339 N.E.2d 157; 376 N.Y.S.2d 463; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2203


September 9, 1975, Argued  
October 28, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Commissioner of Health of the State of New York and others, challenged a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which, in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, granted respondent landowners' petition and annulled the commissioner's determination imposing fines and penalties upon the landowners for violations of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116.


OVERVIEW: The landowners deeded to various individuals a number of parcels. The commissioner instituted an administrative proceeding alleging that the conveyances were in violation of N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116. The commissioner found that the landowners had divided a tract of land into five or more parcels along an existing or proposed street, highway, easement, or right-of-way, for sale or for rent as residential lots or as residential building plots and had sold or offered for sale one or more of such parcels without having a plan of such subdivision filed with and approved by the Department of Health and without having filed a plan or map in the office of the county clerk. On appeal, the court found that the appellate court was correct in its holding that the commissioner's determination was not supported by substantial evidence. The court found that the deeds contained no restrictions limiting the parcels to residential uses and there were no zoning regulations during the years in question affecting their enjoyment. The record was barren of proof that the landowners sold the pieces of realty for residential purposes or held themselves out as subdividers of the land for said purposes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court, which annulled the commissioner's determination imposing fines and penalties upon the landowners for violations of New York Public Health Law, relative to their sale of various parcels of real estate.


HEADNOTES

Health -- subdivision map -- determination assessing penalties against petitioners for subdividing tract into five or more parcels for sale as "residential lots" or "residential building plots" without filing subdivision map ( Public Health Law, §§ 1115, 1116) is not supported by substantial evidence; deeds from petitioners did not limit parcels to residential uses, there were no zoning regulations affecting their enjoyment, and there was no proof that petitioners sold parcels for residential purposes or held themselves out as subdividers for such purposes.

The determination of the State Commissioner of Health assessing penalties against petitioners, following charges that petitioners had subdivided a tract of land into five or more parcels for sale as residential lots or residential building plots and had sold or offered for sale one or more such parcels without filing a subdivision plan therefor ( Public Health Law, § 1116), is not supported by substantial evidence. The terms "residential lots" and "residential building plots" as used in section 1115 of the Public Health Law are not defined. However, the deeds from petitioners contain no restrictions limiting the parcels to residential uses, there were no zoning regulations affecting their enjoyment and there was no proof that petitioners sold the parcels singly or collectively for residential purposes or held themselves out as subdividers for such purposes. The use of the land by the grantees, in the absence of proof connecting that use with petitioners, does not constitute evidence binding on petitioners to the effect that the original tract was divided for sale as "residential lots" or "residential building plots".

 

9 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dairylea Cooperative, Inc. v. Walkley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


38 N.Y.2d 6; 339 N.E.2d 865; 377 N.Y.S.2d 451; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2215


September 3, 1975, Argued  
October 28, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Albany County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant milk dealer sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which denied its application for a judgment annulling a determination of appellee commissioner.


OVERVIEW: The milk dealer sought review of a determination of the commissioner that granted another milk dealer's application for an extension of its milk license to include a larger sales area. The trial court concluded that the milk dealer was not arguably within the zone of interests to be protected by N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. § 258 and consequently lacked standing. The milk dealer contended that its interests fell within § 258 because § 258 was designed to regulate the milk producing industry by preventing practices that were unfair and destructive of competition. The court held that the milk dealer had standing because a fundamental tenet of the system of remedies was that when a government agency sought to act in a manner adversely affecting a party, judicial review of that action was proper. The court held that there was no legislative intent that negated the milk dealer's standing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court because the milk dealer had proper standing to seek judicial review.


HEADNOTES

Agriculture -- milk control -- extension of dealer's license -- standing to challenge -- petitioner, licensed milk dealer, has standing to challenge by judicial review determination of Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets granting respondent's application for extension of its license to include sales area served by petitioner; Agriculture and Markets Law reflects legislative purpose to prevent destructive competition and petitioner, as injured competitor, may require compliance with that statute -- judicial review is limited.

1. Petitioner, a licensed milk dealer, has standing to challenge a determination of the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets granting respondent milk association's application for an extension of its license to include a sales area presently served by petitioner. While petitioner may not challenge the determination on due process grounds or by specific statutory right, when a government agency acts in a manner adversely affecting a party, judicial review may be had. There is no underlying legislative intent to preclude review and, although petitioner lacks the right to intervene in the department proceedings, that does not preclude such review. The Agriculture and Markets Law reflects an overriding legislative purpose to prevent destructive competition and petitioner, as an injured competitor, has standing to require compliance with that statute, particularly since the agency may act without a hearing.

2. The judicial review in this proceeding will be limited, considering the fact that the commissioner must issue a license or extension unless he finds by a preponderance of evidence that the applicant is unqualified or that the issuance will tend to a destructive competition or be contrary to public interest.

 

10 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cassidy v. Municipal Civil Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 526; 337 N.E.2d 752; 375 N.Y.S.2d 300; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2182


September 8, 1975, Argued  
October 21, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Westchester County (New York) in his N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding that adjudged N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 61 was constitutional, and that dismissed his action challenging the methods of appointing persons to the position of housing code enforcement supervisor. A direct appeal was obtained pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5601(b)(2).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenged respondent civil service commission's hiring process after he was the highest individual on the eligibility list, but was not given the municipal housing code enforcement supervisor position. Petitioner alleged that the commission had to give him the job and that the hiring process violated N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6 and due process. The court affirmed the denial of his request for relief under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78. N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 61 allowed any of the top three persons to be given the job and was consistent with N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6. The constitution did not require that the person with the highest score on the eligibility list be given the job, for to an extent the questions of merit and fitness were to be determined by an examination, but it was impracticable to fully determine the merit and fitness of an employee or appointee by a mere examination. The commission had to be cloaked with the power to choose a qualified candidate that possessed all attributes necessary for the responsible performance of the duties. The court found no due process violation, for until the position was awarded to petitioner, he had no protectable interest, only a hope.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the supreme court that found the civil service laws that applied to the hiring process for municipal appointments constitutional, and that dismissed petitioner's action, was affirmed, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- civil service -- New York State Constitution (art V, § 6) does not require appointment of individual with highest mark on eligible list -- Civil Service Law ( § 61, subd 1) which provides that appointment may be made from one of three persons standing highest on eligible list is consistent with Constitution -- no due process guarantee was denied.

1. Petitioner received the highest rank in a promotional examination but the second highest ranking candidate was chosen for the position. The New York State constitutional provision (art V, § 6) does not require the selection of the highest individual on the eligible list. It provides that merit and fitness be ascertained "as far as practicable" by examination. It was not intended that examination scores be the sole determinant of fitness.

2. The Civil Service Law ( § 61, subd 1), which provides that one of the three persons standing highest on the eligible list may be appointed, is consistent with the constitutional provision. The appointing authority is given the power to choose the qualified appointees who possess all the attributes necessary for the position -- not merely the ability to rank highest on the test.

3. No alleged right of petitioner is of sufficient substance to merit the protection of the constitutional due process guarantees.

 

11 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Taub


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 530; 337 N.E.2d 754; 375 N.Y.S.2d 303; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2183


September 12, 1975, Argued  
October 21, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of New York (state) challenged a decision from the Erie County Court (New York), which reversed a city court's decision to convict defendant for using sound amplification equipment on a public street corner without first having obtained the permit required by Buffalo, N.Y., Ordinances ch. XXV, § 1703 (ordinance).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a candidate for a local school board. The city court convicted defendant of using sound amplification equipment on a public street corner without obtaining a permit as required by the ordinance. The county court reversed her conviction because the ordinance was an unconstitutional infringement of her First Amendment rights. The state appealed, and the court affirmed. The court held that the ordinance was unconstitutional as written. The ordinance contained provisions that impermissibly impinged upon the rights of free speech and assembly. The ordinance cast a chill over the freedom of speech because it required persons desiring permits to apply for them at least five days in advance. The ordinance was overbroad because it required the disclosure of every person who was to speak through the equipment. Finally, the ordinance was a prior restraint on speech because it mandated the denial of a permit if any person involved was previously convicted of a crime or of disorderly conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the county court which reversed defendant's conviction for using sound amplification equipment on a public street corner without first having obtained a permit as required by the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- sound amplification equipment -- Ordinance of City of Buffalo (ch XXV, § 1703), which requires permit to use sound amplification equipment on public street, is unconstitutional -- provisions requiring application for permit to be made at least five days before equipment is to be used, requiring that name of every person who will speak through equipment be disclosed on application, denying permits when any person involved has been convicted of crime or disorderly conduct, and requiring that nature of subject matter to be broadcast must be described, infringe upon rights of free speech and assembly.

1. Respondent was convicted for using sound amplification equipment on a public street corner without first having obtained the permit required by a City of Buffalo Ordinance (ch XXV, § 1703). The ordinance, as presently written, is unconstitutional.

2. Those parts of the ordinance which list the times and places where sound equipment may not be used and define criteria, such as congested traffic conditions, street repairs, or overcrowding caused by overlap of permits, to guide the police in determining whether considerations of public safety are extensive enough to permit limitations on the granting of a permit in the first instance, are sufficiently specific.

3. But in other respects the ordinance contains provisions which infringe upon the rights of free speech and assembly: (a) It requires persons desiring permits to apply for them at least five days before they are to be used. The purposes of publicizing views might be frustrated by the need to wait five days. Only an administrative review of denials is provided for and no time limit for such review is specified. (b) The ordinance requires that the name of every person who will speak through the equipment be disclosed on the application; it further orders that permits be denied when any person involved has been convicted of a crime or of disorderly conduct. The result is to bar free speech. Denial of a permit on the ground that a person has been convicted of a crime or disorderly conduct is not permissible -- it also constitutes a prior restraint on freedom of speech. (c) The ordinance requires that applicants for permits describe the nature of the subject matter to be broadcast. This, too, constitutes a form of prior restraint, and it also puts excessive discretion to decide what may be broadcast over amplifying equipment in the hands of the police.

 

12 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Boston Stock Exchange v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 535; 337 N.E.2d 758; 375 N.Y.S.2d 308; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2184


September 4, 1975, Argued  
October 21, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, stock exchanges, sought review of a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which directed entry of a judgment declaring N.Y. Tax Law § 270-a valid and constitutional, modifying a trial court's denial of a motion by defendant state tax commission (Commission) to dismiss a complaint brought by the stock exchanges for a declaration that § 270-a was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The stock exchanges brought an action against the Commission, seeking a judgment declaring N.Y. Tax Law § 270-a unconstitutional on the grounds that it denied the stock exchanges equal protection of the laws and discriminated against interstate commerce in violation of U.S. Const. art I, § 8. The trial court granted the Commission's motion to dismiss the action. An intermediate court modified the trial court's ruling and directed a judgment declaring § 270-a valid and constitutional. The stock exchanges appealed, contending that § 270-a discriminated against interstate commerce by encouraging nonresidents to sell New York securities on New York exchanges. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the statute was valid and constitutional. Section 270a distinguished between residents and nonresidents of New York in favor of the latter as well as the former. The stated legislative goal of § 270-a, to neutralize the economic advantage conferred by a prior version of the tax on exchanges located outside the state, was a valid one. A sale by a nonresident on a New York exchange -- the type of transaction the law allegedly encouraged -- was still interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, with costs, the intermediate court's judgment, which modified, by granting in part the Commission's motion to dismiss the action brought by the stock exchanges, the trial court's denial of the Commission's motion to dismiss the action brought by the stock exchanges.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- stock transfer tax -- sales by nonresidents -- section 270-a of Tax Law, which provides, with respect to sales of stock within New York State by nonresidents, 50% reduction in stock transfer tax (Tax Law, § 270) and $ 350 maximum tax on sales of large blocks of stock, does not violate equal protection clause by arbitrarily making amount of tax depend on place of sale, since full tax is payable, notwithstanding place of sale, unless seller is nonresident, and distinction with respect to nonresidents is justifiable, since in-State transactions are less susceptible to tax evasion -- statute does not discriminate against interstate commerce by encouraging nonresidents to sell New York securities in New York, since such sale is in interstate commerce, and, with respect to non-New York securities, out-of-State exchanges retain their advantage.

1. Section 270-a of the Tax Law, which provides with respect to sales of stock within New York State by nonresidents a 50% reduction in the stock transfer tax due under section 270 of the Tax Law and a $ 350 maximum tax on sales of large blocks of stock, does not violate the equal protection clause by arbitrarily making the amount of the tax depend on the place of sale. The full tax is payable, notwithstanding place of sale, unless the seller is a nonresident, and the distinction between in-State and out-of-State sales of New York securities by nonresidents is justifiable on the ground that in-State transactions are less susceptible to tax evasion.

2. The statute does not discriminate against interstate commerce in favor of intrastate commerce; rather, it neutralizes an advantage conferred upon out-of-State stock exchanges by the prior taxing scheme. By encouraging nonresidents to sell New York securities in New York, the statute does not discriminate against interstate commerce in favor of intrastate commerce, since such a sale is in interstate commerce, and, with respect to non-New York securities, the out-of-State exchanges retain their advantage.

 

13 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sgaglione v. Levitt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 507; 337 N.E.2d 592; 375 N.Y.S.2d 79; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2121


September 26, 1975, Argued  
September 29, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Judgment modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant class of civil service employees and organizations challenged an order by the trial court (New York), which denied their motions for injunctions, summary judgment, and for declaratory rulings that the New York State Financial Emergency Act was unconstitutional, invalid, and violative of N.Y. Const. art. I., § 7 and art. V, § 6.


OVERVIEW: The class claimed that N. Y. Const. art. I., § 7 and art. V, § 6 were violated by the State Financial Emergency Act (Act) because it allowed appellee comptroller, as trustee of certain employees' retirement funds, unlimited power over those funds. The court agreed and it modified the judgment. It held that the pension fund of the Employees Retirement System was the property of its member, and as such it could not have been taken without the due process of law. Moreover, the comptroller's decision to invest a small portion of the assets of the retirement fund in bonds would not per se have constituted an impairment of the benefits of the members of the retirement system. However, the comptroller, as the trustee of the retirement funds being vested with the discretion to invest in such securities as are authorized by statute, constituted a violation of the nonimpairment clause because the means designed to assure benefits to public employees and those already retired would have been impaired by the Act. The court held that the judgment had to be modified to declare § 14 unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment, without assessing costs to either party. It found that the comptroller's investment of the retired class members' pension funds violated the New York Constitution's Impairment clause.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- impairment of retirement benefits -- section 14 of New York State Financial Emergency Act for City of New York, which mandates State Comptroller to purchase bonds of Municipal Assistance Corporation for City of New York from funds of State retirement systems, violates section 7 of article V of State Constitution, prohibiting impairment of benefits of State retirement systems; Legislature may not strip Comptroller of his discretion in making investments of retirement funds.

1. Section 14 of the New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868, 869, 870), which mandates the State Comptroller to purchase at face value bonds of the Municipal Assistance Corporation for the City of New York (MAC) in the amount of $ 125,000,000 from the funds of the State Employees' Retirement System and the State Patrolmen's and Firemen's Retirement System, violates section 7 of article V of the New York State Constitution, prohibiting impairment of benefits of any retirement system of the State or civil division thereof. Although the constitutional provision is limited to "benefits" to which members of a retirement system are entitled, that necessarily implies protection of the sources of funds for those benefits. While the Legislature may expand or restrict the classes of investments the Comptroller may make, it is powerless under the constitutional provision to mandate that he mindlessly invest in whatever securities it directs. To strip the Comptroller, who is solely vested with discretion to make investments, of his personal responsibility is to remove a safeguard integral to the maintenance of the security of the sources of benefits.

2. It is immaterial whether the mandate is addressed to a small proportion or to a substantial part of the funds in the care of the Comptroller.

3. Whether the retirement benefits will be sufficiently protected by the assigned existing taxes to secure MAC bonds or will be protected by future tax levies of the State and its subdivisions need not be considered. The benefits are already protected by the tax levying power.

People v. Acme Markets, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 326; 334 N.E.2d 555; 372 N.Y.S.2d 590; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2009


April 28, 1975, Argued  
July 10, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant food supermarkets were charged with violation of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 9 for selling prohibited items on Sunday. The Erie County Court (New York) affirmed a trial court decision convicting defendants of violating the law. Defendants challenged the decision and the trial court's denial of their motions to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: Defendants maintained that they had been subject to discriminatory enforcement in violation of the equal protection clause of the state and federal constitutions and that the statute was facially invalid. The court reversed the convictions and held that the actions of the prosecutor clearly evidenced discriminatory enforcement. The complaints received about Sunday sales were instituted by union representatives and were made only against certain food supermarkets. The union was responding to member complaints about work on Sundays and the cut in total work hours for senior members caused by additional hiring of part-time employees. The court noted that § 9 had never been actively enforced and was only prosecuted upon the complaint of a private party. The union's effort to manipulate the law for purely private purposes clearly evidenced discriminatory enforcement. While discriminatory enforcement was limited to State action, the record showed that the power of the prosecutor's office was lent to the union in their campaign and their actions became those of the State for purposes of the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art I, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions of defendants for violation of the Sunday sales law.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sunday sales law -- discriminatory enforcement -- prosecution of defendant supermarkets under Sunday sales law at instance of private interest group constitutes discrimination violative of equal protection, where there has been history of disuse of statute and absent policy of general enforcement, since exercise of prosecutional discretion constitutes State action and criminal justice should be equally dispensed -- Sunday sales law does not unconstitutionally establish religion -- accordingly, orders affirming judgments of conviction should be reversed and informations dismissed.

1. Prosecution of the defendant supermarkets under the Sunday sales law (General Business Law, § 9) at the instance of an interest group for its private purposes constitutes discrimination violative of equal protection, where there has been a history of disuse of the statute and absent a policy of general enforcement. It had been the policy over a period of years to prosecute violations only on complaints by private citizens, and in the instant case supermarkets were singled out among food markets for prosecution by an employee union to deal with a reduction in hours caused by Sunday sales. The exercise of prosecutional discretion in such circumstances constitutes State action and brings the equal protection guarantee into play. Criminal justice should be equally dispensed.

2. The Sunday sales law does not unconstitutionally establish religion.

3. Accordingly, the orders affirming judgments of conviction should be reversed and the informations dismissed.

 

15 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Yonkers Community Development Agency v. Morris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 478; 335 N.E.2d 327; 373 N.Y.S.2d 112; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2049


June 11, 1975, Argued  
July 10, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) affirmed judgments entered in condemnation proceedings, granting judgment of condemnation to plaintiff city and directing that the city be permitted to enter immediately upon the condemned property. Defendants, tenants and landowners in the area selected for redevelopment, appealed.


OVERVIEW: The landowners' denied that the land was substandard and charged that it was to be taken, cleared and provided for a private purpose, the expansion of a leading industrial employer in the city. The court held it was unnecessary, as a precondition to the taking, to determine that the public benefit in assuring the retention of the private employer was sufficient to outweigh the benefit that was conferred on the employer. There was nothing inherently wrong in serving both the city's need for renewal of its substandard land and its desire to keep the employer. The city's bare pleading of its "substandard" finding, failed to indicate the grounds upon which it concluded that the land was substandard. However, the landowners failed to raise properly by their pleadings the issue of the quality of the taken land. In the meantime, the taking had occurred without hindrance, the buildings were demolished, and the owners sought not, in effect, the retention of their properties but the wreaking of some kind of vengeance on the city.


OUTCOME: The order of condemnation was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- urban renewal -- removal of substandard conditions, including economic underdevelopment and stagnation, is sufficiently dominant public purpose to justify condemnation of defendants' land by plaintiff community development agency, and fact that vehicle for renewed land use is private agency does not alter permissible nature of taking -- although plaintiff failed to indicate grounds upon which it concluded that area was substandard, defendant landowners failed to properly raise issue of quality of land taken, while in meantime buildings have been destroyed, and landowners are no longer entitled to relief.

1. Removal of substandard conditions is a sufficiently dominant public purpose to justify the exercise of the condemnation powers of government by the plaintiff community development agency, and economic underdevelopment and stagnation will render an area eligible for such urban renewal. The fact that the vehicle for the renewed use of land is a private agency, in this case a corporation engaged in manufacturing, does not change the permissible nature of the taking of substandard property, notwithstanding that the interests of the private party are served by the taking.

2. Although substandard or "blighted" land should be liberally defined to include areas of improper land use and unwise planning, with wide agency discretion to decide what constitutes "blight", the facts supporting such a determination should be set forth. The plaintiff community development agency failed to indicate the grounds upon which it concluded that the defendants' land was substandard and upon which it would have entitled it to summary judgment unless such grounds were shown by the landowners to be without foundation. However, the defendant landowners have failed to properly raise the issue of the quality of the land taken, while in the meantime the buildings have been destroyed, and the defendants are no longer entitled to relief.

Victorson v. Bock Laundry Machine Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 395; 335 N.E.2d 275; 373 N.Y.S.2d 39; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2040; 91 A.L.R.3d 445


April 29, 1975, Argued  
July 2, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed, with costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants in three separate cases appealed decisions of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department, holding that claims for strict liability accrued at the time of injury and that, therefore, the three year statute of limitations under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214 (4) and (5) applied.


OVERVIEW: Defendants in three separate cases appealed decisions by the court of appeals which held that claims for strict products liability accrued at the time of injury and that, therefore, the three year statute of limitations under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 214 (4) and (5) applied. The court affirmed, holding that strict products liability sounds in tort rather than in contract and that, thus, the tort statute of limitations must be applied. Therefore, the court concluded that the three-year period of limitation with respect to plaintiffs' causes of action for personal injuries stemming from defendants allegedly defective products began to run at the date of injury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate division's judgment, holding that strict products liability sounds in tort rather than in contract and that, thus, the tort statute of limitations applied.


HEADNOTES

Limitations of actions -- strict products liability -- period of limitations with respect to strict products liability claims by remote users for injuries arising out of defective products begins to run at time of injury, since cause of action sounds in tort rather than contract -- period of limitations should be three-year period applicable to negligence actions.

1. The period of limitation with respect to strict products liability claims by remote users for injuries arising out of defective products begins to run at the time of injury. Strict products liability sounds in tort rather than contract, since liability is based on social policy rather than a jural relationship entered into between two parties prior to injury, and, accordingly, the cause of action should accrue at the time of injury. While the passage of time may decrease a manufacturer's capability to defend, it complicates the plaintiff's problem of proving that the defect existed when the product left the manufacturer's plant.

2. No material factors suggest that the period of limitations should be different for strict products liability than for negligence and the three-year period in CPLR 214 (subds 4, 5) should apply.

Boryszewski v. Brydges


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 361; 334 N.E.2d 579; 372 N.Y.S.2d 623; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2012


May 8, 1975, Argued  
July 2, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent state legislators sought review from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed, on the law and the facts, an order denying a motion to dismiss a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 with respect to the individual petitioner taxpayers, who sought a declaration that the state budget provision for legislators' pensions and fringe benefits was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The appellate division dismissed taxpayers' constitutional challenge to the legislators' pension provision of the state budget bill on the ground that St. Clair v Yonkers Raceway, 13 N.Y. 2d 72, narrowly limited persons having capacity to make such challenges to those personally aggrieved thereby, and then only if the determination of the grievance required a determination of constitutionality. The court overruled that decision, holding that the time had come to bring the state's practice with respect to review of state legislative action into conformity not only with the practice in the majority of other states but also with the procedural standing of taxpayers to challenge local actions. The court found that the failure to accord such standing would be in effect to erect an impenetrable barrier to any judicial scrutiny of legislative action. Therefore, the motion to dismiss should not have been granted, but the lower courts should have issued a declaratory judgment on the merits rejecting taxpayers' contentions that the legislative and executive retirement plan violated provisions of the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that there was no substance to taxpayers' claims that the executive and legislative retirement plan was unconstitutional. The motion to dismiss for lack of standing should have been denied and a judgment was to be entered below containing a declaration in accordance with the court's views and denying taxpayers' prayers for relief.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- standing -- taxpayers' suits -- petitioner taxpayers have standing to challenge, on constitutional grounds, State legislative enactments authorizing expenditure of State moneys where failure to accord standing would effectively prevent judicial scrutiny of legislative action -- legislative and executive retirement plan of State of New York does not compensate officeholders beyond their terms in office or constitute payment of unauthorized fees in violation of State Constitution (NY Const, art XIII, § 7; art III, § 6), since benefits are deferred component of current compensation -- petitioners' "First Amended Petition" should be dismissed, since its allegations, that lump sum payments to legislators in lieu of expenses are unconstitutional, are so inexactly stated that they fail to state identifiable cause of action upon which declaratory relief may be granted in favor of either party.

1. The petitioner taxpayers have standing to challenge, on constitutional grounds, State legislative enactments authorizing the expenditure of State moneys where failure to accord standing would effectively prevent judicial scrutiny of legislative action. The individual citizen-taxpayer, who is encouraged to take an active interest in government and would be most likely to invoke the powers of the judiciary, should not be excluded from access to the judiciary, which is an integral aspect of the doctrine of separation of powers.

2. The legislative and executive retirement plan of the State of New York (Retirement and Social Security Law, § 80-a) does not compensate officeholders beyond their terms in office or constitute payment of unauthorized fees in violation of the State Constitution (NY Const, art XIII, § 7; art III, § 6), since retirement benefits are a component of current compensation, in which presently earned payment is deferred, and neither constitutional provision relied upon forbids future payment of currently earned income.

3. Respondents' motion to dismiss, to the extent that it is applicable to petitioners' subsequently served "First Amended Petition", should be granted, since petitioners' allegations, that lump sum payments to legislators in lieu of expenses are unconstitutional, are so inexactly stated that they fail to state an identifiable cause of action upon which declaratory relief may be granted in favor of either party.

 

18 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Emilio M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 173; 332 N.E.2d 874; 371 N.Y.S.2d 697; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1943


June 5, 1975, Argued  
June 24, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, matter remitted to the Appellate Division, First Department, for review of the facts ( CPLR 5613), and the question certified answered in the negative in a memorandum.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the New York County Supreme Court (New York) that reversed a family court judgment concluding that appellee had committed acts that, if done by an adult, would constitute the crimes of manslaughter, assault, and possession of a dangerous weapon and committing him to a detention center.


OVERVIEW: Appellee, a minor, was arrested and taken to the police station, and his mother was notified that he was in police custody. Appellee was questioned both before and after her arrival. The family court concluded that appellee had committed acts that, if done by an adult, would constitute manslaughter, assault, and possession of a dangerous weapon and ordered that he be committed to a detention center. The appellate division reversed, concluding that appellee's statements should have been excluded, as the police failed to comply with the mandates of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 724. In reversing, the appellate court concluded that police sufficiently complied with the statute; while appellee's parent was not contacted until his arrival at the police station, this did not constitute undue delay. Further, as the appellate division had not yet designated any facility suitable for questioning children, precise compliance was not possible. Additionally, the statutory mandate that the child be taken immediately to family court was subject to an exception when the police officer determined that it was necessary to question the child.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate division reversing the family court judgment was reversed, as appellee's statements to police were properly admitted where police sufficiently complied with the mandates of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 724 and where there was no evidence of prejudice to appellee.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- juvenile delinquents -- respondent, person alleged to be juvenile delinquent, was properly accorded right under Family Court Act ( § 724, subd [a]) to have parent immediately notified of arrest, although no call was made until arrival at station house, since there was no undue delay -- since Appellate Division had not yet designated any facility in First Department as suitable for questioning of children, as required by Family Court Act ( § 724, subd [b], par [ii]), precise statutory compliance was impossible -- requirement that respondent should be taken directly to Family Court (Family Ct Act, § 724, subd [b], par [ii]) was not violated when police took respondent to station house, since there is express exception when it is determined that child should be questioned -- as at least mixed question of fact, issue of taint with respect to respondent's written statement, on basis of questioning prior to respondent's mother's arrival, is not reviewable by Court of Appeals -- accordingly, there appears to have been substantial compliance with terms of statute, such that irregularities do not require suppression of written statement.

1. Respondent, a person alleged to be a juvenile delinquent, was properly accorded his right under subdivision (a) of section 724 of the Family Court Act to have his parent immediately notified of his arrest. While no call was made until arrival at the station house, the call was made without undue delay and such delay as did occur was reasonably justified.

2. Since the Appellate Division had not yet designated any facility in the First Department, including the station house to which respondent was taken, as suitable for the questioning of children, as required by the Family Court Act ( § 724, subd [b], par [ii]), precise statutory compliance was impossible.

3. The statutory requirement that respondent should be taken directly to the Family Court (Family Ct Act, § 724, subd [b], par [ii]) was not violated when the police took respondent to the station house, since the mandate is subject to an express exception if it is determined that the child should be questioned.

4. As at least a mixed question of fact, the issue of taint with respect to respondent's written statement, on the basis of questioning prior to respondent's mother's arrival, is not reviewable by the Court of Appeals.

5. Accordingly, there appears to have been substantial compliance with the terms of the statute, such that any irregularities, taken either singly or in combination, do not require suppression of respondent's written statement.

 

19 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Broad Properties, Inc. v. O'Hara


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 986; 337 N.E.2d 120; 374 N.Y.S.2d 603; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 2070


June 4, 1975, Argued
