20 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Broadie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 100; 332 N.E.2d 338; 371 N.Y.S.2d 471; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1932


March 24, 1975, Argued  
June 18, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Eight defendants appealed separate orders of the Appellate Divisions of the Supreme Court (New York) affirming their respective sentences and convictions for drug trafficking. Defendants alleged that the drug trafficking statutes under which they were convicted imposed cruel and unusual punishment and were disproportionate to their crimes pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 5 and U.S. Const. amend. VIII.


OVERVIEW: Eight defendants challenged the constitutionality of statutes classifying the drug-trafficking crimes for which they were convicted as class A felonies, the highest rank of crime in the state. Defendants also challenged the sentencing provisions under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.00, which imposed a mandatory maximum sentence of life imprisonment and minimums from one or six years to eight and one-third years. The principal issue was whether the laws mandating life imprisonment, and therefore lifetime parole on parole release, prescribed sentences so disproportionate as would constitute cruel and unusual punishment in violation of constitutional limitations contained in N.Y. Const. art. I, § 5 and U.S. Const. amend. VIII. Drug trafficking was a widespread phenomenon that impoverished the users and led to violent crime. The court affirmed the judgments upholding the drug-trafficking statutes. The court held that the punishments imposed for these crimes were not grossly disproportionate or cruel and unusual in the constitutional sense based upon the legislature's rational view of the gravity of the offenses, the danger posed by the offenders, and the penological purposes served.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the appellate divisions upholding defendants' sentences for drug trafficking.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- dangerous drugs -- cruel and unusual punishment -- sections 220.40, 220.39 and 220.18, which classify crimes for which defendants were convicted as class A felonies and applicable sentencing provisions (Penal Law, § 70.00, subd 2, par [a]; subd 3, par [a], cls [ii], [iii]), imposing mandatory maximum term of life imprisonment and minimums of one or six to eight and one-third years for sales of drugs, are not so disproportionate to crime as to constitute cruel and unusual punishment; Legislature could reasonably conclude that drug trafficking was grave offense and threat to society, engendering violent crime, and that sentencing statutes, although severe and inflexible, would serve to isolate and deter; while drug offenses are more severely punished than other crimes in State and same crime in other States, narcotics laws are not irrationally severe.

Sections 220.40, 220.39 and 220.18, which classify the crimes for which the defendants were convicted as class A felonies and the applicable sentencing provisions (Penal Law, § 70.00, subd 2, par [a]; subd 3, par [a], cls [ii], [iii]), imposing a mandatory maximum term of imprisonment for life and minimums of one or six years to eight and one-third years for sales of drugs, are not so disproportionate to the crime as to constitute cruel and unusual punishment. The cruel and unusual punishment clause (NY Const, art I, § 5; US Const, 8th Amdt) is flexible, prohibiting gross disproportionality of punishment, a determination to which the gravity of the offense and the danger to society of the offender are decisive. Narcotics sales are symptomatic of a widespread phenomenon in which crimes of violence are engendered against law enforcement officers, drug traffickers themselves, addicts and the public. Facing a high recidivism rate in drug-related crimes and an inadequate response to less severe punishment, the Legislature could reasonably shift the emphasis from rehabilitation to isolation and deterrence. While the drug offenses are punished more severely and inflexibly than almost any other offense in the State and more severely than in any other jurisdiction, the punishments are not irrationally severe, given the dimensions of the problem.

 

21 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Morales


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 262; 333 N.E.2d 339; 372 N.Y.S.2d 25; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1960


March 21, 1975, Argued  
June 17, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a verdict convicting defendant of criminally selling a dangerous drug in the third degree, and which directed a new trial.


OVERVIEW: As a sanction for his failure to comply with a notice of alibi statute, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 250.20, defendant was prevented from calling an alibi witness. The court found that the statute was unconstitutional. The court applied its holding as to the statute's unconstitutionality retroactively. Therefore, on the ground that the alibi witness testimony should not have been excluded, the judgment of conviction was properly reversed. The due process standard, sought to be applied retroactively, profoundly and directly affected the fact finding process itself. The requirement that the seriousness of the decision for which retroactivity was sought, be balanced against the seriousness of its impact on the administration of justice, was easily met. The decision was of serious constitutional dimension, and the effect on the administration of justice by retroactive application was, at most, relatively inconsequential. The court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the notice of alibi statute was unconstitutional, and applied its holding retroactively to determine that alibi witness testimony against defendant should not have been excluded. The lower court's order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- alibi -- notice-of-alibi -- where defendant was prevented from calling alibi witness for failure to comply with notice-of-alibi statute ( CPL 250.20), subsequent holding that statute is unconstitutional should be retroactively applied on direct appeal, since inability to call witness affects fact-finding process and effect upon administration of justice is inconsequential -- testimony by undercover police officer as to station house viewing of defendant six hours after arrest did not violate defendant's due process rights, since viewing was not for identification purposes, but only to assure that officers had arrested proper person, and since second viewing by trained officer who was deliberate participant in criminal encounter does not bear burden of caveats involved in identification by nonprofessional -- police officer's testimony as to second viewing did not constitute improper bolstering, since only third-party bolstering is prejudicial hearsay -- accordingly, there should be new trial.

1. Where the defendant was prevented from calling an alibi witness for failure to comply with New York State's notice-of-alibi statute ( CPL 250.20), a subsequent holding that the statute is unconstitutional should be given retroactive effect on direct appeal. While a defendant who complied with the statute should not derive benefit from the declaration of unconstitutionality, since the effect of the statute, which required disclosure of alibi witnesses, was only to hinder such a defendant's preparation to rebut the prosecution's rebuttal of the alibi, a defendant who has been prevented from calling a witness suffers a detriment which profoundly and directly affects the fact-finding process. On balance, the effect of retroactive application on the administration of justice is, at most, inconsequential since there has been no significant reliance on the statute by law enforcement authorities and since any prejudice may be counteracted by the trial court.

2. Testimony by an undercover police officer as to a station house viewing of the defendant six hours after arrest did not violate the defendant's due process rights. The officer had allegedly purchased heroin from the defendant, contacted other officers, and then relocated the defendant for arrest by other officers. The station house viewing was not for identification, but was to ascertain if the officers had arrested the proper person. The second viewing by a trained police officer who was a deliberate participant in the criminal encounter does not bear the burden of the caveats involved in an identification by a nonprofessional. Rather than being improper, the procedure assured that an innocent man was not arrested.

3. The police officer's testimony with regard to his second viewing did not constitute improper bolstering, since only third-party bolstering is prejudicial hearsay, and his testimony regarding his first viewing, although bolstering the testimony of the other officers, was nothing more than the strengthening of eyewitness testimony by other testimony to the same effect.

4. Accordingly, there should be a new trial.

 

22 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Graham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 633; 331 N.E.2d 673; 370 N.Y.S.2d 888; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1921


January 6, 1975, Argued  
June 12, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and the People appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which modified, on the law, and as modified affirmed a judgment convicting defendant of murder in the second degree. The modification consisted of convicting defendant of manslaughter in the first degree and remanding the matter to the trial court for resentencing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was originally convicted for murder in the second degree after being indicted for murder in the first degree. On direct appeal of the conviction, the appellate division reduced the conviction to the lesser included offense of manslaughter in the first degree. A federal appeals court held that defendant was deprived of his constitutional right to a trial when the appellate division summarily reduced the conviction to first degree manslaughter. The court found that when defendant was retried for murder in the second degree, that portion of the order of the appellate division setting aside the conviction for murder in the second degree, which under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 470.20(3), then carried with it a dismissal of the charge, was still in effect because it had never been expressly nullified. Therefore, upon the second trial, the court lacked the power to try defendant for murder in the second degree and the appellate division did not err in setting defendant's conviction aside.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order that as modified convicted defendant of manslaughter in the first degree and remanded the matter for resentencing, because the trial court lacked the power to retry defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- former jeopardy -- defendant could not be retried for murder in second degree after Appellate Division reduced conviction thereof to manslaughter in first degree for insufficiency of proof resulting from suppression of evidence, despite Federal court nullification of that appellate holding on ground that defendant was deprived thereby of right to be tried on lesser charge without inadmissible evidence before jury; reduction of original conviction necessarily involved reversal of conviction on higher charge and affirmance of lower charge, and Federal court order did not nullify entire order, leaving reversal portion, such that effect thereof is same as acquittal -- reversal of conviction on higher charge was equivalent to dismissal thereof and mere granting of new trial could not restore charge -- retrial on lower charge was not precluded since only order relating to higher charge was dismissed, notwithstanding Federal nullification of conviction on lesser charge -- defendant was properly convicted on lesser charge on retrial for higher crime, since proof resulting in second improper conviction for higher charge was sufficient to establish guilt of lower charge, and Appellate Division could reduce conviction under CPL 470.15 (subd 2) because tainted evidence was not admitted and lesser offense was one for which jury could have convicted -- accordingly, Appellate Division's second reduction of conviction to manslaughter in first degree, following retrial, was proper and case was properly remanded for resentencing defendant.

1. The defendant could not be retried for murder in the second degree after the Appellate Division summarily reduced his conviction thereof to manslaughter in the first degree for insufficiency of proof resulting from suppression of certain evidence, despite a Federal court nullification of that appellate holding on the ground that the defendant was deprived thereby of the right to be tried on the lesser charge without the inadmissible evidence before the jury. Unless an appellate order setting aside a conviction is reversed, the defendant may not be retried for the offense and the effect is the same as acquittal. The Federal court order did not nullify the Appellate Division order in its entirety. The reduction of the original conviction to manslaughter in the first degree necessarily involved a reversal of the conviction on the higher charge and an affirmance of the conviction for the lesser included offense of manslaughter in the first degree. There is nothing offensive in the partial invalidation of an appellate order modifying a conviction, as is best illustrated by the procedure of cross appeals in which the court considers only the appeal of each party from that portion of the order which adversely affects him.

2. When the defendant was retried, the portion of the Appellate Division order setting aside the conviction for murder in the second degree, which necessarily carried with it a dismissal of that charge, was still in effect, and the mere granting of a new trial on another count could not restore the dismissed charge.

3. Since it was only the order relating to the charge of murder in the second degree which was equivalent to a dismissal, and the conviction of manslaughter in the first degree was affirmed, retrial on the latter charge was not precluded, notwithstanding Federal nullification of the conviction.

4. On retrial the defendant was properly convicted of manslaughter in the first degree, since the proof resulting in his second improper conviction of murder in the second degree was sufficient to establish guilt of the lesser included offense and the conviction may be reduced under CPL 470.15 (subd 2). Since the tainted evidence was not admitted, the lesser offense was one for which the jury could have convicted.

5. Accordingly, the Appellate Division's second reduction of the conviction to manslaughter in the first degree, following retrial, was proper and the case was properly remanded for resentencing the defendant.

 

23 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hellerstein v. Assessor of Islip


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 1; 332 N.E.2d 279; 371 N.Y.S.2d 388; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1922


February 12, 1975, Argued  
June 5, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a dismissal of appellant's petition. Appellant's petition, brought pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 706, alleged that a town's entire assessment roll was void because the assessments were not made in accordance with N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 306.


OVERVIEW: Appellant property owner claimed that an entire town's assessment roll was void. Appellant brought an action, pursuant to N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 706, against respondent town assessor. Appellant's petition alleged that respondent's assessments were not made in accordance with N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 306. The lower court affirmed a dismissal of appellant's petition. The court modified the lower court's judgment by requiring respondent to make future property assessments at full value. The court held that respondent's actions in assessing town property at a fraction of its market value was a flagrant violation of § 306; the general, widespread practice of fractional property assessments in violation of § 306 did not constitute a valid reason for respondent's violation of § 306; and that appellant was not entitled to have the past assessment rolls declared a nullity.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower court's affirmance of a dismissal of the petition of appellant property owner by requiring respondent town assessor to make future property assessments at full value. The court held that respondent's actions in assessing town property at a fraction of its market value was a flagrant violation of the relevant state statute and that appellant was not entitled to have the past assessment rolls declared a nullity.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- assessments -- undervaluation -- respondent township's practice of utilizing fractional real property assessments is in violation of section 306 of Real Property Tax Law; it is perpetuated by ability it affords taxing authorities to increase revenues while maintaining tax rate, to tax various forms of property differently, to affect State aid based upon valuation and to discourage claims of unconstitutional overvaluation, making it difficult to ascertain inequality resulting from incompetence, favoritism or corruption -- settled assessment rolls should not be disturbed by declaration of nullity, since fiscal chaos would result and, accordingly, order directing future assessments to be made at full value should go into effect within reasonable time, but not later than December 31, 1976, and in interim any tax levies, liens, foreclosures or transfers should not be subject to challenge for failure to comply with section 306 of Real Property Tax Law.

1. Petitioner is entitled to an order directing respondent township to make future assessments at full value as required by section 306 of the Real Property Tax Law. The widespread practice of utilizing fractional real property assessments involves a flagrant violation of the statute, perpetuated by such factors as the ability it affords taxing authorities to increase revenues while maintaining a stable tax rate, to tax various forms of property at different rates and to affect the amount of State aid which is based upon assessed valuation. Since assessments may not exceed full value (NY Const, art XVI, § 2), fractional valuation discourages claims of overvaluation, and a taxpayer has the more difficult task of proving inequality of assessment. Undervaluation makes it difficult to ascertain whether there is uniformity of assessment or inequality as a result of incompetence, favoritism or corruption.

2. An invalidation of the assessment rolls would create fiscal chaos for respondent township, which would be a sufficient basis to deny an order of mandamus and, since relief under section 706 of the Real Property Tax Law, contesting an illegal assessment, would have the same impact, the settled assessment rolls should not be disturbed by declaring them a nullity. Accordingly, an order directing future assessments to be made at full value should go into effect within a reasonable time, but not later than December 31, 1976, and in the interim any tax levies, liens, foreclosures or transfers shall not be subject to challenge for failure to comply with section 306 of the Real Property Tax Law.

 

24 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Amsterdam v. Helsby


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


37 N.Y.2d 19; 332 N.E.2d 290; 371 N.Y.S.2d 404; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1923; 89 L.R.R.M. 2871; 78 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P53,807


March 25, 1975, Argued  
June 5, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: In City of Amsterdam v Helsby: Appeals transferred to the Appellate Division, Third Department, without costs.

In City of Buffalo v New York State Employment Relations Bd.: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Public Employment Relations Board sought review of the judgment of the lower court, which declared N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 209 unconstitutional and enjoined respondent City of Amsterdam from exercising any power, rights, and duties with respect to enforcement thereof. In a separate action, appellant City of Buffalo appealed from a judgment that declared § 209 constitutional and valid in all respects.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 209 provided that disputes arising in the course of collective bargaining negotiations between a public employer and its firemen and policemen were to be submitted to an arbitration panel established under the jurisdiction of appellant Public Employment Relations Board (PERB). In the cases brought on appeal, respondent City of Amsterdam and appellant City of Buffalo had reached an impasse in their negotiations with appellant PERB and respondent Employment Relations Board. It was argued that § 209 was unconstitutional and the court disagreed and affirmed the judgment against appellant City of Buffalo. The court held that N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2(c) made it clear that the home rule powers sustained an exercise of local authority with respect to the regulation of the hours of work, compensation, and other issues, of the employees to the extent that such exercise was not inconsistent with any general law. The court held that the legislature had delegated to appellant PERB, and through appellant PERB to ad hoc arbitration panels, its constitutional authority in the limited situation where an impasse occurred and that the delegation was both proper and reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent Employment Relations Board, but held that because the case involving respondent City of Amsterdam had both procedural and constitutional issues, a direct appeal as of right did not lie. The court also held that there was no constitutional prohibition against the legislative delegation of power, with reasonable safeguards and standards, to an agency established to administer an enactment.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- Public Employees' Fair Employment Act -- amendments to section 209 of Civil Service Law, providing for binding arbitration when negotiations between public employer and its policemen and firemen reach impasse, do not violate Home Rule provisions of New York Constitution; after arbitration is sought, attempt by local government to regulate hours of work and compensation would be inconsistent with general law, arbitration procedure established by Legislature -- each amendment is general law -- delegation of authority to Public Employment Relations Board not unconstitutional.

1. The 1974 amendments to section 209 of the Civil Service Law, which provide that disputes arising in the course of collective bargaining negotiations between a public employer and its firemen and policemen are to be submitted to binding arbitration when the negotiations reach an impasse, do not violate the Home Rule provisions of the New York Constitution (art IX, § 2, subd [c]). The Home Rule powers sustain the regulation of the hours of work and compensation of employees by a local government only to the extent that such exercise is not inconsistent with a general law enacted by the Legislature, and when an impasse in negotiations is reached and arbitration is sought, an attempt by a local government to regulate hours of work and compensation of policemen and firemen would be inconsistent with the arbitration procedure established by the Legislature.

2. Since the Public Employees' Fair Employment Act (Civil Service Law, § 200 et seq.) applies to all public employers, it is a general law and, since neither of the amendments to section 209 is any narrower in application, each amendment is a general law.

3. There is no unconstitutional delegation of authority by the Legislature to the arbitration panels. The Legislature delegated to the Public Employment Relations Board (PERB), and through PERB to the arbitration panels, its constitutional authority to regulate hours of work and compensation where an impasse occurs and established specific standards which must be followed by the panels. Such delegation is proper and reasonable.

4. The arbitration panels have not unconstitutionally been granted the power of taxation.

5. The amendments do not violate the one-man -- one-vote principle and, therefore, deny equal protection of the law to citizens of municipalities.

6. Since the case of City of Amsterdam v Helsby involves a procedural as well as a constitutional issue, a direct appeal to the Court of Appeals does not lie.

 

25 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wein v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 610; 331 N.E.2d 514; 370 N.Y.S.2d 550; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1859


May 2, 1975, Argued  
May 27, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer and defendant City of New York sought review of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) decision which affirmed the trial court judgment that held plaintiff had standing to bring an action under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 51 to challenge the New York City Stabilization Reserve Corporation Act and which denied plaintiff's application for an injunction and held the statute constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer and defendant City of New York challenged the lower court's decision to affirm the judgment by the trial court that found plaintiff had standing to bring an action under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 51 to challenge the New York City Stabilization Reserve Corporation Act. Plaintiff also challenged the findings that denied plaintiff's application for an injunction, granted defendant's motion for summary judgment, and declared the act constitutional. The court modified the lower court's judgment to state that plaintiff did state a valid cause of action for relief and affirmed all other findings. The injunction was properly denied, as the notes, bonds, or other obligations of the Stabilization Reserve Corporation (SRC) were not the debt of either defendant or the state, and neither defendant nor the state was liable thereon. Additionally, any payments made by defendant to the SRC would have been proper, as defendant had an absolute right following the receipt of any state-aide monies to pay a portion thereof to a public benefit corporation under N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to state that plaintiff taxpayer asserted a valid cause of action, and thereafter affirmed all other judgments, as defendant City of New York was not liable for any indebtedness of the Stabilization Reserve Corporation and defendant was permitted via the constitution to make payments to public benefit corporations.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- indebtedness -- New York City Stabilization Reserve Corporation Act (Public Authorities Law, §§ 2530 - 2549), which provides for sale of bonds by public corporation, with proceeds to be paid to City of New York and yearly deficit of corporation to be paid by city or, if necessary, by State Comptroller from city's per capita State aid or Stock Transfer Tax Fund, is constitutional; act does not violate constitutional prohibition (NY Const, art VIII, § 1) against loan of city's credit in aid of public corporation, since appropriation would be gift rather than payment of indebtedness -- since city can in no way become indebted, there is no violation of sections 2 or 4 of article VIII or section 5 of article X of Constitution which prohibit city from contracting indebtedness without pledging its full faith and credit, from contracting indebtedness exceeding debt limitation and from becoming liable for obligations of public corporation, and no lien is created by city's exercise of right to pay portion of State aid moneys to public corporation -- plaintiff has standing to bring action under provisions of section 51 of General Municipal Law and city is proper and necessary party, since complaint is directed at its constitutional duties.

1. The New York City Stabilization Reserve Corporation Act (Public Authorities Law, §§ 2530 - 2549) is constitutional. The act provides for sale of bonds by a public benefit corporation, with the proceeds to be paid to the City of New York and yearly deficits of the corporation to be paid by the city. It is specified that neither the city nor the State is liable for debts of the corporation, and if the city fails to make appropriations therefor the State Comptroller will pay the required amounts from the first moneys becoming available to the city from per capita State aid or the Stock Transfer Tax Fund. The act complies with section 1 of article VIII of the New York Constitution, which prohibits the loan of the city's credit in aid of a public corporation, since any appropriation would be, according to the terms of the act, a gift rather than payment of indebtedness.

2. Since the city can in no way become indebted, there is no violation of sections 2 or 4 of article VIII or section 5 of article X of the Constitution which prohibit, respectively, the city from contracting indebtedness without pledging its full faith and credit, from contracting indebtedness in excess of its debt limitation and from becoming liable for the obligations of a public corporation. The city has a right to pay a portion of its State aid moneys to a public corporation and the mere directive by the State and city respecting the distribution of such funds creates no lien thereon.

3. Reference in the complaint to the constitutional debt limitation of the City of New York and to the insufficiency of borrowing power of the city under such restriction to permit the contemplated sale of bonds under the act does not indicate an overindebtedness separate from that questioned with respect to the Stabilization Reserve Corporation, and no cause of action is made out in relation thereto.

4. Since the plaintiff himself specifically requested judgment pursuant to subdivision (c) of CPLR 3211, which converts a motion to dismiss into a summary judgment motion, he cannot be heard to complain that he received insufficient notice that the court would treat the defendant's CPLR 3211 motion as a CPLR 3212 motion for summary judgment.

5. The plaintiff has standing to bring this action under the taxpayer provisions of section 51 of the General Municipal Law. The city is a proper and necessary party, since the complaint is directed at the manner in which the city meets its constitutional mandates, which obviates the technical arguments respecting the nonjoinder of the State and the Stabilization Reserve Corporation.

6. Accordingly, the Stabilization Reserve Corporation Act was properly declared valid, and the complaint stated a cause of action for the relief demanded.

 

26 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fischbach & Moore, Inc. v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 605; 331 N.E.2d 511; 370 N.Y.S.2d 545; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1858


March 17, 1975, Argued  
May 13, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Appellate Division for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two interstate corporations and a New York corporation, appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed their petition and confirmed a determination of respondent State Tax Commission sustaining an unincorporated business tax imposed upon petitioners under article 23 of the Tax Law. The three corporations had formed a joint venture.


OVERVIEW: The main issue was whether each petitioner could be subjected to the unincorporated business tax on that portion of the 1961 New York joint venture income, which exceeded the percentage of the total corporate income on which New York franchise taxes were required to be paid under the allocation formula. The court determined that petitioners were entitled to an unincorporated tax exemption limited to the amount, which was included in the corporate partners' income allocable to New York State as a result of the application of New York's allocation formula and not just an exemption for an amount equal to the allocation ratio percentage of the income allocable to New York. N.Y. Tax Law § 709(2) meant just what it said: It was the amount of corporate income included in that allocable to New York pursuant to ratio that was the limit of the exemption and not just the ratio percentage of that New York income. Thus, the statute spoke of an "amount" of income rather than percentage. Only any amount of income from the joint venture that exceeded the amount of total corporate income allocable to New York for corporate franchise tax purposes was subject to the unincorporated business tax.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate division, which was in favor of the Commission, and remitted the matter for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- unincorporated business income tax -- corporate petitioners which engaged in joint venture in New York State, are liable for corporate franchise tax upon income allocable to New York State and, additionally, for unincorporated business tax, subject to exemption for "amount" included in net income allocable to State for corporate franchise tax purposes, and such tax is based upon joint venture income in excess of amount of total corporate income allocable to New York, not joint venture income in excess of percentage of such income upon which corporate franchise taxes were paid.

The corporate petitioners engaged in electrical or construction work on a project within New York State and paid corporate franchise taxes ratably to the extent that their total income was attributable to New York State. As joint venturers the petitioners were additionally liable for unincorporated business taxes, subject to an exemption from such tax under subdivision (2) of section 709 of the Tax Law for the "amount which is * * * included in a corporate partner's net income allocable to this state [for corporate franchise tax purposes]". The petitioners may not be exempted from unincorporated business tax only on the portion of New York State joint venture income which equals the percentage of that income upon which corporate franchise taxes were paid, since the statute speaks of "amount" rather than percentage of income. Only the amount of joint venture income which exceeds the total corporate income allocable to New York State for corporate franchise tax purposes is subject to unincorporated business tax.

 

27 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Maldini v. Ambro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 481; 330 N.E.2d 403; 369 N.Y.S.2d 385; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1843


January 14, 1975, Argued  
May 8, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, town and officials, amended a zoning ordinance to create a residence district providing for a retirement community district. The town's subsequent resolution granted an application of defendant corporation to rezone its land. Plaintiff homeowners filed an action for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief. The Supreme Court (New York) affirmed the trial court, which upheld both the amendment and the resolution.


OVERVIEW: The town's resolution allowed the corporation to rezone its land from a district allowing single-family dwellings to a retirement community district for the purpose of building residences designed for older people. The application for rezoning was granted following a public hearing. None of the homeowners appeared or raised any objection at that hearing or at the earlier one regarding passage of the amendment. The homeowners claimed that the town board exceeded the zoning powers delegated it by the legislature and impermissibly applied a classification based upon age. The court held that the grant provided a sufficient basis for the town's amendment to provide for multiple residences for elderly people. The enabling legislation empowered the town to regulate and restrict the use of land for the purpose of promoting the health, safety, morals, or the general welfare of the community. The amendment had a rational basis. There was no indication that the intent or effect of the ordinance was to segregate the community according to age or to benefit a particular building developer in the absence of a need for specialized housing for the elderly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgement of the appellate court.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- age discrimination -- defendant town did not exceed power to regulate land use for general welfare by creating "Retirement Community District", and it validly reclassified parcel of land to such district for multiple residences for aged, since action was inclusionary, rather than exclusionary, correcting pattern of housing deprivation; depreciation of surrounding property will not shield zoning classification from adaptation to changing needs, and age considerations are appropriate, when rationally related to proper governmental purpose, as nondiscriminatory exercise of power to provide for all people.

The defendant town did not exceed its powers to regulate land use for the community health and general welfare (Town Law, § 261) by amending its zoning ordinance to create a residence district providing, among other uses, for a "Retirement Community District", and it validly granted a reclassification of a parcel of land as a "Retirement Community District" for the purpose of building multiple residences for aged persons. The town's action was inclusionary, rather than exclusionary, and is a movement toward correction of social and historical patterns of housing deprivation. Possible depreciation in value to surrounding residential properties, by virtue of increased traffic, will not shield the existing zoning classification from adaptation to changing community needs. Age considerations are appropriate when rationally related to a proper governmental purpose, such as meeting the special needs of an important segment of the population by establishing, as opposed to denying, a zoning district allowing residences designed for, but not limited to, aged persons. There is no indication of intent to discriminate against younger people, to benefit a particular builder or to reap tax base benefits without the drain on municipal services created by families and children, and there is no arbitrary specific age limit in the ordinance. "Senior citizenship" is within the normal expectancy of most people, rather than an unalterable classification, and provision for the elderly may be viewed as a nondiscriminatory exercise of the power to provide for the general welfare of all people, since, even if fairly debatable, the town board's legislative judgment must control.

 

28 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Adoption of Malpica-Orsini


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 568; 331 N.E.2d 486; 370 N.Y.S.2d 511; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1856


February 17, 1975, Submitted  
May 8, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unwed father challenged the judgment of the Family Court, Westchester County, New York, which approved the petition of respondent step-father for the adoption of the child without the consent of the father.


OVERVIEW: The father contended that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §111(3), which limited consent to the adoption of a child born out of wedlock to the natural mother, was unconstitutional in that it violated the due process and equal protection clauses of the United States Constitution by unjustly discriminating between fathers of children born out of wedlock and all other parents. On appeal, the court held that if consent was to be required of the father of an out-of-wedlock child, that was a matter for the legislature; the courts had no right to expand the terms of the instant statute. The classification of § 111(3) discriminating between fathers of children born out of wedlock and all other parents was reasonable, not arbitrary, and, because it kept in mind the paramount consideration of a child's welfare, the legislative action was justified. The court held that although it was an adoption proceeding, the father, having been given notice by court direction, the right to object, and a full hearing with representation by an attorney, was not deprived of due process, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law §111.


OUTCOME: The judgment that permitted the adoption without the consent of the unwed father was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- child born out of wedlock -- Domestic Relations Law ( § 111, subd 3) requires natural mother's consent for proposed adoption, but not natural father's consent; such classification is reasonable and does not deny equal protection of laws; due process was accorded natural father by giving him notice and hearing on his objections; judgment of adoption affirmed.

For the adoption of a child born out of wedlock, the Domestic Relations Law ( § 111, subd 3) requires the natural mother's consent, but does not require the natural father's consent. This legislative distinction or classification is reasonable and constitutional, irrespective of whether or not paternity has been determined by an order of filiation and whether or not the natural father has paid some support. The constitutional requirement of equal protection of the laws is not served by giving an out-of-wedlock child's natural father a possibly extortionate veto against an adoption which is in the child's best interests. In this case, due process was accorded to the natural father when the court gave him notice of the petition for adoption and he appeared by counsel and objected and the court held a hearing thereon and determined that the proposed adoption by the man whom the natural mother has recently married is in the best interests of the child. The judgment of adoption is affirmed.

Canastota v. Queensboro Farm Products, Inc.

Albert Simon, Inc. v. Myerson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 300; 327 N.E.2d 801; 367 N.Y.S.2d 755; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1764


February 20, 1975, Argued  
April 1, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commissioner challenged an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's judgment denying an application to annul the commissioner's determination and dismissing a petition. The order also declared New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B32-40.0 unconstitutional, and enjoined the commissioner from withdrawing license renewals of respondents licensees.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner had previously granted renewals for the licensees' "common show" licenses to distribute pinball machines but later sought to withdraw his approval. The intermediate court of appeals had declared § B32-40.0 unconstitutional insofar as it prohibited the licensing as "common shows" of coin-operated games of chance. The licensees contended that the commissioner's determination was arbitrary, and that because the state had pre-empted the field, the ordinance was invalid. The commissioner argued that the ordinance was a constitutional exercise of the city's police powers. The court reversed the order and dismissed the petition. The court held that: (1) the legislation adopted by the municipality was reasonably related to a proper governmental purpose, namely the prevention of evils associated with gambling; (2) there was an expressed, rational legislative judgment that there was a strong relationship between gambling, and the evils that attended it, and pinball machines; (3) the ordinance had a rational basis and did not violate due process; and (4) the commissioner's prior approval as a result of an erroneous application of the law did not result in estoppel.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, with costs, the order issued by the intermediate court of appeals, and held that the commissioner did not err in withdrawing approval for licensing and installation of the licensees' pinball game machines at various premises in the city. The court also dismissed the licensees' petition.


HEADNOTES

Gaming -- pinball machines -- licensing -- regulation of gambling -- determination of Commissioner of Consumer Affairs of City of New York withdrawing licensing of pinball machines was proper, since article 5 of chapter 32 of Administrative Code of City of New York prohibits gaming devices consisting of any game involving element of chance or any pinball machine whether or not such machine is gambling device; express legislative judgment that there is strong relationship between such machines and gambling is not irrational, and legislation is rationally related to proper governmental purpose -- prior erroneous approval and licensing of such machines does not result in estoppel -- article 225 of Penal Law does not indicate intent to reserve to State exclusive regulatory power over gambling.

1. A determination of the Commissioner of Consumer Affairs of the City of New York withdrawing approval for the licensing and installation of various pinball game machines pursuant to article 5 of chapter 32 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York was proper. The code's provisions prohibit gaming devices, consisting of "any game involving any element of chance" or any "pinball" machine, whether or not such machine is a gambling device which awards a prize, free play or other benefit to a player. It cannot be said that the express legislative judgment that there is a strong relationship between these devices and gambling is irrational, and the legislation is reasonably related to the proper governmental purpose of the prevention of evils associated with gambling.

2. No estoppel results from the commissioner's prior erroneous application of the law in originally approving and licensing these machines, since a public official is not thereafter relieved of the obligation to apply and enforce the law correctly.

3. There is no indication in article 225 of the Penal Law of a legislative intent to reserve to the State exclusive regulatory power over gambling, and the city is not prevented from regulating pinball machines or games of chance.

 

32 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lang


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 366; 329 N.E.2d 176; 368 N.Y.S.2d 492; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1815


February 20, 1975, Submitted  
April 1, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified, by eliminating jail time, and affirmed as modified a conviction of defendant for an attempt to violate N.Y. Elec. Law § 421(5).


OVERVIEW: Defendant offered cash to a person who was considering running for an elected office so that the person would withdraw. Defendant was convicted in the trial court for an attempt to violate N.Y. Elec. Law § 415(5) and sentenced to a fine and time in the county jail. The intermediate appellate court affirmed the conviction but applied its discretion to modify the sentence by eliminating the jail time. Defendant asserted that § 415(5) was impermissibly vague and overbroad. The court affirmed the conviction and held that § 421(5) was not impermissibly vague, that it was a reasonable restriction upon the individual for the public good and that the subdivision was not void for over-breadth. The court recognized that the statute was not narrowly defined, but recognized that under such circumstance it was unnecessary to require more limited definition since by incorporating fraudulent in the statute it was sufficient to allow the reasonable person to understand conduct that was prohibited.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- fraudulently affecting result of primary election -- subdivision 5 of section 421 of Election Law, which provides that any person who fraudulently and wrongfully does act tending to affect result of primary election is guilty of misdemeanor, is constitutional; it is not impermissibly vague, is reasonable restriction upon individual for public good, and is not void for overbreadth -- defendant, who offered $ 1,000 to named person to induce him not to be candidate in primary election was properly convicted of attempt to violate subdivision 5 of section 421; her acts were fraudulent and wrongful -- by failing to except to charge, alleged error not preserved for review.

1. Subdivision 5 of section 421 of the Election Law, which provides that any person who fraudulently and wrongfully does an act tending to affect the result of a primary election is guilty of a misdemeanor, is constitutional. Subdivision 5 is not impermissibly vague, is a reasonable restriction upon the individual for the public good, and is not void for overbreadth. While the subdivision is marked by flexability rather than meticulous specificity, its design is apparent. The word "fraudulent" connotes a deliberate deception upon the electorate and a corrupt act to prevent a free and open election. In view of the innumerable methods by which a primary may be tampered with, it was almost impossible for the Legislature to articulate with precision every prohibited transaction.

2. Defendant, an attorney, who offered $ 1,000 to a named person to induce him not to be a candidate in the Democratic primary for the office of Councilman in the City of Long Beach at the 1971 election, was properly convicted of an attempt to violate subdivision 5 of section 421 of the Election Law. Her acts were fraudulent and wrongful and any person of ordinary intelligence would perceive them to be such.

3. By failing to interpose an exception to the trial court's charge, defense counsel has not preserved the alleged error for review on appeal.

 

33 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ingle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 413; 330 N.E.2d 39; 369 N.Y.S.2d 67; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1821


February 18, 1975, Argued  
April 1, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed his conviction, upon a plea of guilty, for attempted criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.15.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for attempted criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.15. He received five years' probation. The appellate division affirmed, and defendant appealed. On appeal, the central issue was whether a police officer could arbitrarily stop an automobile solely to examine the motorist's license and registration or to inspect the vehicle for possible equipment violations. The court, in reversing defendant's conviction, found that police officers were authorized to conduct "routine traffic checks" if the stop was based upon specific and articulable facts which, taken together with rational inferences from those facts, reasonably warranted the intrusion. Additionally, police officers could conduct nonarbitrary, uniform, and systematic inspections. Here, the stop was an unreasonable limited seizure. The officer's subsequent discoveries constituted derivative evidence obtained by an illegal seizure that should have been suppressed.


OUTCOME: The intermediate court's order affirming defendant's conviction was reversed and conviction was vacated; defendant's conviction for attempted criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree was vacated because the contraband discovered was derivative evidence obtained by an illegal seizure that should have been suppressed because the police officer improperly stopped defendant's vehicle without cause.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- unlawful search and seizure -- police officer may not arbitrarily stop automobile, solely because of unusual appearance, to examine license and registration or to inspect for equipment violations; such stops must be only upon reasonable suspicion of violation or according to nonarbitrary, uniform procedures in order to balance State's compelling interest in highway safety with motorist's interest in being free of arbitrary State intrusion -- defendant's conviction of attempted criminal possession of dangerous drug in fourth degree, based upon evidence obtained in illegal seizure of automobile, is vacated.

A police officer may not arbitrarily stop an automobile on a public highway, solely because of its unusual appearance, to examine the motorist's license and registration or to inspect the vehicle for possible equipment violations. The defendant's conviction of attempted criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the fourth degree, based upon evidence obtained in an illegal seizure of his automobile, is accordingly vacated. A single automobile on a public highway may be stopped only upon reasonable suspicion of a violation of the Vehicle and Traffic Law and, absent a minimal degree of such suspicion, a "routine traffic check" may be conducted only according to nonarbitrary, nondiscriminatory, uniform procedures, such as at roadblocks, checkpoints and weighing stations. An automobile stop is a limited seizure within constitutional limitations. The State's compelling interest in highway safety, requiring some form of on-the-spot inspection, is properly balanced with the motorist's general right to be free from arbitrary State intrusion by an elimination of any arbitrary element in the practice.

 

34 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nieves


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 396; 330 N.E.2d 26; 369 N.Y.S.2d 50; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1819


February 17, 1975, Argued  
March 26, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, judgment of conviction vacated and information dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed his conviction for attempted possession of gambling records in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: To uncover illegal gambling policy slips, a police detective obtained a search warrant to search a restaurant occupied by a named suspect and to search any "persons occupying said premises" pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 690.15(2). The warrant was executed. Defendant, who was not named in the warrant and who was sitting in the restaurant, was told to empty his pockets. He complied and policy slips were recovered. Defendant was charged with attempted possession of gambling records. He moved to suppress the evidence seized on the ground that he was not named or described in the warrant and that the search of his person violated his constitutional rights. The motion was denied, and defendant was convicted. The lower appellate court affirmed. He sought review, arguing the court erred when it refused to suppress the seized evidence. The court vacated the judgment and dismissed the information. It held that even though N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 690.15(2) allowed searches of any person present at the scene, the warrant was too general. The court found the warrant lacked any limitation of the scope of the area to be searched and lacked a sufficiently particular description of its targets.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court's decision, vacated defendant's conviction of attempted possession of gambling records in the second degree, and dismissed the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- search warrants -- search warrant authorizing search of restaurant, named individual, not defendant, and "any other person occupying said premises", issued upon information that named individual received money and policy papers and went into restaurant, is not sufficiently particular with respect to defendant, occupant of restaurant, sitting alone, since there was no probable cause to believe every occupant of specified premises possessed policy slips, and conviction for attempted possession of gambling records is, accordingly, vacated.

1. A search warrant authorizing a search of certain premises, a named individual, not the defendant, and "any other persons occupying said premises" did not meet the particularity requirements of the Fourth Amendment with respect to the defendant and the defendant's conviction for the crime of attempted possession of gambling records should, accordingly, be vacated. The warrant was issued upon information that a "known and convicted policy operator" had several times received money and policy papers in front of the specified premises, a restaurant, and had gone inside after each transaction. Upon execution of the warrant, a search was conducted of the named individual, a companion, and the defendant who was seated alone at a table. Although CPL 690.15 (subd 2) provides for a warrant directing a search of a designated place and any person therein, the description in the warrant and its supporting affidavit must be sufficiently definite to enable the searcher to identify the persons, places or things to be searched. In this case the sufficiency of the description hinges upon whether there was probable cause to believe that each and every occupant of the specified premises possessed the policy slips sought under the warrant. The facts before the issuing Judge, based upon a single, one-hour observation of the exterior of the premises seven days prior to the warrant application, did not support such an inference or indicate that the premises were limited to criminal activity, and the description of the defendant was, therefore, too general.

2. An application for a warrant pursuant to CPL 690.15 must delineate the character of the premises, the nature of the illegal activity, the number and behavior of persons present during the times when the warrant is sought to be executed and whether any person apparently unconnected with the illegal activity has been seen at the premises. The locus of the search must be limited to the area of criminal activity, and, in determining the reasonableness of the application, the necessity for this type of search should be considered.

 

35 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rochester Carting Co. v. Levitt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 264; 326 N.E.2d 808; 367 N.Y.S.2d 242; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1749


February 14, 1975, Argued  
March 24, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the judgment of the trial court that granted an application by the landowner for a judgment directing respondent comptroller to pay an additional sum of post-judgment interest on a judgment of the Court of Claims, and dismissed the petition.


OVERVIEW: The comptroller issued the landowner a check, the balance of principal due and post-judgment interest until 20 days after the time to appeal had expired. The landowner thereupon demanded additional post-judgment interest to cover the further period to the date of actual payment. The comptroller rejected the demand by virtue of the 20-day limitation on post-judgment interest in the Court of Claims Act (Act). The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court and held that the denial of the landowner's request for additional interest was proper because the landowner did not show that the comptroller's payment of judgments was substantially and seriously delayed beyond the 20-day limitation period, as a matter of course. The court found that the delay in payment may have been attributable in large part to the landowner's conceded failure to file a certified copy of the judgment as required by the act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- interest on judgment of Court of Claims -- limitation on post-judgment interest on judgment of Court of Claims from date thereof "until the twentieth day after the comptroller is authorized to issue his warrant for" payment (Court of Claims Act, § 20, subd. 7) is constitutional; it does not deprive claimant of just compensation for appropriation of real property -- substantial delay in payment of judgment beyond 20-day period, not shown by claimant.

1. Subdivision 7 of section 20 of the Court of Claims Act insofar as it limits interest on a Court of Claims judgment against the State from the date thereof "until the twentieth day after the comptroller is authorized to issue his warrant for the payment thereof" is constitutional. That limitation does not deprive a claimant of his right to "just compensation" for the appropriation of real property in eminent domain. The compensation required by the State and Federal Constitutions includes interest on an award from the date of taking to the date of adjudication. Post-judgment interest is awarded as a penalty by legislative grace and, absent a showing of unreasonable delay in payment of the judgment, the 20-day limitation must be sustained.

2. In the instant case in which claimant's land was appropriated and an award made by the Court of Claims, claimant had not proven a substantial delay beyond the 20-day period. Any delay in payment of the claim may be due in large part to claimant's failure to file a certified copy of the judgment (Court of Claims Act, § 20, subd. 6).

 

36 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Rosenthal v. Hartnett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 269; 326 N.E.2d 811; 367 N.Y.S.2d 247; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1750


January 9, 1975, Argued  
March 24, 1975, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs, the administrative determination reinstated and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent hearing officer sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court (New York), which annulled on constitutional grounds pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5601(b)(2), a hearing that was held under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 228 that found petitioner guilty of speeding in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1180(a).


OVERVIEW: Respondent hearing officer challenged the judgment of the appellate court that annulled a hearing of constitutional grounds pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 5601(b)(2) that was held under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law 228 and which found petitioner driver guilty of speeding in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law 1180(a). Respondent challenged the appellate court's judgment that petitioner was deprived due process because he was convicted of speeding by clear and convincing evidence instead of beyond a reasonable doubt. The court reversed and found that the real question was the constitutionality of the substitution in the adjudication functions by an administrative agency and administrative procedures for the courts of criminal jurisdiction and criminal procedures. The court held that the transfer of traffic infractions to an administrative agency was clearly authorized by the legislature for reasons of reducing congestion in the criminal courts. The court rejected petitioner's contention that he was denied due process by the use of the clear and convincing standard in the adjudication of civil fines because fines were routinely imposed under a standard less than beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: A judgment that annulled an administrative hearing that found petitioner driver guilty of speeding by clear and convincing evidence was reversed because the legislature clearly intended to transfer adjudication of traffic offenses to an administrative agency and away from the criminal courts and there was no due process violation because fines were regularly imposed by a standard less than beyond a reasonable doubt.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- administrative adjudication of traffic infractions -- burden of proof -- substitution in adjudication of traffic infractions of administrative agency and administrative procedures for courts of criminal jurisdiction and judicial procedures, where conviction can result only in fines, is not unconstitutional; transfer authorized due to volume of traffic on highways and congestion in criminal courts -- statute (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 227, subd. 1), establishing burden of proof on People in such proceedings as "clear and convincing evidence", does not unconstitutionally deny due process -- nor is there denial of equal protection.

1. Petitioner was found guilty of speeding and fined $ 15 after a hearing before an administrative hearing officer whose determination was affirmed in an administrative review proceeding. Article 2-A of the Vehicle and Traffic Law authorizes administrative adjudication of traffic infractions in cities with populations of 275,000 or more, and subdivision 3 of section 227 mandates that no penalty shall include imprisonment. The substitution in adjudication of traffic infractions of an administrative agency and administrative procedures for courts of criminal jurisdiction and judicial procedures, where conviction can result only in fines with no imprisonment, is not unconstitutional. Strong presumptions of constitutionality attach to any action of the Legislature, and the transfer of traffic infractions to an administrative agency was clearly authorized due to the volume of traffic on the highways and the congestion in criminal courts.

2. Subdivision 1 of section 227 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which establishes that the burden of proof upon the People in such proceedings be by "clear and convincing evidence", does not unconstitutionally deny due process. An incident to the proper transfer of traffic infractions to an administrative agency, where determinations cannot result in imprisonment, is the substitution of an administrative rather than a judicial standard of proof. Civil fines and penalties are routinely imposed by administrative action based on lesser standards than guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.

3. Petitioner was not denied equal protection, since equal protection does not require territorial uniformity of law within a State.

People v. Jackson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


36 N.Y.2d 726; 328 N.E.2d 487; 367 N.Y.S.2d 975; 1975 N.Y. LEXIS 1762
