Lewis v. American Federation of Television & Radio Artists


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 265; 313 N.E.2d 735; 357 N.Y.S.2d 419; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1507; 86 L.R.R.M. 2892


March 27, 1974, Argued  
June 12, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant radio commentator challenged a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order granting respondent union's motion for summary judgment in appellant's action to render union dues a violation of the U.S. Const. amend. I right to freedom of speech.


OVERVIEW: Commentator stated that he was required by his employer to join union, pay dues, and comply with union's orders or directives as a condition of employment. He sought a declaratory judgment that such requirement, as a condition of speaking over radio, violated his right to freedom of speech. Unions cross motion for summary judgment was granted which the appellate division affirmed and further declared that the National Labor Relations Act (NLRA), 29 U.S.C.S. § 158(a)(3), insofar as it authorized union to require commentator to pay dues as a condition to speaking on radio, did not violate the First Amendment. Commentator appealed the judgment. The court of appeals affirmed. It held that the requirement for payment of dues, and the union shop provision were not shown on the record to have violated commentator's rights, nor did such payment amount to a tax upon his right of free speech. The union shop provision in union's contract with employer might bar access of employer's channels to commentator if he ceased to pay dues or their equivalent to union. However, it would not limit his right to freely express his views, or to utilize other channels or avenues not so bound by agreement.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals affirmed the judgment and declaration that the NLRA, insofar as it authorized union to require commentator to pay dues as a condition to speaking on radio, did not violate the First Amendment. If commentator ceased to pay union dues his access to employer's channels might have been barred but his right to freely express his views would not have been limited.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- free speech -- union shop agreement, which has effect of requiring commentator on public affairs to pay union dues as condition of speaking on radio, does not violate his constitutional right of free speech.

The National Labor Relations Act (U. S. Code, tit. 29, § 158, subd. [a], par. [3]), insofar as it authorizes an employer who is involved in interstate commerce to make a union shop agreement with a labor organization which has been duly certified as collective bargaining representative, and insofar as said statute authorizes such certified union to require a person whom said broadcasting network employs as a radio commentator on public affairs to pay dues or their equivalent to the union, does not violate, or amount to a government-authorized tax upon, the commentator's constitutional right to express his views freely on radio (U. S. Const., 1st Amdt., and art. I, § 8, subd. 3). A declaratory judgment to that effect is affirmed.

 

56 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bergstein v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 318; 313 N.E.2d 767; 357 N.Y.S.2d 465; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1510


May 4, 1974, Argued  
June 12, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school board challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's dismissal of respondent teacher's suit alleging termination of his employment in violation of the First and Fourteenth Amendments and ordered a new hearing.


OVERVIEW: The probationary teacher was denied tenure pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 3012. He sued the school board alleging its decision to deny him tenure was based on his activity in political causes and the fact that he was Jewish. The trial court dismissed his complaint. The appellate court ordered a new hearing at which the school board must show that tenure was not denied for impermissible reasons. On appeal the court held (1) the evidence presented did not support the teacher's contentions that the school board denied him tenure for unconstitutional reasons, (2) in ordering a new hearing, the appellate court improperly shifted the burden of proof on the issue of constitutional deprivation from the teacher to the school board, (3) the teacher had the burden of proving that the school board's reasons for denying tenure were improper, (4) testimony by several school board members that they based their votes in part on statements about the teacher made to them by others did not constitute hearsay, and (5) the board members' states of mind were relevant and they could testify to out-of-court statements made by others which circumstantially indicated what a board member believed at the time.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- teachers -- tenure -- petitioner, teacher, failed to substantiate his allegations that he was denied tenure in violation of his constitutional rights as there was no support for his contentions that tenure was denied because of his political philosophy, his method of instructions, his attire or his religious beliefs -- petitioner has burden of proof to produce evidence to show deprivation of his rights -- statements relied on by board members made to them by others tending to establish their state of mind at time they cast their vote were not hearsay.

1. In a proceeding to review the determination of a school board denying tenure to petitioner, a teacher of social studies, petitioner failed to substantiate his allegations that the denial was made in violation of the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the United States Constitution. There is no support for his contentions that tenure was denied because he attended a political rally at other than school hours, he made available to his students a pamphlet explaining student rights under the First Amendment, he wore a western-style hat and he was a member of the Jewish race and religion.

2. In remanding the proceeding for a new hearing to require the board to produce legal and competent evidence to establish that tenure was not denied for impermissible reasons, the Appellate Division improperly shifted the burden of proof to the board. In such cases, the petitioner has the burden of proof to produce such evidence to show deprivation of his rights.

3. The statements relied on by some members of the board made to them by others about petitioner were not hearsay. The truth or falsity of those statements was not at issue but rather the reason for the votes by the board members and, as to that, the statements tended to establish the members' state of mind at the time they cast their votes.

 

57 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Eboli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 281; 313 N.E.2d 746; 357 N.Y.S.2d 435; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1554


March 19, 1974, Argued  
June 5, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), arguing that the felony coercion statute, N.Y. Penal Law § 135.65, violated constitutional guarantees of due process and equal protection, in that the same elements were required for misdemeanor coercion, and the choice to charge as a felony or misdemeanor was left solely to the prosecutor's discretion.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged their convictions, contending that the felony coercion statute, N.Y. Penal Law § 135.65, violated constitutional guarantees of due process and equal protection, in that the same elements were required for misdemeanor coercion under N.Y. Penal Law § 135.60, and the choice to charge as a felony or misdemeanor was left solely to the prosecutor's discretion. While the court agreed that the statutes as written offered wide prosecutorial discretion, it did not agree that the discretion was such as to render either statute unconstitutional. The discretion to decide what was an exceptional case warranting prosecution for the lower degree was entrusted to the prosecutor. More serious crimes should be charged under statutes providing more serious penalties. Thus, the court did not find that such reliance, on its face, violated the constitutional guarantees of due process or equal protection. The order appealed from was accordingly affirmed.


OUTCOME: Finding no constitutional defect in allowing the prosecutor to use discretion in deciding whether to charge felony or misdemeanor coercion, the court affirmed the conviction of appellants.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- coercion -- although definitions of coercion in first degree, felony, and coercion in second degree, misdemeanor, are almost identical when coercion is committed by instilling in victim fear of physical injury or property damage, statute defining coercion as felony does not violate constitutional guarantees of due process and equal protection; opportunity for prosecutorial choice is no bar to prosecution -- there is no claim by defendants, convicted of attempted coercion in first degree, of discrimination or abuse of discretion by prosecutor and testimony supports his choice of felony section -- no objections having been made to charge as to conspiracy in third degree, issue preserved for review.

1. Although the definitions of the crimes of coercion in the first degree, a class D felony (Penal Law, § 135.65), and coercion in the second degree, a class A misdemeanor (Penal Law, § 135.60, subds. 1, 2), are almost identical when the coercion is committed by instilling in the victim fear of physical injury or property damage, the statute defining coercion as a felony does not violate the constitutional guarantees of due process and equal protection. Overlapping in criminal statutes and the opportunity for prosecutorial choice is no bar to prosecution. Read in the light of the Practice Commentaries and the related extortion statutes, there is a guideline differentiating felony and misdemeanor coercion. In general, the misdemeanor is to be charged where the method of coercion is covered by subdivisions 3 to 9 of section 135.60 and the felony is to be charged if the additional element of a threat of personal or property injury is involved.

2. While unlawful discrimination in the exercise of a prosecutor's power to charge would violate the equal protection guarantee, defendants, who were convicted of attempted coercion in the first degree, a class E felony, make no claim of actual discrimination or other actual abuse of discretion and the testimony at the trial supports the District Attorney's choice of the felony section.

3. The issue with respect to the trial court's instructions concerning the elements of conspiracy in the third degree was not preserved for review since no objection was made to the charge.

4. While some confusion was generated by the court's explanation of the difference between conspiracy in the second and third degrees, defendants cannot claim prejudice since they were convicted of the lesser degree.

5. The evidence supporting defendants' conviction of attempted coercion in the first degree, conspiracy in the third degree, and three counts of assault in the third degree was not insufficient as a matter of law.

 

58 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Parsons v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 190; 313 N.E.2d 57; 356 N.Y.S.2d 593; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1579


March 29, 1974, Argued  
May 15, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and judgment of Supreme Court, Albany County, reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant directors challenged a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that reversed, on the law and the facts, a judgment of the trial court in the directors' favor. The directors had sought to cancel notices of tax determination issued by respondent State Tax Commission.


OVERVIEW: The commission served each of the individual directors with a notice for the full amount of the corporation's tax liability. The notices stated that the tax due had been determined in accordance with N.Y. Tax Law § 1138. The first notice was canceled and replaced by a second, again for the full amount of the tax due. This time the notice stated that the named individual was personally liable for the tax. The directors requested a hearing, arguing that because the corporation had filed returns containing correct computations of taxes owing, the commission lacked authority both to have issued the notices of determination against them and to conduct administrative hearings pursuant to the notices. The trial court found in favor the directors and the appellate court reversed, stating the N.Y. Tax Law § 1138 authorized the administrative procedure employed by the commission. On review, the court reversed. The court found that the commission exceeded its statutory authority in proceeding administratively against the directors after the corporation had filed returns containing correct computations of tax liability.


OUTCOME: The appellate court decision that reversed the trial court's holding in favor the directors' in their claim to cancel tax notices was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- sales tax -- State Tax Commission exceeded its statutory authority in proceeding administratively by way of notice of determination and hearing to recover unpaid taxes against petitioners, officers and directors of corporation which had filed returns containing correct computation -- conditions for administrative procedure in section 1138 of Tax Law are not present -- such procedure not authorized by general powers granted in section 1142 (subd. 6) of Tax Law.

1. The State Tax Commission exceeded its statutory authority in proceeding administratively by way of notice of determination and hearing to recover unpaid sales taxes against petitioners, officers and directors of a corporation which had filed timely returns containing correct computations of tax liability.

2. Neither section 1131 (subd. [1]) of the Tax Law, defining "persons required to collect tax", nor section 1133, providing that every person required to collect the tax shall be personally liable, empowers the commission to issue notices of determination and hold hearings pursuant to those notices.

3. The sole provision for administrative procedure is contained in section 1138 of the Tax Law which authorizes (subd. [a]) such procedure if a return is not filed or is incorrect or insufficient and further provides (subd. [b]) that if the commission believes that the collection of the tax will be jeopardized, it may assess the tax prior to the filing of a return and prior to the date when the return is required to be filed. Neither of these conditions is present.

4. The commission's administrative procedure employed against petitioners was not authorized by the general powers granted in section 1142 (subd. 6) of the Tax Law to assess and readjust sales taxes. The Legislature did not intend to extend the commission's authority to use the procedures of section 1138 beyond the bounds of that section. Section 1138 specifies two situations in which the administrative procedure is appropriate and it will not be inferred that such procedure was intended to apply to situations not mentioned. That section contains a strict condition precedent, deposit of the amount of the tax due or an undertaking, to judicial review of a posthearing determination and this will not be extended to situations not expressly prescribed. Finally, where the circumstances specified in section 1138 are not present, an action to enforce payment may be brought and a warrant procedure against property is provided for.

 

59 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kubli v. Rosetti


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 68; 312 N.E.2d 167; 356 N.Y.S.2d 29; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1589


March 22, 1974, Argued  
May 9, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs and disbursements to plaintiff-respondent, and the order of the Appellate Term reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property clerk sought review of the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed the judgment of the appellate term that affirmed a judgment of the civil court in favor of appellant, on appellee finder's claim to a bearer bond, under N.Y. Pers. Prop. Law §§ 251-258.


OVERVIEW: The finder turned over to the property clerk a bearer bond which she had found. After three years had elapsed, the property clerk demanded the bond, pursuant to N.Y. Pers. Prop. Law §§ 251-258. The property clerk refused. On the finder's action to recover the bond, the civil court found for the property clerk and dismissed the complaint. The appellate term affirmed. The appellate division reversed and directed judgment for the finder. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the appellate division and reinstated the order of the appellate term. The court held that the bearer bond fell into the definition of "instrument", under N.Y. Pers. Prop. Law § 251. Thus, the bond could not be returned to the finder but had to be retained in the property clerk's custody pending delivery to the person entitled to it, pursuant to N.Y. Pers. Prop. Law §§ 253 and 255, although it lacked any indicia of ownership on its face and contained no information which could lead the police to discover that person.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division and reinstated the order of the appellate term, thus dismissing the finder's complaint against the property clerk for the return of a bearer bond.


HEADNOTES

Lost property -- unregistered bearer bond -- unregistered United States bearer bond found by plaintiff is "instrument" within meaning of section 251 (subd. 2) of Personal Property Law and cannot be returned to plaintiff as finder (§ 255, subd. 2) but is to be retained in police custody until delivered to person entitled thereto -- Federal Treasury regulation has not pre-empted State policy.

1. An unregistered United States bearer bond found by plaintiff more than three years before the commencement of the present action to recover the bond from the Property Clerk of the New York City Police Department cannot be returned to plaintiff as finder. Subdivision 1 of section 251 of the Personal Property Law defines "property" as money, chattels and tangible personal property other than instruments and subdivision 2 of section 251 defines an "instrument" as a "bond". Lost "property" goes to the finder (§ 253, subd. 8; § 254, subd. 2) and title vests in him whereas a lost "instrument" is to be retained in police custody until delivered to the person entitled thereto (§ 253, subd. 6; § 255, subd. 1). No "instrument" is to be returned to the finder (§ 255, subd. 2).

2. An interpretation of subdivision 2 of section 251 of the Personal Property Law, which defines an "instrument" as a check, draft, promissory note, bond, bill of lading, warehouse receipt, stock certificate or other paper or document, that would limit the term "instrument" to commercial paper and, therefore, except bearer bonds finds no support in the language of the statute.

3. The word "bond" is not qualified by the concluding language in subdivision 2 of section 251 "or other interest in property or in an enterprise", the United States Government allegedly not being an "enterprise", but is qualified by the phrase "evidencing, representing or embodying a chose in action or a right with respect to property". The bond found by plaintiff evidences a right in the bearer to payment.

4. A Federal Treasury regulation providing that the Treasury will pay bearer bonds to the person presenting them and, where they are lost, will not undertake to protect owners has not pre-empted the policy of New York State. The regulation applies only to bearer securities not yet due and, consequently, has no application to the present bond which had matured.

5. Even if the bond were not yet due, questions of title to Federal bearer securities are to be decided by State law.

6. It is for the Legislature to determine whether to protect the expectations of finders of such bonds in the same manner as it already protects the expectations of finders of "property".

 

60 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Association of the Bar v. Lewisohn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 143; 313 N.E.2d 30; 356 N.Y.S.2d 555; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1604


March 28, 1974, Argued  
May 8, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Finance Administrator of the City of New York, sought review of two judgments of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), the first of which affirmed a trial court's order directing respondent club to be removed from the city's tax rolls and the second of which reversed a trial court's judgment and restored the tax exempt status of respondent bar association's property.


OVERVIEW: The association and club owned properties that qualified for the exemption for real property taxes provided by the state constitution and N.Y. Real. Prop. Law § 420 for properties owned by charitable or education organizations and used exclusively for such purposes. Section 420 was amended and renumbered as N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 421, which allowed a city to terminate by local law the exemption for bar associations and scientific organizations. The city adopted such a local law and notified the association and club that their properties would be returned to the tax rolls. On appeal, the court rejected the association and club's argument that they were entitled to a tax exemption for the reason that their activities were by their very nature charitable or educational, and the court held that the properties did not qualify for the exemption under § 421. The court found that the association was not organized and conducted primarily for charitable or educational purposes and that, likewise, the club was not conducted for such purposes. The court further found that § 421, which permitted the return of the properties to the tax rolls, did not violate due process or equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's judgment which affirmed a trial court's order directing the club to be removed from the city's tax rolls and the appellate division's judgment which reversed a trial court's judgment and restored the tax exempt status of the bar association's property.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- exemptions -- pursuant to amendment to section 421 of Real Property Tax Law permitting local governments to terminate tax exemptions for Bar Associations and scientific groups, City of New York validly restored real property of Association of Bar of City of New York and Explorers Club to tax rolls -- Association of Bar is primarily concerned with professional interests of its members and is not conducted primarily for charitable or educational purposes -- Explorers Club is conducted primarily to promote exploration and research in earth sciences -- section 421 does not violate due process or equal protection clauses of Constitutions.

1. Pursuant to an amendment to section 420 (now § 421) of the Real Property Tax Law (L. 1971, ch. 414) which continued tax exemptions for Bar Associations and scientific groups but permitted local governments to terminate the exemption by local law, the City of New York validly restored the real property of the Association of the Bar of City of New York and the Explorers Club to the tax rolls effective January 1, 1972 (Local Laws, 1971, No. 46 of City of New York).

2. The Association of the Bar of the City of New York is not organized and conducted primarily for charitable or educational purposes, for which purposes the exemption still applies, but is an association of professionals primarily concerned with the interests of its members. While its functions may be characterized as educational and, to a lesser extent, as charitable, these are incidental only and while it confers a public benefit, public benefit is not the test for exemption. In addition, Bar Associations have been characterized separately by the Legislature for exemption purposes and the Association has never sought exemption under the broader categories for charitable or educational organizations.

3. The Explorers Club is conducted primarily for scientific purposes. While it has charitable and educational functions, they are adjunctive to its purpose of promoting exploration and research in the earth sciences. The public benefit conferred by the Club is not the test for exemption. In addition, the Legislature has chosen to classify scientific organizations separately and the Club has not heretofore claimed exemption as a charitable or educational organization.

4. Section 421 of the Real Property Tax Law does not violate the due process or equal protection clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions. The Legislature has determined that the continuous removal of real property from the tax rolls imposes a particular hardship on local governments and the taxpayers and that the erosion of municipal tax bases must be stemmed. Consequently, it reasonably drew distinctions between organizations conducted primarily for charitable and educational purposes and those conducted for scientific or Bar Association purposes.

 

61 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cook


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 100; 312 N.E.2d 452; 356 N.Y.S.2d 259; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1638


January 14, 1974, Argued  
May 1, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a manager of a drugstore, was convicted by the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) of violating the Administrative Code of the City of New York by not maintaining a difference in price between brands of cigarettes that had a higher tar content and those which had a lower tar content. Defendant brought an appeal.


OVERVIEW: The defendant admitted that he did not differentiate on the basis of tar and nicotine content in pricing the various brands of cigarettes, stating it was virtually impossible. The defendant contended that the price-differential requirement imposed by the city was illegal. The court held that because the State had the authority under its police power to properly enact a price differential, the question was whether the State had, in any way whatsoever, authorized the city to enact such regulations. The court held that the city was granted the exercise of the police power to promote health by the sovereign people acting through the New York State Constitution and the legislature. The court held that because it was not unreasonable for the city to conclude that the higher prices required of the more harmful cigarettes would induce the cigarette consumer to switch to a less harmful variety, the price differential based on tar and nicotine content was clearly within the police power over matters pertaining to health.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that convicted defendant of not maintaining a difference in price between brands of cigarettes with a higher and those with a lower tar content.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- price differential between cigarette brands -- requirement in New York City Tax Regulations that retailers maintain difference in price between brands of cigarettes that have higher tar and nicotine content and those having lower content and that amount of this price differential be at least equivalent to tax attributable to such content is proper exercise of police power to promote health -- in addition, imposition of price differential is within city's authority under enabling statute.

1. Pursuant to an enabling statute (L. 1952, ch. 235, as amd. by L. 1971, ch. 394), the City of New York placed a tax on the sale of cigarettes based on the tar and nicotine content thereof. The requirement in the city Tax Regulations that retailers maintain a difference in price between brands that have a higher tar and nicotine content and those that have a lower content and that the amount of this price differential be at least equivalent to the tax attributable to such content is a proper exercise of the police power, not inconsistent with any provision of the Constitution or other general law.

2. The City of New York has been granted the exercise of the police power to promote health through the State Constitution and the State Legislature (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 2, subd. [c], [ii], [10]; Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10, subd. 1, [ii], a, [12]; New York City Charter, § 27, subd. a). The price differential is reasonably related to the reduction of the use of the more harmful brands of cigarettes since it is intended to force a consumer to pay a higher price for harmful cigarettes and, therefore, clearly pertains to health.

3. The fact that the differential regulates prices does not render it invalid. Such regulation has a substantial relation to the health objective of reducing the amount of high tar and nicotine cigarettes smoked by city inhabitants.

4. The price differential does not deprive retailers of pricing flexibility permitted by State law. The fact that the State also taxes cigarettes has no significant relation to the city's price differential and, therefore, does not create an inconsistency.

5. There is no requirement that a local law be enacted in response to a uniquely local situation. Since the price differential is not inconsistent with a general State policy, there is no requirement that the problem remedied be based on peculiarly local conditions.

6. Since the enabling statute specifies that it was delegating power to the city to tax the tar and nicotine content of cigarettes and provides for judicial review, it is not violative of section 1 of article XVI of the State Constitution.

7. The enabling statute authorized the city to impose a tax on cigarettes such as the Legislature has the authority to impose and the Legislature has the power to fix prices of commodities. Therefore, the city has the power to impose the tax which includes the price differential. This broad reading is supported by the legislative history of the 1971 amendment.

8. The difficulty in the practical application of the price-differential requirement does not render the regulation unconstitutionally vague and, in any event, defendant has, who sold two brands at the same price neither alleged nor proved any difficulty in ascertaining the proper prices under the differential with respect to the brands of cigarettes which formed the basis of his conviction.

9. Treating vending machines as a separate classification is reasonable.

10. The enactment is not "overbroad" because it cannot be said that the purpose could be as effectively accomplished by advertising or notice requirements.

11. Even if the Federal Public Health Cigarette Smoking Act of 1969 is preemptive, it does not preclude a price differential.

63 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dauernheim, Inc. v. Town Bd. of Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 468; 310 N.E.2d 516; 354 N.Y.S.2d 909; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1675


February 13, 1974, Argued  
March 28, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant board appealed a decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the dismissal of plaintiff landowner's action to have a zoning ordinance declared unconstitutional and entered judgment in favor of the landowner.


OVERVIEW: The landowner's property was zoned for residential use and he sought to have it changed to business use. The landowner brought an action to have the zoning ordinance declared unconstitutional as to his land and asserted that the land was unfit for residential use. The trial court dismissed the action. The appellate division reversed and entered judgment in favor of the landowner. On appeal, the court reversed. The court stated that the landowner did not establish that the land could not yield a reasonable return if used for residential purposes. The court stated that the landowner was entitled to have his property yield a reasonable return and that it did not have to be the most profitable or greatest return. The court stated that the landowner failed to carry the burden and did not show that the statute was unconstitutional therefore the presumption of constitutionality prevailed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's judgment entered in favor of the landowner and reinstated the trial court's judgment that dismissed the landowners action to have a zoning ordinance declared unconstitutional as it applied to the landowner's property.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- complaint in action for zoning change from residential to business use properly dismissed where there was market for residential property in area and plaintiff's property could profitably be developed for that purpose -- zoning was not spot zoning but was part of comprehensive plan for general welfare of community -- ordinance insofar as it affects plaintiff's property is constitutional.

1. In an action in which a zoning change from a residential to a business use is sought, the complaint, alleging that the area in which plaintiff's property is situated has become business and commercial in nature and that the property is unfit for residential use, was properly dismissed. While plaintiff is entitled to a reasonable return on its property, it need not be the greatest return. There was evidence that there was a market for residential property in the area and that plaintiff's property could profitably be developed for that purpose. A slight or even a significant difference in the amount of profit does not ipso facto establish confiscation of the property.

2. The zoning is not spot zoning, but is part of a comprehensive plan of long duration designed for the general welfare of the community. Much of the area around plaintiff's property is zoned and developed as residential and it can be profitable to develop the property in accordance with the zoning classification.

3. The zoning ordinance insofar as it affects plaintiff's property is constitutional. The property is reasonably adapted for the present classification. That a greater profit will result if it is zoned commercial or business does not establish confiscation where the residential use permits a reasonable return. The action of defendants in seeking to maintain the area for residential purposes was a proper one and it cannot be said that the denial of the requested change of use was arbitrary or unreasonable. The presumption of constitutionality prevails.

Hurd v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


34 N.Y.2d 628; 311 N.E.2d 504; 355 N.Y.S.2d 369; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1697


February 14, 1974, Argued  
March 27, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action attacking the constitutionality of N.Y. Local Fin. Law § 11.00(a)(42-a), appellant city requested review from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the judgment finding N.Y. Local Fin. Law § 11.00(a)(42-a) constitutional, in favor of respondent property owners.


OVERVIEW: The city contended that it could exclude from its tax limit the city's future annual requirement for pension and retirement liabilities. The court affirmed the order finding N.Y. Local Fin. Law § 11.00(a)(42-a) unconstitutional and held that the city's future annual requirement for pension and retirement liabilities were not excluded from the tax limit provided in N.Y. Const. art. VIII, §§ 10 & 11 because a current tax-paying generation should not bear a burden other than one that is appropriate to it or shift to future generations a burden not appropriate to them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order finding that the city's policy of excluding the city's future annual requirement for pension and retirement liabilities from its tax limit was unconstitutional.

66 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nival


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 391; 308 N.E.2d 883; 353 N.Y.S.2d 409; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1702


January 18, 1974, Argued  
February 21, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of Appellate Division of Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified and, as modified, affirmed the trial court's judgment rendered upon a verdict convicting defendant of robbery in the second degree and grand larceny in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery in the second degree and grand larceny in the third degree. At the trial, the victim was unable to identify defendant. She did testify, however, to her previous identification of two men shortly after the robbery. A police detective subsequently testified that defendant was one of the persons that the victim identified at that time. The court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that the detective's testimony was properly admissible to establish defendant's identity. Under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 60.25, once a witness testified to a previous identification, the fact that defendant was the person whom the witness identified could be established by testimony of another person to whom the witness promptly declared his recognition at the time he made the prior identification. The court rejected defendant's argument that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 60.25 should not be applied since defendant was indicted before the effective date of that section and that to apply the statute would be a violation of the constitutional prohibition on ex post facto laws. A statute authorizing the use of evidence not previously admissible was not an ex post facto law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, holding that detective's testimony as to victim's identification of defendant after robbery was properly admissible to establish defendant's identity. New York statute that authorized such testimony could be retroactively applied because statutes authorizing use of evidence not previously admissible were not ex post facto laws.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- identification of defendant -- upon failure of robbery victim to identify defendant at trial, although victim did testify to her previous identification of two men shortly after robbery, testimony of detective that defendant was one of persons identified by victim in presence of detective was admissible in evidence ( CPL 60.25) -- while defendant was indicted before effective date of CPL 60.25, to apply it to present case would not violate prohibition against ex post facto laws.

1. Although the victim of a robbery was unable to identify defendant at the time of the trial, she did testify to her previous identification of two men at her store shortly after the robbery. The testimony of a detective that defendant was one of the persons that the victim identified at her store in the presence of the detective was admissible in evidence. CPL 60.25 applies to a situation where the witness cannot make an in-court identification but has on a previous occasion identified defendant. Under such circumstances any other witness may then establish that the defendant is the same person that the eyewitness identified on the previous occasion. The testimony of the witness and the other person taken together constitutes "evidence in chief" ( CPL 60.25, subd. 2).

2. While defendant was indicted before the effective date of CPL 60.25, to apply that section in the present case would not be a violation of the constitutional prohibition as to ex post facto laws. A statute authorizing the use of evidence not previously admissible is not an ex post facto law.

 

67 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Donner v. New York City Employees' Retirement System


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 413; 308 N.E.2d 896; 353 N.Y.S.2d 428; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1704


January 8, 1974, Argued  
February 21, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which unanimously affirmed a judgment of the trial court in a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, denying the employee's application to re-enroll as a member of the city retirement system upon his re-entry into city employment.


OVERVIEW: The employee entered into a city retirement system contract in 1941, which contained a right of re-entry into the system if he became a city employee again, before attaining age 70, after he had originally retired. When the city re-hired the employee, he sought re-entry into the retirement system, which was denied. The court reversed and remitted, holding that: (1) under the express language of the retirement statute, the employee's membership in the retirement system ceased when he retired in 1955 and was granted an annual retirement allowance; (2) when the employee re-entered city employment in 1968, at age 67, his retirement allowance ceased; (3) the fact the retirement system lower its re-enrollment age from 70 to 65 before the employee re-entered the system did not control; (4) the employee's opportunity to re-enter the retirement system, while conditional upon being rehired by the city, was nevertheless a retirement benefit; and (5) the city was not obliged to re-employ the employee, but having done so, the conditional aspect of the benefit was satisfied and the employee had a right to be re-enrolled as a member. This right was protected N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that affirmed the trial court's judgment denying the employee's application to re-enroll as a member of the city retirement system upon his re-entry into city employment and remitted the case to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- retirement -- New York City -- amendment to section B3-47.0 of Administrative Code lowering age from 70 to 65 at which retiree may re-enter Retirement System upon being rehired by city may not be applied to petitioner who re-entered city service at age 67 since when he became member of system maximum age was 70 and to deprive petitioner of such retirement benefit would be unconstitutional.

1. Petitioner became a member of the New York City Employees' Retirement System in 1941 when section B3-47.0 of the Administrative Code provided that a member who had retired had the right to again become a member of the Retirement System if he were re-employed by the city before attaining the age of 70. Petitioner retired in 1955 and thereby became a beneficiary of the system. Fifteen days before petitioner re-entered city service in 1968 at the age of 67, that section was amended to lower from 70 to 65 the maximum age at which a beneficiary could again become a member of the system. To apply the amendment to petitioner and deprive him of such a benefit would be unconstitutional. (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 7.) Upon becoming a member of the system, one of the benefits to which petitioner was entitled was to re-enter the system until age 70 upon re-employment and that benefit has been diminished.

2. The opportunity to re-enter the Retirement System, conditional upon being rehired by the city, was a retirement benefit and while the city was not obliged to rehire petitioner, having done so, the condition was satisfied and petitioner had a right to be re-enrolled as a member.

3. The fact that petitioner was not a "member" of the Retirement System at the time of the amendment has no bearing on the issue. The constitutional shield would be ineffective if it could be pierced by denominating potential recipients of retirement benefits as "members" or "beneficiaries".

70 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Taylor v. Sise


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 357; 308 N.E.2d 442; 352 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1974 N.Y. LEXIS 1763


January 18, 1974, Argued  
February 12, 1974, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant indicted individuals sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed the indicted individuals' petitions filed to prohibit appellee judges from presiding over their trials, on the ground that the statute that authorized the judges' appointment, the Emergency Dangerous Drug Control Act, 1973 N.Y. Laws 603, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: There were two separate groups of indicted individuals. The court held that the group that challenged the constitutionality of the legislation had standing but the group that challenged the legislation as violating the constitutional doctrine of separation of powers did not have standing. The court affirmed the dismissal of the petitions. The court determined that nothing in the state constitution guaranteed a defendant charged with a felony the right to have been tried before an elected rather than an appointed judge. The order that appointed the judges was designed to have accomplished the purpose of the constitutional requirement of a temporary assignment. The court held that it would not substitute its own judgment for that of the legislature in determining the particular method that should have been used to meet a given need. The court decided that there was no constitutional infirmity in the actions of any of the three branches of government.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's dismissal of the indicted individuals' petitions that alleged that the appointment of the judges was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- assignment of additional Court of Claims Judges to Supreme Court -- Emergency Dangerous Drug Control Act authorizing appointment of up to 68 additional Judges of Court of Claims to provide Judges for anticipated increase in dangerous drug felony prosecutions is constitutional -- Constitution authorizes temporary assignment of Judge of Court of Claims to Supreme Court to have powers and duties of Justice thereof and nothing in Constitution guarantees defendant charged with felony in New York City right to be tried before elected Supreme Court Justice -- designation of newly appointed Judges of Court of Claims to act as Supreme Court Justices in Criminal Term by order of Appellate Division is proper temporary assignment.

1. The Emergency Dangerous Drug Control Act (L. 1973, ch. 603) insofar as it authorizes the appointment of up to 68 additional Judges of the Court of Claims, in order to provide Judges to preside over an anticipated increase in dangerous drug felony prosecutions as a result of the new law, is constitutional. The Legislature is authorized to increase the number of Court of Claims Judges (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 9) and it is of no legal or practical consequence that it set an outer limit of 68 new Judges rather than fixing the number at that figure.

2. While the Constitution provides for the election of Supreme Court Justices (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 6, subd. c) and further provides that, in New York City, the Supreme Court has exclusive jurisdiction over crimes prosecuted by indictment (art. VI, § 7, subd. a), the Constitution also authorizes the temporary assignment of a Judge of the Court of Claims to the Supreme Court (art. VI, § 26, subd. b), and provides that while temporarily so assigned a Judge shall have the powers, duties and jurisdiction of a Justice of the court to which he is assigned (art. VI, § 26, subd. k). Therefore, nothing in the Constitution guarantees a defendant, charged with a felony in New York City, the right to be tried before an elected rather than an appointed acting Supreme Court Justice.

3. The order of the Appellate Division designating newly appointed Judges of the Court of Claims to act as Supreme Court Justices in the Criminal Term "until the further order of this Court" accomplishes the purpose of the constitutional requirement of a temporary assignment. The indefiniteness of the assignment is equivalent to a temporary designation.

4. The Legislature agreed with the judicially expressed concern that the stricter sentences and limitations on acceptances of lesser pleas to higher crimes charged in the new drug law created an emergency need for additional Judges and the court should not substitute its judgment for that of the Legislature in determining the particular method to meet the need.

5. While the device used to increase the number of Judges to try felony cases has the effect of minimizing whatever purpose is evident in the Constitution to provide for an elective Supreme Court to try such cases, this cross-working of purposes is not equivalent to an ambiguity or conflict in the grant of constitutional powers requiring a court to look beyond the literal language and narrow or restrict the otherwise unqualified grant of power.

6. The contention of petitioners that the acting Supreme Court Justices are likely to be partial or subject to improper executive influence is unacceptable. Moreover, given the validity of the statute, the argument becomes irrelevant.

7. Petitioners have standing to challenge the constitutionality of the appointment and designation of those Judges who are to preside at petitioners' trials, since their interest in the constitutionality of the legislation is personal, direct and substantial.

8. Those petitioners, who allege that the requiring of participation by the executive branch of government in proposing a plan for the efficient use of judicial resources violates the doctrine of separation of powers, are not directly affected by that provision so as to afford them standing to challenge its constitutionality. Nor will the constitutional question presented affect the validity of any orders or judgments of the acting Supreme Court Justices to warrant the court's consideration in the absence of standing.

 

71 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Avant


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 265; 307 N.E.2d 230; 352 N.Y.S.2d 161; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 839


April 26, 1973, Argued  
December 28, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that reversed an order granting their motion to dismiss indictments charging defendants with offering false instruments for filing in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 175.35, and grand larceny in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §155.35, and ordered that the indictments against them be reinstated.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with offering false instruments for filing in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 175.35 and grand larceny in violation of N.Y. Penal Law §155.35. The appellate court reversed the order that granted defendants' motion to dismiss the indictments. Noting that the Supreme Court rendered N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law §§ 103(a) and (b) unconstitutional, the court held that the State could compel any person enjoying a public trust to account for his activities and could terminate services if he refused to answer relevant questions. However, testimony compelled under threat of loss of public employment was inadmissible and could not be used as a basis for subsequent prosecution. The grand jury considered evidence obtained at the expense of a constitutional right, which generally did not require indictment dismissal, and was fatal only when the remaining legal evidence was insufficient to sustain the indictment. However, the Steuding rule was applicable because defendants were a "target" of the investigation. In addition to the dismissal requirement, an exclusionary rule protected defendants from indictment based on any incriminating testimony, and from the use of such evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reinstated the order granting defendants' motion to dismiss the indictments against them. The court ruled that although the statutes were unconstitutional, defendants were not immune from future prosecution, nor had the municipality lost the right to call upon them to account for their public trust.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- immunity from prosecution -- defendants, who had contracted with city whose purchasing practices were being investigated, executed waivers of immunity and Grand Jury returned indictments against them -- General Municipal Law ( §§ 103-a, 103-b) which requires such waiver of immunity, unconstitutional -- rule applicable to public employees not different from that applicable to public contractors -- since "target" of investigation testified, indictment must be dismissed -- reindictment possible if sufficient independent evidence is adduced to support it.

1. Defendants, under subpoena, appeared before a Grand Jury investigating the purchasing practices of a city with which they had entered into a contract, executed a limited waiver of immunity and surrendered the subpoenaed records. Subsequently the Grand Jury returned two indictments against them. Section 103-b of the General Municipal Law provides that any person who, when called before a Grand Jury to testify in an investigation concerning any transaction or contract had with the State or any political subdivision thereof, refuses to sign a waiver of immunity or to answer any relevant question should be disqualified from thereafter selling to or submitting bids to or receiving awards from or entering into any contracts with any municipal corporation, for a stated period. A statement obtained pursuant to this statute is coerced within the meaning of the Fifth Amendment and may not be used at a subsequent criminal prosecution. The fact that the defendants are public contractors and not public employees (cf. Garrity v. New Jersey, 385 U.S. 493; Gardner v. Broderick, 392 U.S. 273) or license holders (cf. Spevack v. Klein, 385 U.S. 511) does not render a different rule applicable.

2. The general rule that the submission of some inadmissible evidence during the course of the proceeding is fatal only when the remaining legal evidence is insufficient to sustain the indictment is inapplicable when the Grand Jury has considered testimony from a witness who is a "target" of the investigation and, in this case, defendants fall into this category. The violation of the constitutional privilege carries with it a dismissal of the indictment.

3. Reindictment is possible, however, if sufficient evidence, independent of the evidence, links, or leads furnished by the prospective defendant, is adduced to support it.

4. The law in effect at the time defendants appeared before the Grand Jury required an affirmative claim of the privilege against self incrimination and an invalid waiver, as was present in this case, would not confer full transactional immunity.

5. Sections 103-a and 103-b of the General Municipal Law as presently enacted are unconstitutional.

 

72 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Moore v. Metropolitan Life Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 304; 307 N.E.2d 554; 352 N.Y.S.2d 433; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 843


November 15, 1973, Argued  
December 28, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: In the first above-entitled appeal: Order affirmed, with costs to appellant.

In the second above-entitled appeal: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, First Judicial Department (New York), granting defendant's motion to dismiss the complaint insofar as it asserted a class action on an insurance policy, and defendant challenged a grant of summary judgment to plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a state employee who received medical insurance from defendant insurer, argued that N.Y. Ins. Law § 221(5)(e) required defendant to reimburse him for services rendered by a licensed psychologist, even though the policy did not cover such services. Defendant argued that the statute did not apply because the policy was issued before the statute's effective date. The court noted that the statute required insurers to provide coverage for services rendered by a licensed psychologist if their policies covered similar services rendered by physicians, and that defendant's policy did so. The court then noted that defendant could renew or discontinue coverage annually. The court found that the statute applied to defendant because defendant had not discontinued coverage on the first renewal date after the statute's enactment. The court also agreed that a class action was not warranted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders, holding that the case did not warrant class action status, and that the insurance law amendment applied even though it was enacted after the policy issued, because the policy was renewed annually and defendant could terminate coverage or increase premiums each year.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- group medical expense insurance -- 1971 amendment to section 221 (subd. 5, par. [e]) of Insurance Law providing for reimbursement to subscribers of group health insurance policies for services of psychologists and stating that amendment was prospective in application is applicable to group health policy issued in 1957 which provided that term of policy was for one year and that policy was renewable from year to year unless terminated -- since insurer had option not to renew or to change premium, prospective application of statute not violative of constitutional prohibition against impairment of obligation of pre-existing contract -- class action properly dismissed.

1. A 1971 amendment to section 221 (subd. 5, par. [e]) of the Insurance Law stated that whenever a group health insurance policy provides for reimbursement to a physician or a psychiatrist for treatment of mental or emotional disorders, a subscriber thereto shall be entitled to reimbursement for such services whether performed by a physician, psychiatrist, or psychologist and was to apply only to policies written or renewed on or after its effective date. That amendment is applicable to a group major medical expense insurance policy issued in 1957 to cover employees of the State of New York which policy provides that its term is for one year and it is renewable from year to year unless terminated by written notice. Therefore, plaintiff is entitled to be reimbursed for a course of treatment with a psychologist for a mental ailment.

2. Since the defendant insurance company had the option not to renew the contract on the next anniversary date of the policy following enactment of the statute or to change the premium, the policy was modified upon renewal by operation of law to include reimbursement for services of psychologists and the prospective application of the statute does not violate the constitutional prohibition against the impairment of an obligation of a pre-existing contract.

3. The statute is not inapplicable to a policy covering "medical services" on the ground that psychologists are not permitted to practice medicine. Some services rendered by a physician may be rendered by a psychologist and where the practices overlap the statute mandates reimbursement for psychologists' services.

4. A class action by plaintiff on behalf of all other similarly situated employees of the State was properly dismissed. The complaint merits no such procedure. Pending legislation providing for a broadened scope and more liberal procedure for class actions is preferable to judicial development in the same direction.

 

73 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Heller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 314; 307 N.E.2d 805; 352 N.Y.S.2d 601; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 844


October 16, 1973, Argued  
December 28, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargued October 16, 1973.



DISPOSITION: In People v. Heller: Upon reargument: Order affirmed.

In People v. Buckley and Goldstein: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the United States Supreme Court, which vacated the judgment of the Court of Appeals (New York), affirming appellants' convictions for violations of N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were convicted of misdemeanors because they violated N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05, which prohibited the promotion or production of obscene material. The United States Supreme Court vacated the convictions and remanded the case to reconsider New York's penal law dealing with obscenity and pornography in light of its Miller decision, which left to local government to determine what is obscenity in the respective communities in which such decisions may be promulgated. The court found that N.Y. Penal Law § 235.01 sufficiently described the prohibited conduct; therefore, it satisfied the void-for-vagueness test. Further, the court found that the statutes complied with the Miller limits as written and construed because they prohibited commercial exploitation of hard core pornography. The court concluded that the state's hard core pornography standard did not go beyond the Miller standard. Accordingly, appellants' convictions were reinstated.


OUTCOME: In each appellants' case, the order of the appellate term was affirmed and the convictions reinstated because the courts below employed standards entirely in accord with applicable state law.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- Penal Law ( § 235.00) is constitutional -- obscenity trial guidelines stated -- State-wide standard, not local community standard, governs -- Trial Judge must preliminarily determine that film or other work lacks any social value, before he can submit this question to jury -- mere pretense or interspersing of some nonpornographic matter should be disregarded -- Judge who personally views film may, without first conducting adversary hearing, issue warrant for seizure of copy of film as evidence.

1. The definition of criminal obscenity in the Penal Law ( § 235.00) is sufficiently specific and is constitutional. ( Miller v. California, 413 U.S. 15, followed.)

2. In a prosecution under that statute the basic guidelines for the triers of fact are: (a) whether an ordinary adult would find that the film, magazine or other work taken as a whole, appeals to the prurient interest, and (b) whether the work depicts or describes, in a patently offensive way, sexual conduct specifically defined by section 235.00, and (c) whether the work taken as a whole lacks serious literary, artistic, political or scientific value. ( Miller v. California, 413 U.S. 15, followed.)

3. Guideline questions (a) and (b) above must be determined, not according to any local community standards, but according to the State-wide standard through the construction given by the appellate courts. ( Miller v. California, 413 U.S. 15, explained.)

4. Guideline question (c) above can be determined by the triers of fact only after the Trial Judge, in the absence of the jury and without disclosing his determination to them, has independently and fully resolved this issue against the accused, as a preliminary matter of law.

5. Guideline (c) above is set forth in the Penal Law ( § 235.00, subd. 1, cl. [c]) in these words: "it is utterly without redeeming social value ". Properly construed, these words mean that a mere pretense or interspersing of some nonpornographic matter should be disregarded.

6. A Judge who is authorized to issue warrants and who has personally viewed a motion picture film and has found it to be obscene may, without first conducting an adversary hearing on the issue of probable obscenity, issue a warrant for the seizure of a copy of the film as evidence in an obscenity prosecution against the exhibitor. ( Heller v. New York, 413 U.S. 483.)

7. Accordingly, judgments convicting the defendants of promoting obscenity (Penal Law, § 235.05) are affirmed.

People v. Bush


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 921; 308 N.E.2d 451; 352 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 858


November 19, 1973, Argued  
December 28, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed his conviction for felony murder and manslaughter in the second degree. He contended that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 295-1 was unconstitutional because it did not require reciprocity in requiring notice of an alibi defense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with felony murder and common-law murder. At the time of defendant's trial in 1971, § 295-1 allowed the prosecutor to demand defendant to identify his alibi defense if he intended to offer one at trial. If defendant did not comply and serve a bill of particulars, the trial court could exclude any alibi testimony offered by defendant at trial. Defendant contended that the absence of reciprocity in the notice-of-alibi statute rendered it unconstitutional. The court agreed and held the statute to be unconstitutional. However, under the general principle allowing a court to make such rulings prospective in criminal litigation, the court ordered that its ruling applied only to cases in which a trial was commenced after June 11, 1973. It noted that the application of § 295-1 in defendant's case did not operate to exclude any alibi testimony. Defendant furnished the names of his alibi witnesses on demand and their testimony was admitted at trial. Although defendant was denied advance notice of the identity of the prosecution's rebuttal witnesses, he was not inhibited in his own opportunity to establish his alibi.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder -- notice of alibi -- selection of jury -- contentions by defendant, convicted of felony murder and manslaughter, that trial court's selection of jury under procedure then mandated by Judicial Conference (22 NYCRR 20.13) was violative of his rights to fair trial, that absence of reciprocity in notice-of-alibi statute (Code Crim. Pro., § 295-1) rendered it unconstitutional, that record did not support trial court's finding as to voluntariness of statements made by him, that remarks made by prosecutor during summation deprived him of fair trial, and that trial court committed reversible errors in its charge -- judgment of conviction was properly affirmed -- however, section 295-1 of Code of Criminal Procedure is held unconstitutional, with this decision to apply to cases in which trial began after June 11, 1973, date of United States Supreme Court decision holding similar notice-of-alibi statute of Oregon unconstitutional ( Wardius v. Oregon, 412 U.S. 470), but not to cases where trial began prior to that date unless defendant was prevented from introducing testimony to support his alibi defense as sanction for his failure to comply with notice-of-alibi statute -- Court of Appeals does not reach question of retroactivity on direct appeal in such cases -- defendant's other contentions are without merit.

Myerson v. Lentini Bros. Moving & Storage Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 250; 306 N.E.2d 804; 351 N.Y.S.2d 687; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 869


September 19, 1973, Argued  
December 27, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant carrier challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the order of the trial court that granted the motion to compel compliance of a subpoena duces tecum that was issued by respondent, the Commissioner of the Department of Consumer Affairs of the City of New York (Commissioner), pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2308(b).


OVERVIEW: Following an investigation by the city, the Commissioner issued a subpoena duces tecum to the carrier, requesting all of its books and records. The Commissioner's justification for the subpoena was that numerous complaints had been issued against the carrier. When the carrier refused to comply, the Commissioner filed a motion to compel, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 2308(b), which the trial court granted. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed and concluded that the subpoena was overbroad and that the Commissioner failed to provide sufficient justification for its request. The court reasoned that allowing such a broad subpoena would subject innocent parties to administrative abuse. The court denied the motion to compel and granted the carrier's motion to quash the subpoena.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court and denied the Commissioner's motion to compel and granted the carrier's motion to quash the subpoena duces tecum.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- subpoena duces tecum -- subpoena duces tecum requiring production of all business records for 17 months will not be judicially enforced upon general allegations by local consumer protection agency of "numerous complaints" -- subpoena quashed, without prejudice to city commissioner's renewal of motion to compel compliance based on proper foundation, or making more inquiries into complaints and issuing more limited subpoena, based on sufficient factual showing -- Transportation Law ( § 160) does not pre-empt field or exclude municipal consumer protection law.

1. Acting under New York City's Consumer Protection Law of 1969 (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 64), which is designed to prohibit deceptive or unconscionable trade practices by those who provide consumer goods or services, the city's Commissioner of Consumer Affairs issued a broad subpoena duces tecum to a corporation which is in the business of moving household goods within the city; and subsequently the commissioner moved for an order to compel compliance with the subpoena ( CPLR 2308, subd. [b]). The subpoena would require the corporation to produce practically all of its books and records for the preceding 17 months. The commissioner averred that she had received "numerous complaints" of underestimates and overcharges which led her to believe that the corporation was engaged in a pattern of deceptive practices. Such a general averment cannot support such a broad subpoena duces tecum issued by a local regulatory agency. Although the courts do not require a municipal regulatory agency to show probable cause for the issuance of a subpoena, the courts must guard innocent parties against the possibilities of administrative abuse of power and harassment.

2. Accordingly, judicial enforcement of the subpoena is denied and the subpoena is quashed. Of course, the commissioner may renew the motion based on a sufficient showing, may make additional preliminary inquiries into recent complaints, or may issue a subpoena of limited scope, with proper supportive foundation, and then apply anew for judicial enforcement.

3. The Transportation Law ( § 160), which regulates motor carriers of household goods in New York City, does not pre-empt the field and exclude a local law, such as New York City's Consumer Protection Law, from regulating carriers of household goods within the city.

Jones v. State


Claim No. 54555


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 275; 307 N.E.2d 236; 352 N.Y.S.2d 169; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 870


October 15, 1973, Argued  
December 27, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, and case remitted to Court of Claims for further proceedings in accordance with opinion herein, and, as so modified, order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant widow sought review of the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order denying a motion by respondent state to dismiss the claim for failure to state a cause of action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant widow filed a complaint against respondent state alleging in her first action that respondent was negligent in failing to warn the decedent of an impending riot of which respondent had, or should have had, knowledge. The second action was based upon intentional tort. It alleged that during the forceful retaking of the prison where decedent was employed as a clerical worker, respondent's trooper, without just cause or provocation, willfully and intentionally assaulted and battered appellant's husband by firing several shots, one or more of which struck her husband and caused his death. The appellate court granted respondent's motion to dismiss, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed as to the dismissal of the negligence action, holding that appellant was relegated to workmen's compensation as her exclusive remedy. However, the court reversed the dismissal of the second cause of action, holding that it properly alleged that respondent's trooper willfully and intentionally assaulted appellant's husband. The court held that respondent could be held liable for an assault if it was without cause or provocation and, thus, dismissal of the action was improper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's dismissal of appellant widow's negligence action, holding that appellant was relegated to workmen's compensation as her exclusive remedy. However, the court reversed the dismissal of the second cause of action, which alleged an intentional tort, holding that appellant properly asserted that respondent state's trooper willfully and intentionally assaulted her husband without cause or provocation.


HEADNOTES

State -- liability for death of prison employee -- claim for death of decedent, clerk employed at Attica State Correctional Facility who was taken hostage during riot, properly dismissed; claimant's exclusive remedy is workmen's compensation since it was alleged that State was negligent in failing to warn decedent of riot while decedent was performing his duties -- claim alleging that State trooper, during forceful retaking of prison "wilfully and intentionally assaulted" decedent causing his death states cause of action; use of excessive force would be sufficient to render State liable.

1. Claimant's decedent, employed by the State Department of Correctional Services as an accounts clerk at Attica Correctional Facility was taken hostage during the 1971 riot and was killed when the prison was retaken by law officers. Claimant's first cause of action to recover for decedent's death which alleged that the State was negligent in failing to warn decedent of the impending riot of which the State had, or should have had, knowledge was properly dismissed. Claimant is relegated to workmen's compensation as her exclusive remedy since the claim alleges that the State was negligent while decedent was performing his duties as clerk.

2. The second claim alleging that a State trooper, during the forceful retaking of the prison, "wilfully and intentionally assaulted" decedent causing his death states a cause of action. With the waiver of immunity found in section 8 of the Court of Claims Act, the State became liable for the torts of its agents on the basis of respondeat superior, notwithstanding that the agent was engaged in a"governmental" activity. The use of excessive force in the retaking would be sufficient to render the State liable.

3. Claimant faces a heavy burden of proof to establish the use of excessive force and in the light of all the circumstances dismissal of the second cause of action was inappropriate.
