Corr v. Westchester County Dep't of Social Services


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 111; 305 N.E.2d 483; 350 N.Y.S.2d 401; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 919


October 12, 1973, Argued  
November 21, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a judgment of the trial court annulling the denial by respondent Westchester County Department of Social Services (local department) of an application for medical assistance and the determination of respondent State Department of Social Services (state department) affirming that determination.


OVERVIEW: The applicant was a lifelong resident of the state before moving for a short time to New Jersey to care for her ailing son. After her son died, she returned to New York, where most of her family lived. She was immediately hospitalized and then took residence in a nursing home, where she was expected to stay for the remainder of her life. The local department decision denying her application for medical benefits due to lack of qualifying residency was affirmed by the state department, and annulled by the trial court. On appeal from the intermediate appellate division's reversal of the trial court's judgment, dismissal of the petition, and confirmation of respondents' determinations, the court reversed. The court held that the applicant established a residence in the state for purposes of N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 366-a even though such was initiated by and with the purpose of her being placed in a hospital. The court held that the locality of the applicant's residence in the state could not define residence for purposes of public assistance to exclude those living in institutions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the intermediate appellate division's order annulling the trial court judgment, and reinstated the trial court's judgment annulling respondents' denial of the applicant's request for medical benefits.


HEADNOTES

Social services -- medicaid -- petitioner, out-of-State resident hospitalized upon coming into New York and thereafter transferred to nursing home, acquired New York residence for purposes of qualifying for medical assistance payments -- petitioner has established nursing home as her domicile -- qualification for assistance may not be conditioned on minimum period of residence.

1. Petitioner, 89 years old and suffering from generalized arteriosclerosis and unable to care for herself, had spent 78 years of her life in New York State and of these, 47 in Westchester County. Having moved to New Jersey in 1962 to live with her ailing son who died in 1969 and then wanting to move closer to her remaining children who lived in Westchester County, she returned to New York in November, 1971 and was immediately hospitalized and thereafter transferred to a nursing home in Westchester where, in February, 1972, she registered to vote based on her nursing home address. Based upon her prior relationships with New York State, petitioner has established a residence in New York for purposes of qualifying for medical assistance payments (Social Services Law, § 366-a) even though her return to New York was initiated by and with the purpose of her being placed in a hospital.

2. Although a patient or inmate of an institution ordinarily does not gain or lose a residence or domicile but retains the domicile he had when he entered the institution, the conduct of the petitioner establishes that the nursing home is to be her permanent home or domicile.

3. Qualification for assistance may not be conditioned on any minimum period of residence.

 

80 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


241 East 22nd St. Corp. v. City Rent Agency


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 134; 305 N.E.2d 760; 350 N.Y.S.2d 631; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 922


October 10, 1973, Argued  
November 21, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlords sought review of two decisions by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which held that 1971 N.Y. Laws 1012 amending the Local Emergency Rent Control Act was constitutional and that the New York City, N.Y., Rent, Eviction and Rehabilitation Regulations amend. 33 was invalid.


OVERVIEW: In two separate actions, the landlords challenged the constitutionality of Amendment 33. The appellate court determined that, pursuant to the Local Emergency Rent Control Act, the housing commissioner had the authority to declare the amendment invalid because it was more restrictive or stringent than prior regulations. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court decisions. The court concluded that: (1) the power granted to the housing commissioner did not violate the state constitution; (2) the housing commissioner reasonably exercised his power after finding that the amendment was more restrictive or stringent than present law, which resulted in the landlords being deprived of compensation to which they were entitled; (3) while the standard followed by the housing commissioner was rather broad, the complexity of rent controls and the presumption of constitutionality attached to legislative enactments persuaded the court that the standard was sufficient and the statute should be upheld; and (4) the landlords were entitled to have their hardship applications processed consistent with the law and regulations absent the challenged amendment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed both orders of the appellate court and held that the hardship applications presented by the landlords were to be construed absent consideration of the challenged amendment to the rent control laws.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- chapter 1012 of Laws of 1971 giving State Housing Commissioner veto power over regulations affecting controlled housing when proposed regulation is more restrictive than provisions previously in force does not violate home rule provisions of Constitution -- there is no unlawful delegation of legislative power without standards -- disapproval by commissioner of amendment 33 of New York City's Rent Regulations as more restrictive was reasonable.

1. Chapter 1012 of the Laws of 1971 which, in effect, reposes a veto power in the State Housing Commissioner over regulations affecting controlled housing accommodations when the proposed regulation is more stringent or restrictive than the provisions previously in force, does not violate the home rule provisions of the State Constitution. Chapter 1012 is general legislation applicable to any city of the State having a population of one million or more inhabitants, despite the fact that there might be but one city to which it could apply. Moreover, the subject of rent control is primarily a matter of State concern and a function of the State at large. Chapter 1012, therefore, does not relate to the property, affairs or government of the City of New York within the home rule provision (art. IX, § 2, subd. [b], par. [2]) of the Constitution.

2. Chapter 1012 does not unlawfully delegate legislative authority without standards to guide the exercise thereof. In exercising his discretion to approve or withhold approval of a more restrictive regulation than those previously in effect, the commissioner's power is circumscribed by the statement of policy contained in the Local Emergency Rent Control Act, which chapter 1012 amends, that the transition from regulation to a normal market of free bargaining between landlord and tenant is the objective. While this is a broad standard to guide the commissioner's discretion, in view of the strong presumption of constitutionality attaching to legislative enactments and the complexity of rent control and regulation, such standard is sufficient.

3. Amendment 33 of the City of New York's Rent Regulations, effective January 1, 1972, amended the provisions dealing with ("hardship") adjustments based on net annual return to the owner-landlord to conform them to the procedures and standards of the computerized maximum base rent (MBR) system. In particular, an apartment at its individualized MBR could not be assessed its share of the hardship increase even though the building as a whole might not be yielding its prescribed statutory return of 8.5%. In determining that Amendment 33 was more restrictive than existing regulations because it bars or limits the collectibility of an increase in rent which is warranted under previous regulations without compensating landlord for the loss of rent to which he is entitled, the commissioner acted reasonably.

 

81 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kesselbrenner v. Anonymous


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 161; 305 N.E.2d 903; 350 N.Y.S.2d 889; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 925


October 17, 1973, Argued  
November 21, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mentally ill patient challenged the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's order that denied respondent director of a state mental hospital's (director) motion to transfer the patient to a high security state hospital because he was determined to be dangerously mentally ill under former N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 85.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied the director's request to have the patient transferred to a state hospital for patient's who were criminally convicted. The appellate court reversed and determined that the patient was dangerously mentally ill under former § 85 and ordered him transferred to the state criminal hospital. The patient appealed. The court reversed and reinstated the trial court's order. The court held that the law was unconstitutional insofar as it mandated that dangerously mentally ill patients in civil state hospitals, whose confinement was not based on a criminal charge or conviction, be transferred to a state hospital, which was a correctional facility for mentally ill convicted criminals. To have subjected the patient to a greater deprivation of personal liberty than was necessary to achieve the purpose for which he was being confined violated due process. While the patient was dangerously mentally ill, his confinement, necessary for the protection of others, to be constitutional, must have been therapeutic, not punitive. There was no way to construe former § 85 to preserve its constitutionality because it provided no substitute for such a transfer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court that transferred the patient to a state hospital for mentally ill convicted criminals.


HEADNOTES

Insane and other incompetent persons -- transfer to Matteawan State Hospital -- section 85 of Mental Hygiene Law which requires that "dangerously mentally ill" patient in civil State hospital who is not criminal be transferred to Matteawan State Hospital is unconstitutional.

Section 85 of the Mental Hygiene Law (now § 29.13 of the new Mental Hygiene Law) insofar as it mandates that "dangerously mentally ill" patients in civil State hospitals, whose confinement is not based on a criminal charge or conviction, be transferred to Matteawan State Hospital, a correctional facility for mentally ill convicted criminals, is unconstitutional. To subject a person to a greater deprivation of personal liberty than necessary to achieve the purpose for which he is being confined is violative of due process. While appellant is dangerously mentally ill, his confinement, necessary for the protection of others, to be constitutional, must be therapeutic, not punitive. There is no way to construe section 85 to preserve its constitutionality since it provides no substitute for Matteawan.

84 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bornholdt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 75; 305 N.E.2d 461; 350 N.Y.S.2d 369; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 985


September 17, 1973, Argued  
October 23, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), affirming their convictions for felony murder and one defendant's conviction for intentional murder.


OVERVIEW: A police officer began questioning defendants after he observed them run a light. During the questioning, a fight broke out and one defendant shot the officer. Both defendants were convicted of felony murder, while defendant shooter was also convicted of intentional murder. The court of appeals' orders affirmed the convictions. Defendant who did not shoot the officer contended the affirmative defense provisions of the felony murder statute, N.Y. Penal Law §125.25(3), were unconstitutional because they compelled a defendant to disprove any connection with the murder. The court affirmed the orders because the affirmative defense provisions were constitutional. The defenses required knowledge and proof that only defendant would have been privy to, so that shifting the burden of persuasion to defendant would not create a hardship. Requiring the prosecution to disprove the defenses beyond a reasonable doubt would place an undue burden on it and reduce the statute's authority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder -- evidence was sufficient to prove felony murder beyond reasonable doubt -- court's instruction that flight after arrest for felony constitutes crime of escape whether flight takes place before or after shooting was not erroneous -- burden of defendant to establish affirmative defense to felony murder by preponderance of evidence is not violation of due process -- court's denial of motion by defendant for severance was not abuse of discretion -- court properly sustained objections to questions designed to show witness omitted facts in prior statements to which he was now testifying -- defendant, although tranquilized, was competent to participate in proceedings -- sufficient evidence was introduced to prove sanity at time of crime.

1. Evidence that the defendants assaulted a police officer, that the police officer drew his service revolver and told the defendants that they were under arrest, that the defendants resisted the restraint and got away, and that in the course of getting away, defendant Bornholt shot and killed the officer for which defendant Victory, participant in the underlying felony, must share, was sufficient to prove all the elements of felony murder beyond a reasonable doubt.

2. The court's supplemental instruction that "flight after a lawful arrest by a policeman for the felony of assault in the second degree does constitute the crime of escape in the second degree whether that flight takes place before or after the shooting" was not erroneous. In stressing that the shooting must have occurred in furtherance of committing the crime of escape, the court's charge sufficiently informed the jury that, if the defendant did not intend to escape at the time the killing occurred, it could not convict of felony murder.

3. In view of the disparities in convenience of proof and opportunity for knowledge and in light of the basic underlying principle of constructive malice and accomplice liability, the defendant Victory's burden of establishing the affirmative defense to felony murder by a preponderance of the evidence is not a violation of constitutional due process.

4. The denial by the trial court of a motion by defendant Victory for a severance on the ground that he intended to call his codefendant, Bornholdt, as a witness to introduce a statement, purportedly made by said codefendant, exculpating defendant Victory was not an abuse of discretion. Defendant Victory never offered proof as to what Bornholdt's testimony would be except for a conclusory statement, did not show how the purported statement would aid his defense to the felony murder charge and did not show that Bornholdt would in fact testify. Finally, the motion was untimely.

5. A witness may not be impeached at trial by showing that he omitted to state facts at a prior time unless at that prior time the witness' attention was called to the matter and he was specifically asked about the facts. Objections to certain questions regarding the assault were properly sustained where it was not shown that at the prior time the witness' attention was specifically called to the manner in which defendant Victory assaulted the police officer.

6. Defendant Bornholdt, although tranquilized with a drug administered at a hospital while awaiting trial, was competent to participate in the jury selection proceedings.

7. Sufficient evidence was introduced from which a jury could conclude that the defendant Bornholdt knew the consequences of his conduct and that such conduct was wrong.

 

85 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Culhane


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 90; 305 N.E.2d 469; 350 N.Y.S.2d 381; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 986; 71 A.L.R.3d 956


September 18, 1973, Argued  
October 23, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments of conviction reversed and a new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged their convictions from the Ulster County Court (New York) for murder on the grounds that the court erred by refusing to excuse four prospective jurors for cause.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were prison inmates. Appellants and a third prisoner were being transported by car to a hearing. During the course of the trip, an altercation broke out whereby a deputy sheriff and the third prisoner were killed. Appellants were convicted of felony murder and sentenced to death. Appellants alleged that the court erred by refusing to excuse four prospective jurors for cause. The court reversed appellants' convictions and ordered a new trial because the four prospective jurors displayed some degree of actual bias establishing prima facie ground for disqualification under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 376(2). Three of the prospective jurors openly admitted that they had heard news reports about the case and had formed opinions regarding defendants' guilt. The court failed to require two of the jurors to make an expurgatory oath, while the oaths made by the other two jurors were invalid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendants' convictions and remanded the case for a new trial. The court erred by refusing to excuse the jurors because they all displayed actual bias, which established grounds for dismissal under state law. The jurors' expurgatory oaths were invalid or not even made.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- challenges to jury -- in selecting jury to try convicts accused of killing Deputy Sheriff during attempted escape, where People's eyewitness was Deputy Sheriff too, reversible error to disallow defendants' challenges for cause to correction officers and to other biased veniremen -- defendants were thus prejudicially compelled to exhaust their peremptory challenges in order to bar these prospective jurors; judgments of conviction are reversed and new trial ordered.

1. In selecting an unbiased and impartial jury to try two convicts who were accused of the felony murder of killing a Deputy Sheriff during an attempted escape, where the People's eyewitness was a Deputy Sheriff too, it was reversible error to disallow defendants' challenges for cause to two correction officers whose voir dire revealed their actual bias against convicts, and to two other veniremen whose voir dire showed that they too could not render an impartial verdict (Code Crim. Pro., §§ 376, 377). None of these four challenged veniremen swore unequivocally to his impartiality as required by section 376 of the Code of Criminal Procedure.

2. Although these veniremen did not sit on the jury since defendants exercised their peremptory challenges against them, defendants' peremptory challenges were thus exhausted before the jury selection was completed; hence the court's erroneous disallowance of these challenges for cause constituted prejudicial and reversible error (cf. CPL 270.20). The judgments of conviction are reversed and a new trial is ordered.

 

86 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Devane v. Touhey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 48; 304 N.E.2d 229; 349 N.Y.S.2d 361; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1015


October 9, 1973, Argued  
October 16, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Judgment reversed, without costs, and judgment granted in favor of appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff candidates appealed the order of the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) that declared the candidates' petitions for nomination by a political party and by an independent group null, void, and in violation of N.Y. Elec. Law § 138-b.


OVERVIEW: Despite being duly nominated by the Republican Party for public offices, each of the candidates was also nominated by independent groups for the same office and challenged the trial court's invalidation of the independent petitions on the grounds that § 138-b impermissibly prohibited independent groups from nominating their candidate of choice if that candidate had already been nominated by a political party, while allowing candidates to be nominated by more than one political party. Respondent county board of elections contended that the statute was designed to prevent the major parties from preempting the ballot to the exclusion of valid independent groups. In reversing the judgment of the trial court, the court gave little credence to the elections board's argument and instead held that no rational justification existed for a statute that allowed one candidate to accept nominations from two different political groups, but denied another candidate the opportunity to accept nominations from a political group and a political party. Therefore, the court declared that N.Y. Elec. Law § 138-violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, without costs, and granted judgment in favor of the candidates.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- limitation on right to receive independent or party nomination -- section 138-b of Election Law providing that no candidate shall be eligible to receive nomination and to appear on ballot as candidate of both party and independent body is unconstitutional -- it is denial of equal protection when appellants, nominees of Republican Party and independent group, are barred from having their names listed in two separate rows while their opponents, nominees of Democratic and Conservative Parties, are listed in two separate rows.

1. Section 138-b of the Election Law which provides that no candidate for an office other than judicial or State-wide shall be eligible to receive the nomination and to appear on the ballot as the candidate of both a party and an independent body is unconstitutional.

2. In this case, appellants, Republican Party candidates for public office, are ineligible to receive and accept the nomination of an independent group, thereby preventing them from having their names listed on the ballot in two separate rows as nominees of the independent group and of the Republican Party while their opponents, nominees of both the Democratic and Conservative Parties, are not barred from having their names appear in two separate rows for the same office. The advantage to certain candidates being listed on two rows while denying that privilege to others represents a denial of equal protection.

88 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hallock v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 599; 300 N.E.2d 430; 347 N.Y.S.2d 60; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1457


April 23, 1973, Argued  
July 3, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs. Question certified answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state, and power authority, appealed an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which modified and affirmed the trial court's order denying the state and power authority's motions to dismiss plaintiff property owners' complaint. The appellate court certified the question, "Did the trial court err as a matter of law in failing to grant the motion to dismiss the complaint?"


OVERVIEW: The state and power authority required sand and gravel as filler for the construction of a dam power project. The state and power authority took a full fee interest in the property owners property by eminent domain rather than an easement only to extract sand and gravel. The trial court denied the state and power authority's motions to dismiss the property owners complaint. The state and power authority appealed and the court certified the following questions: Did the trial court err as a matter of law in failing to grant the motion to dismiss the complaint? The court held that it could not be determined, on the complaint and the affidavits in support of the cross motions, that to take a fee interest in all the acres was permissible to accomplish the conceded public purpose of extracting a limited amount of sand and gravel. The court held that N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1007authorized the acquisition of interests in property, such as easements. The trial court's order was affirmed, and the certified question was answered in the negative.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order denying the state and power authority's motion to dismiss the property owners' complaint. The court answered the certified question in the negative.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- extent of taking -- owners allege that State intends to use only 300,000 cubic yards of 4,300,000 cubic yards of sand and gravel on property for dam project -- in action for declaratory judgment, record is insufficient to make determination -- State, as agent for Power Authority, is proper party -- questions of necessity of appropriating any particular property and extent of such taking are subject to judicial review -- certified question interpreted not in most strict literal meaning.

1. The soil on plaintiffs' 67.7-acre tract of land located near a proposed dam site is of superior quality for use as sand and gravel for high-grade dam filler. The State, acting on behalf of the Power Authority of the State of New York, appropriated the entire 67.7-acre tract in fee. The owners allege that the State intends to use only about 300,000 cubic yards of the 4,300,000 cubic yards of sand and gravel on the property for the dam project. In this action for a declaratory judgment in which the owners challenge the right of the State and the Power Authority by eminent domain to take a full fee interest rather than an easement only to extract sand and gravel, the record is insufficient to make a determination.

2. The State, as agent for the Power Authority, is a proper party to the action.

3. The questions of the necessity of appropriating any particular property and the extent of such taking are subject to judicial review. The principle that there is no right to condemn land in excess of the need for public purposes, and that no more may be taken than is required for the particular public purpose, applies not only as to the volume of the land to be taken, but as well to the nature or extent of the estate in the property taken. In general, there may not be the acquisition of a fee when only an easement is required.

4. The certified question whether Special Term erred as a matter of law in failing to grant the motion to dismiss the complaint in a declaratory judgment action is interpreted, not in its most strict literal meaning, but to inquire whether Special Term erred in its decision that, on the record before it, there was not an adequate basis for making a declaration in favor of the State and the Power Authority. So understood, the question should be answered in the negative.

 

89 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Floyd v. New York State Urban Dev. Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 1; 300 N.E.2d 704; 347 N.Y.S.2d 161; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1071


June 7, 1973, Argued  
July 3, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owner of conforming property challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) which, in a summary proceeding, declared that § 16(3) of the New York State Urban Development Corporation Act was constitutional in exempting projects of respondent New York State Urban Development Corporation (UDC) from zoning and other local laws.


OVERVIEW: The owner owned land which conformed to local zoning laws near a proposed urban development project. He filed a complaint seeking a declaration that the statute under which UDC purported to act was unconstitutional in that it was in conflict with the legislative powers of the municipality under the home rule provision of N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 2. The lower court declared the UDC's enabling provision constitutional, that decision was affirmed, and this appeal ensued. The appellate court found housing was a matter of state-wide concern and, since the statute under attack was general in nature and affected housing, it followed that local laws otherwise in conflict could not inhibit the operation of the general laws. UDC's powers stemmed from the state constitution itself under N.Y. Const. art. XVIII, which established the legislative power to create such an agency in order to alleviate the housing problem throughout the state in the interests of general health, safety and welfare. Hence, there was no constitutional infirmity in either the creation of UDC or its right to override local zoning laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court declaring a section of the New York State Urban Development Corporation Act constitutional in exempting UDC projects from local zoning laws.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- New York State Urban Development Corporation, in planning to develop 12-acre parcel of real property, may plan and execute projects in disregard of local zoning laws.

1. Plaintiff alleges that defendant New York State Urban Development Corporation (UDC) is planning to develop a 12-acre parcel of real property by providing facilities which, in size and in numbers of persons to be accommodated, will violate a village zoning ordinance which prohibits high-rise apartments, multiple dwellings and residences on lots of less than 7,500 square feet in the area affected. Article XVIII of the State Constitution, which creates the necessary authority for the contemplated project, enabled the Legislature to provide for State housing projects and, pursuant to it, UDC was created (New York State Urban Development Corporation Act [L. 1968, ch. 174, as amd.]). Section 16 (subd. [3]) of the New York State Urban Development Corporation Act provides that: "the corporation and any subsidiary thereof shall, in constructing, reconstructing, rehabilitating, altering or improving any project, comply with the requirements of local laws, ordinances, codes, charters or regulations applicable to such construction, reconstruction, rehabilitation, alteration or improvement, provided however, that when, in the discretion of the corporation, such compliance is not feasible or practicable, the corporation and any subsidiary thereof shall comply with the requirements of the state building construction code". It also provides that: "nor shall any municipality have power to require the corporation or any subsidiary thereof, or lessee therefrom or successor in interest thereto, to obtain any other or additional authority, approval, permit, certificate or certificate of occupancy from such municipality as a condition of owning, using, maintaining, operating or occupying any project acquired, constructed, reconstructed, rehabilitated, altered or improved by the corporation or by any subsidiary thereof." When these quoted portions of the statute are taken together, it is clear that the Legislature, in reciting the words "constructed, reconstructed, rehabilitated, etc.", did not have only building code regulations in mind. It would be too narrow an interpretation to say that the statutory provisions did not contemplate zoning. Accordingly, UDC may plan and execute projects in disregard of local zoning laws.

2. Article IX of the State Constitution is expressly made subject to other provisions of the State Constitution, and section 3 of article IX provides that "[except] as expressly provided, nothing in this article shall restrict or impair any power of the legislature in relation to * * * Matters other than the property, affairs or government of a local government." A general law such as section 16 (subd. [3]), applicable to all municipalities, cannot be construed as a law designed to be disruptive of the property or affairs "of a local government."

3. Section 2 (subd. [b], par. [1]) of article IX provides that local laws cannot be overridden except by "enactment of a statute * * * in one calendar year and the re-enactment and approval of such statute in the following calendar year." That provision reasonably applies only where a special act, disruptive of the powers of a particular municipality, is involved.

4. There is no constitutional infirmity in either the creation of UDC or its right to override local zoning laws.

 

90 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Blye v. Globe--Wernicke Realty Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 15; 300 N.E.2d 710; 347 N.Y.S.2d 170; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1073


May 30, 1973, Argued  
July 3, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff guest sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court, dismissing the action against defendant hotel. The guest filed the action seeking a declaratory judgment of the unconstitutionality of N.Y. Lien Law § 181, a permanent injunction, and damages for mental distress.


OVERVIEW: The guest took up residence at the hotel. Approximately two months later, the guest was locked out of her room for nonpayment of one week's hotel charges. Pursuant to the innkeeper's lien law, N.Y. Lien Law, § 181, the hotel summarily seized the guest's personal property without notice and without an opportunity for a hearing. The guest was left with only the clothes she was wearing, her purse with some personal identification, and small change. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the lower court, which had affirmed the judgment of the trial court; the trial court had dismissed the action against the hotel. The court held that the innkeeper's lien law irreconcilable with evolving concepts of due process and was unconstitutional. The court overruled Waters & Co. v. Gerard, 189 N.Y. 302 (1907) to the extent that it held the contrary. "State action" could have been found in an innkeeper's execution on his own lien. Procedural due process required notice and an opportunity for a hearing before the hotel was allowed to deprive the guest of a possessory interest in her property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, which affirmed a judgment of the trial court, dismissing the action against the hotel. The court held that the innkeeper's lien law was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Liens -- innkeeper's lien -- innkeeper's lien law (Lien Law, § 181), pursuant to which hotel summarily seized personal property of resident for nonpayment of one week's hotel charges, without notice and without opportunity for hearing, is unconstitutional -- Waters & Co. v. Gerard (189 N. Y. 302) overruled.

1. A resident at a hotel was locked out of her room for nonpayment of one week's hotel charges and, pursuant to the innkeeper's lien law (Lien Law, § 181), the hotel summarily seized her personal property without notice and without an opportunity for a hearing. Section 181 of the Lien Law which is the authority for such action is irreconcilable with evolving concepts of due process and is unconstitutional. Insofar as Waters & Co. v. Gerard (189 N. Y. 302) holds to the contrary, it is overruled.

2. Although the return of plaintiff's property to her several months after filing of the complaint renders the claim for injunctive relief academic, the claim for money damages remains. The due process and search and seizure issues remain and the controversy is justiciable.

3. Moreover, judicial reluctance to decide questions which need not be reached should yield when, as here, important constitutional issues are raised and the controversy is of a kind likely to recur.

4. While plaintiff's property was not seized by a State official, but by private persons acting pursuant to State law, in some circumstances, the actions of a private citizen can become the actions of the State for purposes of the due process clause. The execution of a lien traditionally has been the function of the Sheriff. On this view, "State action" can be found in an innkeeper's execution on his own lien.

5. Procedural due process requires notice and an opportunity for a hearing before the State may deprive a person of a possessory interest in his property.

6. The guest's interest in possession and use of his property outweighs the innkeeper's interest in summarily seizing that property to secure the payment of charges.

94 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Calloway v. Skinner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


33 N.Y.2d 23; 300 N.E.2d 716; 347 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1074


June 8, 1973, Argued  
July 3, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People ex rel. Calloway, People ex rel. Robinson and Matter of Richardson: Order affirmed, without costs.

In Matter of Stephens: Order reversed, without costs, and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants and respondent, parolees, sought habeas corpus against respondents and appellant, officials. Two trial courts dismissed the writs; the third ordered a hearing; the fourth rescinded revocation of parole. The Appellate Divisions of the Supreme Court in the Fourth and Second Judicial Departments (New York) affirmed the first three judgments, that of the First Judicial Department reversed the fourth. Parolees and an official appealed.


OVERVIEW: The parolees claimed a due process right to counsel at preliminary parole revocation hearings. The officials claimed that no such right existed. The court noted that the federal Constitution conferred only a conditional or discretionary right to counsel at a final parole revocation hearing, and that the federal due process right to counsel at a preliminary revocation hearing was thus conditional or discretionary. The court determined that the due process right to counsel at a preliminary revocation hearing under the state constitution should be coextensive with that under the federal Constitution and no more, and that whether due process required counsel at such a preliminary stage should depend on the peculiarities of particular cases, the decision to be made on a case-by-case basis in the discretion of the parole board. The court determined that parolees should be entitled to a Morrissey hearing if, on the date of that decision, charges of delinquency had not yet been considered and the parolee had not yet been declared delinquent by a member of the parole board. The court concluded that a parolee was not entitled to bail or release pending a hearing before the parole board.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders in the first, second, and fourth cases, and reversed the order in the third, directing that the petition in that case should be dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Parole -- revocation -- right to counsel -- parolee's due process right to counsel at preliminary revocation hearing under State Constitution coextensive with that under Federal Constitution -- decision as to need for counsel should be made in exercise of discretion in accord with guidelines set forth in Gagnon v. Scarpelli (411 U.S. 778) -- right to preliminary and final parole revocation hearings is prospective -- if on date of decision in Morrissey v. Brewer (408 U.S. 471) parolee had not yet been declared delinquent, he is entitled to preliminary hearing -- parolee is not entitled either to bail or release pending hearing -- conducting hearings at Ossining not violation of Morrissey decision.

1. The Supreme Court, with respect to indigent probationers and parolees, has indicated that the Federal Constitution confers only a conditional or discretionary right to counsel at a preliminary or a final parole revocation hearing (cf. Gagnon v. Scarpelli, 411 U.S. 778).

2. A parolee's due process right to counsel at a preliminary revocation hearing under the State Constitution should be coextensive with that under the Federal Constitution and no more. Whether due process requires the assistance of counsel at this preliminary stage should depend on the peculiarities of particular cases. The decision as to the need for counsel should be made on a case-by-case basis in the exercise of a sound discretion by the Parole Board in accordance with the guidelines set forth in the Gagnon case.

3. The Supreme Court, in Morrissey v. Brewer, stated that its decision requiring preliminary and final parole revocation hearings was applicable only to "future revocations of parole." (408 U.S. 471, at p. 490.) Parole revocation is a process initiated by the issuance of a warrant for the retaking and temporary detention of the parole violator followed by his actual arrest. In determining whether a parolee is entitled to a preliminary hearing, the critical stage in this process is the declaration of delinquency by the Parole Board member. (7 NYCRR 1.17.) If, on the date of the Morrissey decision, the charges of delinquency had not yet been considered and the parolee had not yet been declared delinquent by a member of the Parole Board, then he should be entitled to a Morrissey preliminary hearing for the revocation process had not yet been engaged. That the delinquency may be declared retroactive and that the actual arrest may predate Morrissey is irrelevant for the purpose of determining whether a Morrissey preliminary hearing should be available to the parolee.

4. The right to bail is purely statutory and, in the absence of specific legislative direction, parolees are not entitled either to bail or to release pending a hearing before the Parole Board.

5. Included within a parolee's due process right to a preliminary hearing is the right to have that hearing conducted "at or reasonably near the place of the alleged parole violation or arrest." (408 U.S., at p. 485.) While hearings within the City of New York would be preferable for parole violators arrested in the City of New York for violations occurring there, the reasonable expedient procedure adopted by the board of conducting the hearings at Ossining should not be condemned as violative of the Morrissey decision.

 

95 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Berck


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 567; 300 N.E.2d 411; 347 N.Y.S.2d 33; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1143


May 29, 1973, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and the complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court for the Ninth and Tenth Judicial Districts of the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for loitering in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.35(6).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for loitering in violation of § 240.35(6), and the appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed and dismissed the complaint. The court held that § 240.35(6) was unconstitutional on its face and utterly failed to give adequate notice of the forbid behavior. Section 240.35(6) contained two substantive elements: (1) loitering in or about a place without apparent reason, (2) under circumstances which justified suspicion that a person may have been engaged or about to engage in crime. Both elements failed to give sufficient notice of the conduct proscribed and to advise defendant in sufficiently clear and unambiguous terms of the distinction between harmful and innocent conduct. Also, § 240.35(6) placed virtually unfettered discretion in the hands of the police, which encouraged arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement, because it contained absolutely no guidelines governing the determination as to whether a person was engaged in suspicious loitering. Further, § 240.35(6) plainly undercut the constitutional requirement that arrests were lawful only upon a showing of "probable cause."


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for loitering and dismissed the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 6), which provides that person is guilty of loitering when he engages in certain defined conduct, is in violation of due process of law -- it is not so clear and positive as to give unequivocal warning of rule to be obeyed -- it encourages arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement by police and undercuts constitutional requirement that arrests may be made only upon showing of probable cause.

1. Section 240.35 (subd. 6) of the Penal Law, which provides that a person is guilty of loitering when he: "Loiters, remains or wanders in or about a place without apparent reason and under circumstances which justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime, and, upon inquiry by a peace officer, refuses to identify himself or fails to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct and purposes", is in violation of due process of law. It is not so clear and positive as to give an unequivocal warning to the citizen of the rule which is to be obeyed.

2. Not only does it fail to give adequate notice of the conduct to be avoided and punished but it also places virtually unfettered discretion in the hands of the police and thereby encourages arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement.

3. Moreover, by authorizing an arrest for loitering "under circumstances which justify suspicion that [a person] may be engaged or about to engage in crime," subdivision 6 of section 240.35 undercuts the constitutional requirement that arrests are lawful only upon a showing of "probable cause."

 

96 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fitzpatrick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 499; 300 N.E.2d 139; 346 N.Y.S.2d 793; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1178


April 23, 1973, Argued  
June 7, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified and case remitted to Oneida County Court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Oneida County Court (New York), which rendered judgments upon jury verdicts convicting him of first degree murder and imposing the death penalty. Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in admitting the murder weapon and other physical evidence found in his closet and that the death sentence violated U.S. Const. amend. VIII's prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after robbing a gas station, defendant allegedly shot and killed two police officers. Other police officers eventually traced defendant to a house, where they found him hiding in a closet. After surrendering himself, defendant informed the police that the gun used in the crime was in the closet. Upon searching the closet, the police found the gun and other evidence. At the trial, defendant was convicted upon jury verdicts of first degree murder and sentenced to death. On appeal, defendant argued that the trial court erred in admitting the gun and other evidence found in the closet and that the death penalty violated U.S. Const. amend. VIII. The court reversed the death sentence and remanded for resentencing. Specifically, the court held that the "inevitable discovery" rule was both valid and applicable in the present case as an exception to the exclusionary rule with respect to the admission of the gun and other evidence. But the court ruled that because N.Y. Penal Law § 125.35(5) left the imposition of the death penalty solely to the jury's discretion, the statute violated the constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and held that the trial court did not err, due to inevitable discovery, in refusing to exclude the murder weapon and other evidence seized by police from the closet. But because state law left the imposition of the death penalty to the sole discretion of the jury, the court reversed and remanded defendant's death sentence, holding that it constituted cruel and unusual punishment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- murder -- sentence -- search and seizure -- defendant who had shot two policemen and had been traced to his home and found hiding in closet was not entitled to suppression of gun found in closet -- police would have inevitably found gun during normal course of lawful search -- search not unlawful under rule in Chimel v. California (395 U.S. 752) -- no arrest or search warrant was necessary -- New York statute (Penal Law, § 125.30, subd. 5) which leaves infliction of death penalty to jury constitutes cruel and unusual punishment as interpreted by United States Supreme Court ( Furman v. Georgia, 408 U.S. 238).

1. Defendant, who had previously shot two policemen and had been traced to his home in Syracuse and was found hiding in a closet, was not entitled to suppression of a gun found in the closet. The gun may not be deemed inadmissible as "fruit of the poisonous tree". Evidence obtained as a result of information derived from an unlawful search or other illegal police conduct is not inadmissible where the normal course of police investigation would have inevitably led to such evidence. In this case, the police would have inevitably found the gun during a lawful search.

2. The determination in Chimel v. California (395 U.S. 752) that the lawful arrest of an accused, where accomplished without an arrest warrant, or a warrant to search the area where he is found, permits a search only of his person and the area from which he "might obtain weapons or evidentiary items", does not mean that the gun should be excluded although defendant had been removed from the closet. That case did not disapprove of the search of the room but only to a search of an area remote in time and place from the arrest. Neither did the fact that the police had handcuffed the defendant render the search unauthorized.

3. No arrest or search warrant was necessary. There was probable cause to arrest the defendant, and the entry and search of a house by police officers to find a suspect, whom the police had probable cause to arrest and to believe had recently arrived there, and to look for weapons or fruits of the crime does not render either the entry without warrant to search or the search without warrant invalid. Circumstances here demanded quick action. No constitutional rights of defendant were violated by the warrantless entry, search and arrest.

4. Since the New York statute (Penal Law, § 125.30, subd. 5) leaves infliction of the death penalty to the untrammeled discretion of the jury, that penalty constitutes cruel and unusual punishment within the sense of the Eighth Amendment as that amendment has been interpreted by the United States Supreme Court in Furman v. Georgia (408 U.S. 238).

5. The judgment appealed from is modified by vacating the sentence of death and remitting the case for resentencing.

98 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ruskin v. Detken


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 293; 298 N.E.2d 101; 344 N.Y.S.2d 933; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1269


March 16, 1973, Argued  
May 4, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant policemen sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a trial court decision and granted respondent Commissioner of Investigation's petition for commitment of appellants to jail for contempt.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Commissioner of Investigation subpoenaed appellant policemen to testify regarding entries that they had made in their records. They were advised of their privilege against self-incrimination and told that if they refused to answer, they would be subject to dismissal. Appellants testified that they could not remember the circumstances surrounding the entries, and respondent moved the court to commit appellants to jail for contempt. The trial court denied the motion and quashed the subpoena against appellants. Respondent sought review and the intermediate court reversed and granted the petition for commitment. Appellants sought review arguing that respondent could not compel them to surrender their privilege against self-incrimination. The appellate court affirmed the commitment order. It found that because appellants gave evasive answers and failed to claim privilege, their responses were the equivalent of refusals to answer without reasonable cause, and that those refusals were the basis for contempt.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed an order committing appellant policemen to jail for contempt because their responses to questions posed by respondent Commissioner of Investigation constituted refusals to answer.


HEADNOTES

Contempt -- immunity -- New York City policemen, subpoenaed to testify before Commissioner of Investigation of City of New York, did not make bona fide efforts to answer questions and refused to answer without reasonable cause -- evasive responses may be used in subsequent contempt proceeding -- rationale of Garrity v. New Jersey (385 U.S. 493) not applicable.

1. Appellants, New York City policemen, were subpoenaed to testify before the Commissioner of Investigation of the City of New York and were questioned about entries made on five sheets of police memo book paper. Each of them identified certain entries on the sheets as being in his handwriting, but, when asked under what circumstances or for what purposes the entries had been made, each testified that he could not remember. The entries on the police memo sheets were made pursuant to a common scheme which contemplated passing the sheets among patrolmen of the precinct sector for their additions and corrections. It is incredible as a matter of law, as well as obstructive to the purposes of the investigation, that the policemen could not recall the nature of this common scheme. Accordingly, they did not make bona fide efforts to answer the questions put to them and refused to answer legal and pertinent questions without reasonable cause.

2. Each policeman was advised of his right to counsel and his privilege against self incrimination. They were also advised that, if they refused to answer any questions relating to the performance of their official duties, they would be subject to dismissal from the police force, and that if they did testify, neither their answers, nor any information or evidence gained by reason of those answers, could be used against them in a criminal proceeding. Their evasive responses can be used in a subsequent contempt proceeding pursuant to CPLR 2308 (subd. [b]). The rationale of Garrity v. New Jersey (385 U.S. 493) does not apply to the situation here. The exclusionary rules enunciated in People v. Steuding (6 N Y 2d 214) and in the Garrity case, designed to alleviate the adverse effects of unconstitutional coercion and applied to the testimony of public employees who are under threat of job forfeiture for refusal to testify, do not bar the use of such compelled testimony in a contempt proceeding pursuant to CPLR 2308 (subd. [b]).

Court of Appeals of New York

100 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Botens v. Aronauer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 243; 298 N.E.2d 73; 344 N.Y.S.2d 892; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1294


February 8, 1973, Argued  
May 3, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court and directed judgment in favor of defendant purchaser in the taxpayers' action to compel the determination of a claim to real property sold for delinquent taxes.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers were delinquent in the payment of their real property taxes, so the county, pursuant to the mandate of N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 1002(1), advertised the property for sale to satisfy the delinquent taxes in two area newspapers for six successive weeks, as required by N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 1014(1). The purchaser then bought the property and received a county tax deed. The taxpayers filed an action to have the deed declared invalid. The trial court declared title to be in the taxpayers, and the appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court, holding that title to the property belonged to the purchaser. Although the taxpayers did not actually receive notice of the tax sale or of redemption following the sale, notice was published in two newspapers for the required period of time as specified in §§ 1002(1), 1014(1). Thus, because indirect notice was sufficient to persons interested in property in default in the payment of taxes, the Due Process Clause of the New York Constitution and the United States Constitution were not offended by summary statutory remedies for the collection of ordinary taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order declaring that title to the real property belonged to the purchaser in the taxpayers' action for a determination of a claim to the property, which was sold for delinquent taxes.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax liens, tax sales and tax titles -- notice of tax sale -- provisions of Real Property Tax Law ( §§ 1002, 1014) for notice of tax sale and redemption satisfy requirements of due process and are constitutional -- court suggests that Legislature re-examine statutes since circumstances have changed since original provisions were adopted.

1. Section 1002 of the Real Property Tax Law provides that the county treasurer shall cause a notice of tax sale to be published at least once each week for six weeks in two designated newspapers, specifying a day at the expiration of the six weeks on which the sale will commence. Section 1014 provides that the county treasurer shall, at least three months before the expiration of the one year allowed for the redemption of lands sold for taxes, cause commencement of publication of a notice, once a week for six successive weeks in the designated newspapers, containing a list of the lands sold for taxes and unredeemed, specifying every parcel unredeemed, the amount necessary to redeem and stating that, unless such lands are redeemed on or before such day, they will be conveyed to the purchaser. The procedure followed by the county as required by these statutes gave the property owners sufficient notice that their property would be sold for nonpayment of taxes and notice of redemption. Indirect notice is sufficient to persons interested in property which is in default in payment of taxes. No issue is presented as to the property owners' knowledge that taxes were to be paid or of consequences of nonpayment. The challenged sections ( Real Property Tax Law, §§ 1002, 1014) satisfy the requirements of due process and are not constitutionally infirm.

2. The court suggests that the Legislature re-examine these statutes, since the circumstances of land ownership were much different when the Legislature enacted the provision regarding the selection of two newspapers for the purpose of advertising tax sales and notices of redemption.

Sanford v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 788; 298 N.E.2d 681; 345 N.Y.S.2d 543; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1301; 84 L.R.R.M. 2272

102 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stokes


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 202; 298 N.E.2d 49; 344 N.Y.S.2d 859; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1324


March 15, 1973, Argued  
April 26, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court for the Second and Eleventh Judicial Districts of the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed his conviction for loitering and affirmed his conviction for attempted possession of a hypodermic instrument.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, when questioned by the housing authority police, refused to answer questions. After the officer placed defendant under arrest, he conducted a search and recovered drugs and a syringe. Defendant was charged with loitering, possession of a dangerous drug, and criminal possession of a hypodermic needle. He moved to suppress the evidence and the motion was denied. He was found guilty of loitering and then pleaded guilty to attempted possession of a hypodermic needle. He sought review. The intermediate court reversed the loitering conviction because the complaint was defective but upheld the search and drug conviction. Defendant again sought review arguing that the seized evidence should have been suppressed because there was no probable cause for the underlying loitering arrest, and that the subsequent search was illegal. The appellate court reversed defendant's conviction for attempted possession of a hypodermic instrument. It found that that the officer lacked probable cause to believe that defendant was acting in an unlawful manner and therefore, the search was unlawful and the evidence should have been suppressed.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed an order convicting defendant of attempted possession of a hypodermic instrument, and it dismissed the charge. The court held that there was no probable cause to arrest defendant for loitering and since the loitering arrest was the predicate for the search, the search was unlawful and the evidence should have been suppressed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- Penal Law ( § 240.35, subd. 6) provides in part that person is guilty of loitering when he remains in a place "under circumstances which justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime" -- no evidence that circumstances would justify suspicion that defendant was engaged or about to engage in crime -- he was placed under arrest because he refused to identify himself or explain presence in building -- no probable cause for arrest for loitering -- complaint should be dismissed.

1. At about 11:30 at night, defendant and a friend entered the unlocked lobby of an apartment building. They briefly talked to a third person in the building and then spoke to one another for about eight to ten minutes. At that point, two policemen entered the lobby, and one of them asked defendant if he lived in the building. He replied that he did not. When the officer asked him to identify himself and to explain his reasons for being there, defendant refused and was placed under arrest for loitering. Section 240.35 (subd. 6) of the Penal Law provides that: "A person is guilty of loitering when he * * * Loiters, remains or wanders in or about a place without apparent reason and under circumstances which justify suspicion that he may be engaged or about to engage in crime, and, upon inquiry by a peace officer, refuses to identify himself or fails to give a reasonably credible account of his conduct and purposes". There was no evidence that the circumstances would justify a suspicion that defendant was "engaged or about to engage in crime". The record demonstrates that the defendant was placed under arrest because he refused to identify himself or explain his presence in the building. Such a refusal may not, in and of itself, justify an arrest or a conviction under the statute. There was no probable cause for the defendant's arrest for loitering. The incidental search was unlawful and the evidence taken from the defendant should have been suppressed. The complaint should be dismissed.

2. In the view taken, the court finds it unnecessary to consider the constitutionality of the loitering statute.

 

103 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Guggenheim v. Mucci


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 307; 298 N.E.2d 109; 344 N.Y.S.2d 944; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1326


March 22, 1973, Argued  
April 26, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A law guardian, acting on behalf of petitioner juvenile, sought review of a decision by the Supreme Court at Special Term, Bronx County (New York), which dismissed petitioner's writ of habeas corpus application as moot, and which found that petitioner's incarceration did not require a preliminary inquiry of probable cause.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner juvenile was detained for two months during his case investigation. The court did not hold a probable cause proceeding during the period of incarceration. Petitioner filed a writ of habeas corpus alleging that his constitutional right to due process was denied. The trial court dismissed the petition as moot since petitioner was no longer detained at the time of the hearing, and petitioner sought direct review. The appellate court affirmed the denial of the writ on mootness grounds but modified the judgment to reflect that the denial was on mootness grounds alone. The court implied that if mootness had not been an issue, the denial of the writ would have been overturned because petitioner was entitled to a speedier fact-finding hearing.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed an order dismissing petitioner's writ of habeas corpus application as moot, but modified the dismissal to reflect that the writ was dismissed on the grounds of mootness alone. The court implied that if mootness had not been an issue, it would have overturned the denial because petitioner detention unconstitutionally deprived him of his right to speedy process.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- juvenile delinquency -- unless evidence has established probable cause to detain alleged juvenile delinquent, he cannot constitutionally be detained for much beyond statutory three-day limit, even if homicide is alleged (Family Ct. Act, §§ 728, 729, 744, 747, 748) -- however, meantime he has been released on parole and Family Court in City of New York has adopted regulation directing proper procedure; under these circumstances, judgment should be modified to dismiss writ solely on ground of mootness -- but appeal itself will not be dismissed as moot when question of public importance is likely to recur.

1. Unless evidence has established probable cause to detain an alleged juvenile delinquent, he cannot constitutionally be detained for more than several days beyond the statutory three-day limit (Family Ct. Act, §§ 729, 747). The initial detention hearing (Family Ct. Act, § 728) may be an appropriate time to conduct an inquiry into whether there is probable cause to hold the infant; or, after a fact-finding hearing has been started and has established probable cause to detain the infant, the balance of the fact-finding hearing may be adjourned "for good cause shown" for not more than three days or, in case of homicide or an assault which incapacitates the victim, "for a reasonable length of time" (Family Ct. Act, § 748). On the issue of adjournment, the hearing need not be limited to "competent" evidence (cf. Family Ct. Act, § 744), but may include reliable hearsay even though the question of adjournment involves probable cause to detain.

2. In this alleged homicide case, the infant has been unlawfully detained during some two months of adjournments of the fact-finding hearing, without any evidence establishing probable cause to hold him. His writ of habeas corpus would be sustained, were it not for two facts: -- he has meantime been released on parole, and the Family Court in the City of New York has meantime adopted a regulation directing substantially the above-stated procedure. Since the relief requested would serve no purpose, the judgment appealed from should be modified to the extent of dismissing the writ of habeas corpus solely on the ground of mootness.

3. However, the appeal itself should not be dismissed as moot when a question of general interest and substantial public importance is likely to recur.

People v. Felder


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York
