106 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


American Locker Co. v. Gallman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 175; 297 N.E.2d 96; 344 N.Y.S.2d 358; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1322


March 14, 1973, Argued  
April 26, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which confirmed a determination of respondent State Tax Commission sustaining the imposition upon the taxpayer of sales taxes under article 28 of the New York Tax Law, and dismissed the petition.


OVERVIEW: The question was whether the taxpayer was engaged in the "storage" business as that term had been applied by the courts, and, if so, the extent of its tax liability vis-a-vis its operators. The taxpayer contended that it was not engaged in storing tangible personal property within the meaning of N.Y. Tax Law § 1105(c)(4). It urged that the term "storage" or "storing" connoted custody involving such duration and dominion as to render impossible its application to the public's use of coin-operated checking lockers. The court concluded that the statutory language fairly encompassed the type of service involved. The essence of the service was the provision of a place for the safekeeping of goods, which generally accorded with the ordinary and common meaning of storage. The taxpayer was the taxable vendor of these services and was liable for the collection of the sales tax. The nature of the contractual relationship between the taxpayer and its operators indicated that the former was the vendor within the meaning of the statute. For all intents and purposes, when a patron made use of a locker, he was dealing with the taxpayer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate division, which confirmed a determination of the commission sustaining the imposition of a sales tax upon the taxpayer and dismissing the taxpayer's petition.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- storage -- sale of storage service by petitioner, owner of patented coin-operated lockers which it places under contract with parties engaged in providing automatic checking facilities to public, is taxable under Tax Law (former § 1105) -- nature of contractual relationship between petitioner and its operators indicates that petitioner is vendor within meaning of statute (Penal Law, § 1101).

1. Petitioner owns patented coin-operated lockers which it places under contract with various parties engaged in providing automatic checking facilities to the public. Former section 1105 of the Tax Law provided for the payment of a tax on the receipts from every sale, except for resale, of the service of "Storing all tangible personal property not held for sale in the regular course of business and the rental of safe deposit boxes or similar space." The statutory language fairly encompasses the type of service involved in this case. The essence of this service is the provision of a place for the safekeeping of goods, which generally accords with the ordinary and common meaning of storage.

2. Under article 28 of the Tax Law, a vendor is required to be registered (§ 1134), to file quarterly returns (§ 1136) and to remit the tax due (§ 1137). The nature of the contractual relationship between petitioner and its operators indicates that the former is a vendor within the meaning of the statute (Tax Law, § 1101).

 

107 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Roher v. Dinkins


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 180; 298 N.E.2d 37; 344 N.Y.S.2d 841; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1360


January 9, 1973, Argued  
April 25, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Board of Elections (board) challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which declared N.Y. Educ. Law § 2590-c(6)(34)(b) unconstitutional to the extend that it permitted appointment to the community district school board for a term longer than authorized by N.Y. Const. art. XIII, § 3. Petitioner would-be candidates challenged the failure to direct the holding of a special election.


OVERVIEW: The would-be candidates sought to fill, by election, the unexpired terms of five school board members. The board refused to accept their nominating petitions. The trial court held that Article 52-A of the Education Law was unconstitutional but it failed to direct the holding of an election. The appellate court modified the judgment and held that N.Y. Educ. Law § 2590-c(6)(34)(b), which provided that vacancies on the school board could be filled for the unexpired term, was unconstitutional but it also failed to order an election. The court affirmed. The court noted that school board members were elective officers subject to N.Y. Const. art. XIII, § 3, which provided that an appointment to fill a vacancy only last until the commencement of the political year. The fact that the school board members' terms did not coincide with the calendar year was not dispositive. The court concluded that an election should have been ordered by the trial court but declined to order such an election when the school board's term would imminently expire.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order declaring that part of a statute permitting appointment to the community district school board for a term longer than authorized by the state constitution unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- community school boards -- New York City -- elections -- New York State Constitution (art. XIII, § 3) which provides that "in case of elective officers, no person appointed to fill a vacancy shall hold his office by virtue of such appointment longer than the commencement of the political year next succeeding the first annual election after the happening of the vacancy" and (§ 4) which defines political year as beginning on first day of January, in conflict with Education Law ( § 2590-c, subd. 6, par. [34], cl. b) authorizing appointments for "unexpired term", is applicable to school board elections -- although issue is academic, Special Term should have directed election to be held to fill vacancy in general election of November, 1972 -- court will not direct holding of special election in April or May, 1973 for term to expire in June, 1973 -- matters needing legislative attention discussed.

1. Article 52-A of the Education Law established the New York City Community School District System and provided for the election of community boards of education. The first members of the board were elected in March, 1970 for three-year terms commencing July, 1970 and ending June 30, 1973 (Education Law, § 2590-b, subd. 2, par. [d]). The statute provided that all subsequent members shall serve for two-year terms with elections to be held in May of odd-numbered years and terms to commence on July 1 (Education Law, § 2590-c, subd. 2). In one district, a community board of nine persons was elected in 1970. By July of 1972 five of them had resigned, their places being filled by five members serving by appointment of the board. The Education Law ( § 2590-c, subd. 6, par. [34], cl. b) provides that "Vacancies shall be filled for the unexpired term by the community board." Article XIII (§ 3) of the New York State Constitution provides that "The legislature shall provide for filling vacancies in office, and in case of elective officers, no person appointed to fill a vacancy shall hold his office by virtue of such appointment longer than the commencement of the political year next succeeding the first annual election after the happening of the vacancy." Article XIII (§ 4) of the Constitution defines a political year as beginning on the first day of January. There is a conflict between the Constitution and an appointment for an "unexpired term" as authorized by Education Law ( § 2590-c, subd. 6, par. [34], cl. b). School board members are "elective officers" and school board elections are not excluded from the application of article XIII (§ 3) of the Constitution.

2. Although the issue is now academic, in October, 1972 Special Term should have directed that an election be held to fill the vacancy in the general election of November, 1972 and if the Board of Elections could not be ready by November, a special election should have been ordered for December. Under the State Constitution, when a vacancy in an elective office occurs, the vacancy should be filled by an election in the shortest reasonable time possible. However, the court will not direct a special election to be held in April or May, 1973 for a term to expire at the end of June, 1973.

3. The fact that a vacancy filled at an interim election might not allow for proportional representation in accord with the formula set out in section 2590-c (subd. 6) of the Education Law does not mean that the election should not be held. It is more important to conduct an election and replace an appointed official with an elected official than to adhere to the fine details of proportional representation.

4. While it appears that district lines for school board elections may not coincide with election district lines for other elections, the practical difficulties presented can be overcome. The city board of education can redraw the boundary lines of community board districts wherever possible to coincide with the lines for other districts. The problems presented by the fact that school board election petitions were not due until October, 1972 and that there are eligible voters in the school board election other than the registered voters of the district are matters which deserve legislative attention.

 

108 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Antonopoulou v. Beame


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 126; 296 N.E.2d 247; 343 N.Y.S.2d 346; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1385; 83 L.R.R.M. 3092; 5 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P8635


February 14, 1973, Argued  
March 23, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a teacher and teachers union, challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order denying an application by petitioners and dismissed their petition for an order annulling a decision of respondent comptroller of the city of New York and directing him to comply with a direction to pay petitioner teacher's salary.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner teacher was denied an early termination of her maternity leave. Through her collective bargaining representative, petitioner teachers union, she filed a grievance in accordance with her collective bargaining agreement. In the second step of the grievance procedure against respondent Board of Higher Education of the City of New York, petitioner was retroactively reinstated and awarded her salary. But respondent comptroller of the city of New York refused to comply with the decision insofar as it awarded back pay and argued that a payment of salary retroactively would constitute a gift of public funds. Petitioners commenced a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding. The lower court agreed with the respondent, stating that the award of retroactive salary would be a public gift and violate N.Y. Const. art. VIII, §1. The court reversed, reasoning that the retroactive award of salary was no more a gift than any other award of damages for unlawful deprivation of an opportunity afforded by contract. The court further stated that an award of a collective bargaining grievance procedure was a legally enforceable contractual right and was as binding as any other arbitration award.


OUTCOME: The court reversed with costs the order denying an application by petitioners, a teacher and teachers union, and dismissing their petition for an order annulling a decision of respondent comptroller of the city of New York because the award of retroactive salary was not a gift of public funds, but an enforceable contractual right. The court returned the case to the trial court for further proceedings in accordance with its order.


HEADNOTES

Colleges and universities -- payment of award under collective bargaining agreement -- under collective bargaining agreement of teachers' union with Board of Higher Education, petitioner teacher won decision on May 12, 1970 that restored her to job as of February 1, 1970 with back pay; payment thereof is obligation under contract which municipality must make and is not, because teacher performed no services, gift of public moneys proscribed by Constitution.

A teacher, member of a union which had a collective bargaining agreement with the Board of Higher Education, was placed on maternity leave and, following birth of her child in November, 1969, sought reinstatement in time for the commencement of the spring semester in February, 1970. In the second stage of the grievance procedure under the contract, a decision dated May 12, 1970 retroactively reinstated petitioner teacher as of February 1, 1970 and awarded her back pay. It was error to deny payment thereof on the ground that, since petitioner performed no services, payment would constitute a gift of public funds in violation of the Constitution. The award resulting from the grievance procedure was a contractual right and was not a "gift" of public funds. The award is as binding as an arbitration award and imposes a legal obligation on the part of the municipality to comply with the decision.

 

109 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pahmer v. Hertz Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 119; 296 N.E.2d 243; 343 N.Y.S.2d 341; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1383


December 29, 1972, Argued  
March 23, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: February 14, 1973, Reargued.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, car driver and car rental company, appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified a trial court's order and affirmed as modified. The appellate court held that the denial of plaintiff passenger's motion to dismiss three defenses should apply only to the workmen's compensation defenses and granted the motion as to the California guest statute defenses.


OVERVIEW: Defendants raised the affirmative defenses of the California Statute of Limitations, the California guest statute, and the New York Workmen's Compensation Law. The trial court granted the passenger's motion to dismiss the Statute of Limitations defense and denied her motion to dismiss the other defenses. The appellate court granted the motion to dismiss the California guest statute and affirmed as modified. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court's judgment. Although the appeal was briefed and argued on choice-of-law principles, the high court of California recently declared that the California guest statute violated the equal protection guarantees of the California and United States Constitutions. The classifications that the guest statute created between those denied and those permitted recovery for negligently inflicted injuries did not bear a substantial and rational relation to the statute's purposes of protecting the hospitality of the host-driver and of preventing collusive actions. Because the California guest statute was unconstitutional, it could not be relied upon in the instant action and neither defendant could assert it as a defense to the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment, which modified the trial court's order by holding that the passenger's motion to dismiss three defenses should have applied only to the workmen's compensation defenses, and granting the motion as to the California guest statute defenses.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- guest statute -- in action by residents of this State against driver of automobile and corporation from which it had been rented for injuries incurred when automobile left highway in California and became involved in multicar accident, California guest statute, declared unconstitutional by high court of California, may not be relied upon as defense by either defendant.

In an action by residents of this State against the driver of an automobile and the corporation from which it had been rented for injuries incurred when the automobile left a highway in California and became involved in a multicar accident, the California guest statute may not be relied upon as a defense by either defendant since the California guest statute has been stricken by the high court of California as unconstitutional.

 

110 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Shapiro v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 96; 296 N.E.2d 230; 343 N.Y.S.2d 323; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1395


January 10, 1973, Argued  
March 22, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought review of the order from the Kings County Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which dismissed its complaint against appellee city on the merits and adjudged that New York, N.Y. Local Law No. 36 (1971) was in all respects valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: New York, N.Y. Local Law No. 36 (1971) extended the tax imposed under the city's Unincorporated Business Income Tax Law to previously exempted self-employed professionals, and the employees filed suit challenging the constitutionality. The trial court dismissed the complaint and held that the law was in all respects valid and constitutional. On appeal, the court held that the Due Process Clause was applicable to a tax statute only if the act was so arbitrary as to compel the conclusion that it did not involve an exertion of the taxing power, but constituted, in substance and effect, the direct exertion of a different and forbidden power. The court held that the wide variety of classifications permitted under the principles of equal protection went far to dispose of the employees' claim that Local Law No. 36 was arbitrary and capricious as it treated self-employed professionals as unincorporated business for tax purposes. The court held that the fact that a better taxing system might have been conceived did not render the law invalid. The court affirmed the order of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the trial court that dismissed the employees' complaint against the city and adjudged the local law imposing taxes on the employees valid and constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- unincorporated business tax -- Local Law No. 36 of 1971 of City of New York, which extended tax imposed under city's Unincorporated Business Income Tax Law to previously exempted self-employed professionals, is constitutional and valid -- no violation of due process or of equal protection -- not necessary that tax law apply also to salaried employees.

1. Prior to 1971 the New York City Unincorporated Business Income Tax Law defined an unincorporated business as "any trade, business or occupation" (Administrative Code, § S46-2.0, subd. [a]). In 1971, the State authorized the city to repeal the provision excluding or exempting professionals and to redefine an "unincorporated business" to include not only trades, businesses and occupations but professions as well (L. 1971, ch. 412, § 1). In June of that year the city passed Local Law No. 36 of 1971 to extend the tax imposed under its Unincorporated Business Income Tax Law to previously exempted self-employed professionals. The court at Special Term properly upheld the local law as constitutional.

2. This law was enacted solely as an exercise of the taxing power. It was not motivated by any purpose other than the raising of revenue. In such case the due process clause may not be availed of to condemn the statute.

3. There was no denial of equal protection. The wide variety of permitted classifications in taxation disposes of the plaintiff's claim that Local Law No. 36 is arbitrary and capricious insofar as it treats self-employed professionals as unincorporated businesses for tax purposes.

4. Plaintiff's argument that, even if the Legislature may legally treat self-employed businessmen and self-employed professionals similarly for tax purposes, it is not permissible to impose a business tax on such self-employed taxpayers without imposing the same tax on salaried employees is without merit. A state of facts reasonably can be conceived that would sustain the classification, and that is sufficient.

5. The fact that the tax classification under attack in this case has existed unchallenged in the State law since its enactment almost four decades ago is some evidence that such classification rests upon a just and reasonable ground.

People v. Moore


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 67; 295 N.E.2d 780; 343 N.Y.S.2d 107; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1401


January 8, 1973, Argued  
March 21, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Supreme Court for the Second and Eleventh Judicial Districts in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed her conviction based upon a guilty plea for attempted possession of a dangerous weapon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for attempted possession of a dangerous weapon, to which she pled guilty. On appeal, she contended that a warrantless search of her handbag and seizure of a loaded revolver violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. Her motion to suppress the evidence had been denied before her entry of the guilty plea. The court found that the motion was properly denied, and it affirmed the conviction. A forcible encounter between a police officer and defendant occurred in a precinct station house. The officer had a reasonable suspicion that defendant was carrying a concealed weapon. Based on the totality of the circumstances, although the action was predicated on an informant's tip, there was the requisite indicia of reliability to warrant the search and seizure without a warrant. Thus, the court found no constitutional or statutory violation stemming from such action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for attempted possession of a dangerous weapon following a guilty plea, because the warrantless search of her handbag, based on reasonable belief that she had a weapon, was not unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- stop and frisk -- defendant complained to police officer that her paramour menaced her; policeman arrested him and defendant preceded policeman to station house to file complaint; paramour told officer that defendant had gun; at station house policeman asked defendant for her purse which she gave him; he found loaded gun therein; stop and frisk was reasonable and gun was admissible against defendant.

When defendant complained to a police officer that she had been menaced with a knife by a man with whom she had been living, her assailant was arrested and driven in the police vehicle to the station house. Defendant preceded the officer to the station house to file a complaint. The man, during the ride, informed the officer that defendant was "sick" and had a gun. At the police station, the officer asked defendant for her handbag. When she gave it to him he found a loaded revolver therein for which defendant had no license. The search was reasonable under the "stop" and "frisk" law ( CPL 140.50). The search here occurred in the station house precipitated by a reasonable suspicion based on an informant's tip that defendant was carrying a concealed weapon. The "stop" was justified, its reason being a report of a concealed weapon. Concealed weapons present an immediate and real danger to the public which supports a police search on less than a probability. The gun was admissible in evidence against defendant.

 

112 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


D'Addario v. McNab


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 84; 295 N.E.2d 792; 343 N.Y.S.2d 124; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1403


March 12, 1973, Argued  
March 21, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, members of a county board of elections, challenged the order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed, on the law, a trial court's judgment dismissing respondent town electors' complaint. The appellate court had remanded for a judgment declaring that a town proposition was not legally submitted to the voters and was therefore not effective.


OVERVIEW: A proposition submitted to the town electors at their last general election was carried by a slender margin. The proposition would have changed the town board from a body, all of whose members were elected at large, into one of representatives elected by districts. The intermediate court of appeals nullified on the vote on the ground that the proposition was not submitted in the manner required by law, because the proponents of the proposition failed to publish notice by publication and posting on the town signboard, as required by N.Y. Town Law § 82. The members argued that the town clerk's failure to comply with § 82 was not fatal, while the electors contended that the trial court had properly declared the vote null and void. The court held that: (1) statutory requirements of notice by publication and posting for the submission of propositions were mandatory and actual notice or substantial equivalence were acceptable substitutes; (2) the election was void because the electors did not receive notice of the time and place of the election; and (3) the legislature, not the courts, must determine how each notice shall be given. Accordingly, the court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, without costs, the order rendered by the intermediate court of appeals, which reversed the trial court's judgment dismissing the town electors' complaint and remanded for a judgment declaring that the proposition was not legally submitted to the voters and was not effective.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- submission of proposition -- proposition changing nature of town board was submitted to town electors and carried by narrow margin -- Appellate Division properly nullified vote on ground that proposition had not been submitted in manner required by law (Town Law, § 82) -- statutory requirements of notice by publication and posting for submission of propositions are mandatory.

1. A proposition which would have changed the town board from a body, all of whose members are elected at large, into one of representatives elected by districts was submitted to town electors and carried by the margin of 191 votes out of about 72,000 votes cast. The Appellate Division properly nullified the vote on the ground that the proposition was not submitted in the manner required by law. Section 82 of the Town Law requires that, before propositions may be submitted to the electorate, there must have been notice by publication and posting on the town signboard. There was no attempt of any kind at posting or publication.

2. Statutory requirements of notice by publication and posting for the submission of propositions are mandatory, and actual notice or substantial equivalences will not be acceptable as a substitute unless there has been, in fact, some posting and publication to satisfy the statute. All other statutory requirements to make elections effective, convenient, or even just more efficient, are generally treated as directory only, with respect to which substantial equivalence will be acceptable.

3. Publication and notice are not addressed to the general public as a practical matter. These requirements, in a legal or jurisdictional sense, serve the same function as service of process, including alternative forms of process, in the institution of litigation, or the requirement that tax rolls are to be on public view at the tax office, or that statutes must be promulgated to be effective as law. Such preconditions should not be confused with actual publicity.

 

113 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Shelofsky v. Helsby


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 54; 295 N.E.2d 774; 343 N.Y.S.2d 98; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1412; 83 L.R.R.M. 2067


February 15, 1973, Argued  
March 15, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs supervisors sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a trial court declaratory judgment that the 1971 N.Y. Laws chs. 503, 504, were constitutional in so far as they barred the supervisors from joining a civil service association for public employees.


OVERVIEW: The supervisors claimed that N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 201(7) deprived them of the freedom to associate, was unconstitutionally vague, impaired their right to contract for insurance in violation of due process, and deprived them of equal protection. The board claimed that § 201(7) was constitutional and an appropriate exercise of the state's power to regulate public employees. The court affirmed the appellate court's order declaring § 201(7) to be constitutional and held that the state had a legitimate purpose in regulating the conduct of the public employees and that the supervisors fit the definition of supervisors in § 201(7) and was similar to the definition in 29 U.S.C.S. § 152(11), which was sufficiently detailed to withstand the vagueness challenge. The court further held that there was no constitutional right to be a public employee and when the supervisors agreed to become public employees they consequentially agreed to abide by the statutory provisions that regulated their conduct, including prohibiting them from joining an association, with which the state must bargain.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court order, which affirmed the state court declaratory judgment, which held that the civil service requirements that the supervisors could not join a bargaining association was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- exclusion of managerial personnel from collective bargaining -- provision of Civil Service Law adopted in 1971 (L. 1971, ch. 503, §§ 4, 16; ch. 504, § 1) that "managerial" and "confidential" personnel may not be members of or hold office in organization which represents or seeks to represent public employees is valid.

1. The provision of the Civil Service Law, which was adopted in 1971 to provide for designation of certain public employees as "managerial" and "confidential" and to provide that they may not be members of or hold office in any organization which represents or seeks to represent public employees (L. 1971, ch. 503, §§ 4, 16; ch. 504, § 1), is valid. The exclusion of managerial personnel from collective bargaining rights has long been permitted by statute and decisional law affecting private employment. The need to have responsible management personnel to formulate policy and to handle labor relations is equally applicable to the State in its capacity as an employer.

2. The language of section 201 (subd. 7) of the Civil Service Law, defining managerial and confidential employees, is similar to the Federal statute, which has been upheld, and is sufficiently detailed to withstand attack for vagueness.

3. Plaintiffs' contentions that insurance contract rights would be impaired are not persuasive. Those contracts were entered in contemplation that the State had continuing power to legislate on matters affecting public employees (see 9 N. Y. Jur., Constitutional Law, § 270). Participation in the insurance plans was made contingent on continued association membership.

4. The statute will not impair the association's previous collective bargaining agreement with the State.

5. There has been no showing that exclusion of management personnel from association membership is an unreasonable limitation on State employees.

 

114 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kirkpatrick


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


32 N.Y.2d 17; 295 N.E.2d 753; 343 N.Y.S.2d 70; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1411


January 3, 1973, Argued  
March 15, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed decision from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), affirming their conviction of a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05 by selling obscene material.


OVERVIEW: Both defendant booksellers were convicted of selling obscene material as the trial court found scienter from actual knowledge and the statutory presumption of knowledge, found in N.Y. Penal Law § 235.10. The court below affirmed and defendant sought appeal, claiming that the court erred in failing to grant their motion for judgment as the state had presented no evidence that they actually knew the contents of the magazines they sold. The court, in affirming the decision, held that the presumption of knowledge found in the statute was valid as it was amply supported by the probabilities that they knew what they were selling. The court also found that the presumption did not place an impermissible burden on free speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision as it found that the statutory presumption that defendants knew what they were selling was valid based on the probabilities.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- rebuttable presumption, that one who sells obscene material in course of his business knows its character (Penal Law, § 235.10, subd. 1), is valid and constitutional when applied to "hardcore" obscenity -- defendant who introduces evidence after court fails to grant his motion to dismiss at close of People's case may supply evidence needed for People's case -- managers of bookstore who personally sold many copies of obviously obscene magazine were properly convicted of obscenity (Penal Law, § 235.05).

1. Two bookstores, each of which stocked and sold large quantities of legitimate books and periodicals, also displayed and sold an issue of an "Adults Only" so-called comic cartoon magazine which was found obscene. Each store had a manager who personally sold about 25 copies of this contraband. The trial court properly found, chiefly from the defendants' own testimony, that they knew the obscene content and character of the magazine they were selling.

2. When the trial court, by reserving decision or otherwise, fails to grant the defendant's motion for a dismissal at the close of the People's case, and the defendant then introduces evidence, the defendant may supply a deficiency in the People's case.

3. In a criminal case, it has always been a permissible inference of fact, based on human probabilities, that a possessor of contraband knows its contraband nature. Of course, the inference is rebuttable.

4. The statutory presumption, that a person who sells obscene material in the course of his business knows its content and character (Penal Law, § 235.10, subd. 1), is likewise supported by rational connection and human probability and is likewise rebuttable by evidence, and, when applied to a sale of "hardcore" obscenity, the presumption does not unduly inhibit constitutional freedom of expression, but rather is valid.

5. Accordingly the two managers of bookstores were properly convicted of obscenity (Penal Law, § 235.05).

Board of Education v. Nyquist


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 468; 293 N.E.2d 819; 341 N.Y.S.2d 441; 1973 N.Y. LEXIS 1481


January 5, 1973, Argued  
February 8, 1973, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York (commissioner) and an acting principal, sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court in Albany County (New York), which, in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding and a prior nonfinal order of the appellate division, annulled her appointment, under N.Y. Educ. Law § 2573 (5) and N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6, to permanent principal because she failed to pass the civil service exam.


OVERVIEW: Respondent board of education sought to annul the commissioner's order requiring it to promote the acting principal to a permanent position. The commissioner contended that the acting principal had served 11 years, and she was entitled to the promotion even though she had not passed the required examination for licensing as principal. The commissioner filed a motion to dismiss for insufficiency on the ground that the acting principal had served in excess of the three-year probationary period and that she had acquired tenure under N.Y. Educ. Law § 2573 (1). The trial court, agreeing with the commissioner, granted his motion. The appellate division reversed, holding that the determination was purely arbitrary, and it was in direct contravention of N.Y. Const. art. V, § 6, which required appointment according to merit and fitness ascertained by competitive examination. On remittance to the trial court, the appointment was annulled upon a finding that the pleading raised no issues of fact or legal defenses. The court affirmed, concluding that service under the acting assignment did not satisfy or fulfill the probationary period requirement and that the appointment was contrary to § 6.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the annulment of the acting principal's promotion to principal in a proceeding in which the board challenged her appointment on the ground that she had failed the civil service exam and therefore was not qualified for the position.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- civil service -- appointments -- Commissioner of Education in error in directing Board of Education to grant permanent appointment as principal to one who had been appointed "acting" principal but had never passed examination for such position -- constitutional provision (N. Y. Const., art. V, § 6) that appointments shall be, as far as practicable, by examination may not be avoided -- tenure statute ( Education Law, § 2573, subd. 5) not applicable.

1. A person who had been serving as a licensed assistant principal of a junior high school was appointed "acting" principal of a day elementary school in 1961 under a temporary appointment. On several occasions, she took and failed to pass an examination for principal of a day elementary school. The Commissioner of Education was in error in directing the Board of Education to grant her a permanent appointment as principal. The provision in the New York Constitution (art. V, § 6) that appointments shall be "according to merit and fitness to be ascertained, as far as practicable, by [competitive] examination" may not be avoided. A civil servant may not be appointed without the required examination to a higher position than his license or title calls for upon the basis of his satisfactory performance during temporary or "out-of-title" service in such higher position. The Commissioner acted in a purely arbitrary manner. His determination was therefore properly annulled despite the provisions of section 310 of the Education Law.

2. Although persons who have served a "probationary term" in a position to which they have been appointed are thereupon "entitled to receive permanent appointments" and the tenure which attaches thereto ( Education Law, § 2573, subd. 5), an appointment may only be made by the Board from an eligible list of those who have passed a competitive examination. The appointment to an "acting" capacity of a person who has not passed an examination is not the "appointment" envisaged by the statute.

Brumfield v. Tofany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

119 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lascaris v. Wyman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 386; 292 N.E.2d 667; 340 N.Y.S.2d 397; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 922; 57 A.L.R.3d 1295; 82 L.R.R.M. 2634


November 27, 1972, Argued  
December 28, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county social services commissioner sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court of the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the decision of the trial court that had granted respondent state social services commissioner's motion for summary judgment, and which directed the county commissioner to provide public assistance benefits to striking union workers.


OVERVIEW: The union went on strike against the telephone company. Several of the workers filed for public assistance, registering as required by N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131(4). The county commissioner brought an action in the trial court against the state social services commissioner, seeking confirmation of his determination denying assistance to the striking workers. The county commissioner filed a motion for summary judgment. The trial court granted the motion in favor of the county commissioner. The appellate court reversed the decision of the trial court. On the county commissioner's appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the appellate court. The court determined that N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131 provided that persons who registered and held themselves out as available for employment were eligible for public assistance, and this included persons on strike if they were "unable to maintain themselves" within the sense of § 131 and otherwise complied with the provisions of that statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, which had reversed judgment in favor of the state commissioner allowing public assistance benefits to striking union workers.


HEADNOTES

Social services -- benefits to strikers -- persons on strike may obtain public assistance (Social Services Law, § 131) -- person on strike does not "refuse" employment (Social Services Law, § 131, subd. 4) -- such payment to strikers is not subsidization of strike and does not constitute unconstitutional interference with free collective bargaining.

1. Shortly after commencement of a strike, certain members of local unions applied for public assistance and their applications were denied. In an action by the County Commissioner of the Department of Social Services against the State Commissioner seeking confirmation of this determination, the Appellate Division properly directed plaintiff to pay public assistance benefits to the striking workers who were eligible therefor. It stated that, immediately prior to the 1971 amendment to subdivision 4 of section 131 of the Social Services Law, strikers were eligible to receive public assistance provided that they registered with the State employment office and did not refuse any new employment opportunities and that the amendment to subdivision 4 does not affect their rights.

2. Under subdivision 4, as it read prior to 1971, a person on strike was an "employable person" and ineligible for public assistance if he "refused to accept employment in which he is able to engage." A person on strike does not, simply because he is on strike, "refuse" to accept employment.

3. The amendment of subdivision 4 may be regarded as legislative approval of the administrative construction long accorded section 131 by the State Commissioner, sanctioning assistance payments to strikers.

4. The payment of welfare benefits to needy strikers may not be regarded as the equivalent of State subsidization of the strike in violation of the State's policy of neutrality in labor-management disputes.

5. Plaintiff contends that a striking worker should be ineligible for assistance, since, it being likely that he will return to his "struck" employer at the conclusion of the strike, he has in effect refused to accept any other employment. However, applicants for assistance in the present case have registered for other employment as required by subdivision 4, and there is no evidence that they have failed to attend job interviews or refused referrals or refused to accept an offer of employment.

6. Granting public assistance to strikers does not constitute an unconstitutional interference with the system of free collective bargaining prescribed by the Federal Government.

 

120 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New Rochelle Water Co. v. Public Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 397; 292 N.E.2d 767; 340 N.Y.S.2d 617; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 923


October 30, 1972, Argued  
December 28, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant utilities brought actions against respondent Public Service Commission (commission) challenging that portion of the commission's determination that denied retroactive application of increased permanent utility rates for the period the utilities' proposed rate increases were suspended. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department affirmed the commission's determination. The utilities appealed.


OVERVIEW: The utilities brought actions against the commission challenging that portion of the commission's determination that denied retroactive application of increased permanent utility rates for the period the utilities proposed increases were suspended. The trial court affirmed the commission's determination. On appeal, the utilities contended that N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 113 required the commission to grant reparations to public utility companies where a temporary rate increase granted by the commission during the suspension period was less than the final rate increase. The court held (1) Pub. Serv. Law § 113 conferred upon the commission discretionary authority to order reparations, (2) the records adequately established that the commission exercised its discretion in each case and that its refusal to authorize reparations was not arbitrary, capricious, or an abuse of discretion, (3) due process required recoupment for excessive temporary rate reductions but did not require reparations where proposed rate increases were suspended for a reasonable period of time pending investigation and determination of the final rate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the appellate court, which had affirmed the commission's determination.


HEADNOTES

Public utilities -- rates -- when Public Service Commission grants final rate increase which exceeds temporarily granted rate increase, it may or may not grant retroactivity (Public Service Law, § 113, as amd.); such discretionary power is constitutional -- in fixing final rates, Public Service Commission gave utility company permission to apply, without further hearings, for further rate increase to cover future taxes on new construction, as soon as amount of such taxes would become known; refusal, instead, to allow $ 200,000 estimated adjustment in operating income did not violate due process.

1. When the Public Service Commission temporarily suspends proposed rate increases filed by water companies, and temporarily allows lesser rate increases (5.5% in these two cases), and then finally grants rate increases (7.6% in one case, and 6.95% in the other case) which exceed such temporarily allowed rates, the Commission "may" or may not, in its discretion, make such final rates retroactive (Public Service Law, § 113, as amd. by L. 1970, ch. 271; cf. § 114). This discretionary power in such case to grant or to deny "appropriate reparation to the company" (Public Service Law, § 113, as amd.) is not confiscatory or violative of due process of law or of the equal protection of the laws; rather, it is valid and constitutional. In each of the cases here involved, the Commission's denial of "reparation" was not arbitrary or capricious or an abuse of discretion.

2. In fixing the final rates, the Public Service Commission refused to allow an estimated adjustment in operating income for estimated future taxes of some $ 200,000 on realty and on special franchises which would result from the construction of some new so-called "backbone" facilities which had not yet appeared on the tax assessment rolls; however, the Commission gave the utility company permission to apply, without further hearings, for a further rate increase on an expedited basis to cover such tax increases as soon as the amount of such taxes would become known. This determination by the Commission did not violate due process, but rather was proper and constitutional.

 

121 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schneider v. Rockefeller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 420; 293 N.E.2d 67; 340 N.Y.S.2d 889; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 924


October 31, 1972, Argued  
December 28, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner citizens sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's order that dismissed the petitions, granted respondent legislator's motion for summary judgment, and declared that 1972 New York Laws ch. 11 was a valid constitutional apportionment statute.


OVERVIEW: The citizens argued that in redistricting the Senate and Assembly, the legislature went too far in implementing the equal population principle by unnecessarily dividing some counties, contrary to N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 4 and 5. They contended that chapter 11 constituted a partisan gerrymander and was required to be set it aside on both federal and state constitutional grounds. The citizens contended that the legislature misapplied the constitutional formula for adjusting the size of the senate. The citizens urged that their several alternate plans approached mathematical exactness and minimized or eliminated violations of county lines. The court overruled the citizens' contentions, holding that the procedure followed by the legislature was valid and did not transgress any constitutional provision. The legislative determination was reasonable because the best available census data were used and the constitutional requirements were satisfied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision that denied the citizens motions, dismissed their petitions, and granted summary judgment in all respects.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- redistricting -- reapportioning -- Legislature acted in substantial conformity with principle of equal population in redistricting and reapportioning State Legislature (L. 1972, ch. 11) -- integrity of counties preserved (cf. N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 4, 5) -- Senate and Assembly districts sufficiently compact and contiguous (cf. N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 4, 5) -- alleged gerrymandering of special interest groups not decided -- formula for adjusting size of Senate valid -- legislative method with regard to Bronx and Westchester Counties not unconstitutional -- no violation of State Constitution (art. III, § 14) provision that no bill shall become law unless it is on desks of members for three days -- best available Federal census data used and constitutional requirement satisfied.

1. In redistricting and reapportioning the State Legislature (L. 1972, ch. 11), the Legislature acted in substantial conformity with the principle of equal population. In the Senate, the plan produces a total deviation from population equality of 1.82%; and in the Assembly, the deviation is 3.38%.

2. The Legislature has not unduly departed from the State constitutional command that the integrity of counties be preserved (N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 4, 5).

3. The New York Constitution (art. III, § 4) requires that Senate districts "be in as compact form as practicable" and "consist of contiguous territory" and (§ 5) provides that Assembly districts shall be formed from "convenient and contiguous territory in as compact form as practicable." These constitutional requirements must be harmonized with the principle of substantial equality of population among districts. "Contiguous territory" means "territory touching, adjoining and connected, as distinguished from territory separated by other territory." The requirement of compactness is a practical one but the term "compact" has no precise meaning. Although some of the challenged districts are irregular in form, none are so aggravated or outrageous as would warrant the conclusion that the Legislature has completely departed from the constitutional standard.

4. The alleged gerrymandering of special interest groups -- racial, social, economic or ideological -- is too sensitive and significant an issue to be decided on this record.

5. The procedure, admittedly complex, followed by the Legislature for adjusting the size of the Senate (cf. N. Y. Const., art. III, §§ 3, 4) is valid and does not transgress any constitutional provision.

6. There is no constitutional infirmity in the legislative method with regard to Bronx and Westchester Counties. The legislative method takes into account that a portion of the Bronx belonged to Westchester in 1894, that in 1895 New York County annexed parts of Westchester County (L. 1895, ch. 934), and that in 1912 New York County ceded certain territory, including the territory annexed from Westchester in 1895, to form Bronx County (L. 1912, ch. 548). Under the circumstances, the legislative determination is reasonable.

7. The State Constitution (art. III, § 14) provides that "No bill shall be passed or become a law unless it shall have been printed and upon the desks of the members, in its final form, at least three calendar legislative days prior to its final passage". There was substantial compliance with the letter and spirit of the constitutional requirement.

8. The State Constitution states that the latest Federal census shall be "controlling as to the number of inhabitants in the state or any part thereof" for purposes of State legislative apportionment. (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 4.) Although the court was free to direct discovery of the census data pending its decision on motions for dismissal and summary judgment ( CPLR 3214, subd. [b]), its failure to do so was not, under the circumstances, an abuse of discretion. The best available 1970 Federal census data were used and the constitutional requirement has been satisfied.

903 Park Ave. Corp. v. City Rent Agency


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 330; 291 N.E.2d 141; 338 N.Y.S.2d 901; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 989


June 7, 1972, Argued  
November 3, 1972, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargued September 25, 1972.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order and judgment of the special term court declaring that N.Y. City Local Law No. 7 of 1972 was constitutional and dismissing the landlord's complaint in favor of respondent City Rent Agency.


OVERVIEW: The State of New York enacted the N.Y. Laws of 1971 ch. 372. After sanctioning continued rent control by cities having a population of a million or more, Chapter 372 went on to prohibit the enactment of any local law controlling rents which contained provisions more stringent or restrictive than provisions presently in effect. The City of New York thereafter adopted N.Y. City Local Law No. 7 of 1972, which extended exemptions from rent increases previously granted to eligible senior citizens for a further period of six months. The landlord brought an action to declare Local Law No. 7 invalid and unconstitutional. Unsuccessful in the courts below, the landlord filed an appeal to the court. While the appeal was pending, the city adopted N.Y. City Local Law No. 51 of 1972, which added N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 467-b, expressly authorizing the city to grant exemptions to senior citizens from rent increases if the city fully compensated the landlords for the losses occasioned by such exemptions. The court reversed the order of the appellate court and remitted the case to the special term court with directions to dismiss the complaint upon the ground that the issue presented was moot.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court, without costs, and remitted the case to the special term court with directions to dismiss the complaint upon the ground that the issue presented was moot in an action brought by the landlord to have a local law declared invalid and unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- academic and moot questions -- chapter 372 of Laws of 1971 sanctioned continued rent control and prohibited enactment of local law controlling rents which contained provisions more stringent or restrictive than those in effect -- City of New York thereafter adopted Local Law No. 7 of 1972 extending exemptions from rent increases previously granted -- while appeal was pending in action brought by landlord to declare Local Law No. 7 unconstitutional, City of New York adopted Local Law No. 51 of 1972, pursuant to chapter 689 of Laws of 1972, superseding Local Law No. 7 of 1972 -- only question presented was rendered academic and moot -- issue not one likely to recur -- no justification for continuing to entertain appeal.

1. Chapter 372 of the Laws of 1971 sanctioned continued rent control in New York City, and prohibited the enactment of any local law controlling rents which contained provisions "more stringent or restrictive" than provisions "presently in effect." The City of New York thereafter adopted Local Law No. 7 of 1972 which extended exemptions from rent increases previously granted (Local Law No. 31 of 1970) to eligible senior citizens for a further period of six months. Plaintiff, a landlord, thereupon brought this action to declare Local Law No. 7 invalid and unconstitutional on the ground that it violated chapter 372 of the Laws of 1971. While the appeal was pending from an adverse decision, the city, pursuant to chapter 689 of the Laws of 1972, adopted Local Law No. 51 of 1972 which replaced and superseded the earlier local law (Local Law No. 7 of 1972), and thereby rendered academic and moot the only question presented on the appeal. Since that issue is not one that is likely to recur, there is no reason or justification for continuing to entertain the appeal. The complaint should be dismissed.

2. The court remarks on the deleterious side effects which rent control, as administered by the city, has had on the maintenance of rental housing accommodations, and particularly on housing occupied by many of the elderly tenants affected by the exemption provisions involved in this case.

People v. Keough


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

The trial court disallowed a demurrer to the indictment upon finding the "factual situation" similar to that in People v. Radich, which had not then been decided by the court. The appellate division affirmed, finding that the legitimate public interest in preventing a breach of the peace and an outbreak of violence was threatened. Reversing the appellate division's decision and vacating defendant's convictions, the court found that there was nothing in the photographs, which concededly constituted the sole basis for the indictment, comparable in any substantial degree to the "constructions" in Radich and nothing that offered the likelihood of incitement to disorder and by public exhibition afforded an opportunity for protest and counterprotest with consequent potential of public disorder. An indictment charging that certain photographs defied and cast contempt upon the flag of the United States, in violation of section 136 (subds. d and f) of the General Business Law, should be dismissed. ( People v. Radich, 26 N Y 2d 114, distinguished.)
