People v. Goodman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 262; 290 N.E.2d 139; 338 N.Y.S.2d 97; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1628


September 25, 1972, Argued  
November 1, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed defendant's conviction under Village of Ocean Beach, N.Y., Ordinance art. II, § 4 for not complying with a billboard regulation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant maintained four signs on his drugstore. He challenged his conviction for a violation of Village of Ocean Beach, N.Y., Ordinance art. II, § 4, relating to commercial signs. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the ordinance represented a valid and permissible exercise of the police power and that defendant's conviction thereunder was proper. The court held that the ordinance merely proscribed the erection and maintenance of commercial signs which, in the circumstances and setting, would be unnecessarily offensive to the visual sensibilities of the average person and would materially detract from the community and district pattern. The court denied defendant's equal protection claim, finding that the ordinance had been applied to other individuals as three other persons were convicted under the same ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for violating a local ordinance dealing with billboard restrictions. The court found that the ordinance was a valid and permissible exercise of police power.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- erection of signs -- village ordinance, which provided that signs for existing commercial or business uses might not exceed four square feet in area, is valid and permissible exercise of police power -- defendant, registered pharmacist operating drugstore, properly found guilty of violating ordinance -- aesthetics is valid subject of legislative concern -- means adopted in ordinance reasonably related to community policy -- ordinance not unconstitutionally vague -- defendant failed to show conscious, intentional discrimination -- claim of discriminatory enforcement should be raised before trial by motion to dismiss prosecution upon constitutional grounds.

1. A village ordinance provided that signs within the village for existing commercial or business uses might not exceed four square feet in area. Defendant, a registered pharmacist operating a drugstore, who maintained four signs on his drugstore, each one exceeding four square feet in area, was properly found guilty of violating the ordinance. The ordinance represents a valid and permissible exercise of the police power.

2. Aesthetics is a valid subject of legislative concern. Concededly, this ordinance was motivated by aesthetic considerations. The means adopted in this ordinance are reasonably related to the community policy and are not unduly oppressive.

3. Defendant's business is largely a commercial enterprise and its essential character is not affected merely because, on occasion, first aid is administered there.

4. The ordinance is not unconstitutional for vagueness.

5. Defendant was afforded sufficient opportunity to establish his claim of discriminatory enforcement and failed to meet the heavy burden of showing conscious, intentional discrimination.

6. In this case, the claim of discriminatory enforcement was raised by a motion to dismiss the information at the close of the People's direct case. The claim of discriminatory enforcement should not be considered as an affirmative defense to the criminal charge, to be determined together with the issue of guilt by the trier of fact, but, rather, should be addressed to the court before trial as a motion to dismiss the prosecution upon constitutional grounds.

 

125 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cooke v. Lomenzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 244; 288 N.E.2d 291; 336 N.Y.S.2d 457; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1116


September 12, 1972, Argued  
September 25, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate sought review of the order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the judgment from the trial court directing respondent Secretary of State to conduct a drawing to determine all candidates' positions on the ballot, and dismissed the proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The candidate filed a proceeding against the Secretary under N.Y. Elec. Law § 330. The trial court directed the Secretary to conduct a drawing to determine the position on the ballot, where appropriate, of the vertical and horizontal columns of candidates. On appeal, the intermediate appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment and dismissed the proceeding. On further appeal, the court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's order. The court held that the Secretary's manner of listing the candidates on the ballot was not arbitrary or unreasonable. The Secretary properly denied the candidate's request to have the order of the names of all candidates determined by lot, because displacement on the ballot would have been prejudicial to the candidates of the other parties whose ballot positions were fixed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's order reversing the judgment from the trial court and dismissing the proceeding under the election law.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- position on ballot -- in election for Judge of Court of Appeals, there being three vacancies, Secretary of State listed political parties in accordance with number of votes in last gubernatorial election and Republican candidates in order of certification; candidates of other parties were listed in such way as to guarantee that no name was placed in different column; such manner of listing was neither arbitrary nor unreasonable -- since exercise of right to draw lots for position, granted by Election Law, would prejudice other candidates and render possible malfunction of voting machines, Secretary of State properly denied request of one Democratic candidate for drawing of position by lot.

1. Each of the major political parties nominated three candidates for three vacancies for Judge of the Court of Appeals. The Secretary of State listed political parties on the ballot in accordance with the number of votes cast in the last gubernatorial election and Republican candidates were positioned in the order of their certification to the Secretary of State, the candidates of the other parties being listed in such a way as to guarantee that no name was placed in a different column. This manner of listing was neither arbitrary nor unreasonable.

2. The right, granted by subdivision 1 of section 104 of the Election Law to a candidate to request that ballot position be determined by lot where two or more candidates are nominated by one party for an office to which two or more persons are to be elected, is impaired whenever its exercise might work a displacement on the ballot prejudicial to the candidates of other parties or would otherwise effect a violation of section 248 of the Election Law or any other provision thereof. Accordingly petitioner, a Democratic candidate who received Conservative Party indorsement and who requested a drawing by lot was properly denied it. Difficulties would develop from the exercise of that right, prejudicial to other candidates which would not be obviated by use of a fourth column, since section 248 of the Election Law limits the number of columns or rows to the number of candidates to be elected. Moreover, inherent limitations on the voting machines as well as the prospect of malfunction require that the name of a particular candidate always appear in the same column. Additionally, since petitioner acquiesced in having his name, as a Conservative Party candidate, placed in the third column, the Secretary of State was justified in keeping his name, as a Democratic nominee, in the same column.

 

126 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Patricia A. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 83; 286 N.E.2d 432; 335 N.Y.S.2d 33; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1123


June 2, 1972, Argued  
July 7, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order placing her on probation for one year after adjudicating that she was a person in need of supervision.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was adjudicated a person in need of supervision (PINS) pursuant to N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 712(b). The statute defined persons in need of supervision as males less than 16 and females less than 18 persons who were incorrigible, ungovernable, or habitually disobedient and beyond lawful control of the other authority. Appellant challenged the statute as violating due process for being unconstitutionally vague, and for unconstitutionally discriminating against females. The court reversed the order. The court concluded that the statute was sufficiently definite, but the age-sex distinction was unjustified, and that section of the statute had to be stricken as unconstitutional. The object of the PINS statute was to rehabilitate and treat young persons. There was no reasonable ground for differentiating between males and females over 16 and under 18. The imputation was that females who engaged in conduct, sexual or otherwise, ought more to be censured and their conduct subject to greater control and regulation than that of males.


OUTCOME: Order placing appellant on probation was reversed because the statutory age-sex distinction was not justified and had to be stricken as unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- discrimination on account of sex -- Family Court Act (§ subd. [b]), insofar as it defines person in need of supervision as one who is incorrigible, ungovernable or habitually disobedient and beyond lawful control of parent or other authority, is sufficiently definite to render statute constitutional -- however, insofar as it defines person in need of supervision as male less than 16 years of age and female less than 18 years of age, it unconstitutionally discriminates against females -- no reasonable ground for differentiating between males and females over 16 and under 18.

1. The Family Court Act ( § 712, subd. [b]) defines a person in need of supervision as "a male less than sixteen years of age and a female less than eighteen years of age who does not attend school in accord with the provisions of part one of article sixty-five of the education law [relating to truancy or other nonattendance] or who is incorrigible, ungovernable or habitually disobedient and beyond the lawful control of parent or other lawful authority." The statute is not unconstitutionally vague. The terms, "habitual truant," "incorrigible," "ungovernable," "habitually disobedient and beyond * * * law control" are sufficiently definite to render the statute constitutional.

2. The statute does, however, unconstitutionally discriminate against females. The fact that the statute seeks to provide rehabilitation and treatment for young persons who engage in the sort of conduct there proscribed affords no reasonable ground for differentiating between males and females over 16 and under 18. A girl of 16 or 17 may not be subjected to a possible loss of liberty for conduct which would be licit for 16 and 17-year-old boys. So much of section 712 (subd. [b]) of the Family Court Act as encompasses females between the ages of 16 and 18 must be stricken as unconstitutional.

 

127 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dorn "HH" v. Lawrence "II"


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 154; 286 N.E.2d 717; 335 N.Y.S.2d 274; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1128


May 5, 1972, Argued  
July 7, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment that declared him to be the father of a child born out of wedlock to respondent mother and ordered him to pay child support. The father claimed that the statute that excluded uncorroborated proof of access by others to the woman involved denied him due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: The mother brought a filiation proceeding to determine that the father was the parent of her child born out of wedlock and to require that he support the child. The father offered proof by three of his fellow students at the college petitioner and he attended that each had had sexual intercourse with petitioner during the period within which conception could have occurred. Because this testimony was without corroboration it was excluded. The father was determined to be the parent. The judgment was affirmed on appeal and the father sought further review on constitutional grounds. The court held that: (1) N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 531 required corroboration for such testimony to be admissible; (2) the purpose of the rule was to prevent the father from suborning perjury and provided a rational basis for the statute; (3) persons could be classified for purposes of legislation without infringement of the due process and equal protection guarantees and such classification would not be disturbed if any state of facts could reasonably be conceived to sustain its classification, provided only that it was not palpably arbitrary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that determined the father to be the parent of the child born to the mother out of wedlock and ordered him to support the child, without costs.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- corroboration in filiation proceeding -- statute (Family Ct. Act, § 531), which provides that, in filiation proceeding, testimony of access by others offered by respondent must be corroborated, not unconstitutional -- fact that respondent's proof must be corroborated but petitioner's proof of respondent's access does not require corroboration is not denial of equal protection or due process.

The statute (Family Ct. Act, § 531) which provides that, in a filiation proceeding, "If the respondent shall offer testimony of access by others at or about the time charged in the complaint, such testimony shall not be competent or admissible in evidence except when corroborated by other facts and circumstances tending to prove such access", is not unconstitutional. The fact that the putative father may, with comparative ease, suborn perjury and obtain unscrupulous persons to testify that they had had intercourse with the complainant suggests a rational basis for the classification. The fact that respondent's proof of access by others has to be corroborated but petitioner's proof of respondent's access does not require corroboration is not a denial of equal protection or of due process. The differentiation is between, and the classification is of, adversaries in litigation, distinguished by the affirmative obligation imposed upon one as against the negative posture assumed by the other.

 

128 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Abronovitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 160; 286 N.E.2d 721; 335 N.Y.S.2d 279; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1129


June 1, 1972, Argued  
July 7, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed and the remaining counts of the indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed in part and reversed in part judgments of the county court, which convicted defendants on several counts of obscenity in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 35.05 and denied their motion to suppress the material on which the judgments rested.


OVERVIEW: A judge issued a search warrant authorizing a search of defendants' premises for magazines and photographs alleged in a police affidavit to have been obscene material. The search warrant did not name or otherwise describe the magazines referred to in the supporting affidavit, but permitted the police to search for and seize a wide range of material. The police seized material and arrested defendants. The trial court convicted defendants of several counts of obscenity in violation of § 35.05. The appellate court affirmed the convictions as to two counts and reversed the judgments on the remaining counts. The court reversed the orders of the appellate court, dismissed the remaining counts of the indictment and granted defendants' motion to suppress the material on which the judgments rested. The court found that the police seizure was judicially unsupervised. The court held that the judge was required to have decided preliminarily if the particular material on which he was asked to authorize seizure was obscene and that that decision should not have been left open to the judgment of the police.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the appellate court, which affirmed in part and reversed in part judgments of the county court, which convicted defendants on several counts of obscenity. The court dismissed the remaining counts of the indictment and granted defendants' motion to suppress the material on which the judgments rested.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- search warrant -- defendants' convictions were based on magazines not described in search warrant or underlying affidavit and not before Judge who had issued warrant -- they were seized on judgment of police executing search warrant -- determination whether particular material is obscene must not be left to judgment of police -- indictment dismissed -- search and seizure not justified as incident to lawful arrest.

1. On an affidavit by a police officer stating that defendants at their book store possessed "obscene material", a search warrant was issued authorizing a search of the premises of defendants. It did not name or otherwise describe the magazines referred to in the supporting affidavit, but permitted the police to search for and seize any material which depicted "scenes of nude persons" and which "expose the female and male genital organs, the female and male pubic areas, including the male penis and the female breasts". Defendants' convictions were based on two magazines which were not described in the search warrant or in the underlying affidavit, and, therefore, were not before the Judge who had issued the warrant. They were seized, rather, on the judgment of the police executing the search warrant that they violated section 235.05 of the Penal Law. A Judge must decide preliminarily whether the particular material on which he is asked to authorize seizure is obscene and this must not be left open to the judgment of the police. The indictment should be dismissed.

2. While a warrant of arrest had been issued for defendants for selling four named and described magazines, these magazines were not the ones on which the present convictions rest. As to those that were described in the warrant of arrest, there has been either a dismissal or no conviction. Thus, either the police seized the material on which these convictions are based exercising their own judicially unsupervised judgment under a general search warrant as to what is obscene within the statute; or in making arrests under a warrant concerned with entirely different publications and material, they made a similar unsupervised judgment in seizing other material unrelated to that arrest on which these convictions are based.

3. The procedure followed by the police here is such that it is unnecessary to reach the question whether the two publications are obscene within a constitutionally permissible construction of the New York statute.

 

129 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kovarsky v. Housing & Development Administration


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 184; 286 N.E.2d 882; 335 N.Y.S.2d 383; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1131


June 1, 1972, Argued  
July 7, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenants challenged the conversion of their building under The Rent Stabilization Law of 1969 (RSL), New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code ch. 51, tit. YY, to co-operative ownership. The trial court dismissed the tenants' petition against respondent city officials. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the trial court's judgment. The tenants appealed.


OVERVIEW: The tenants occupied a residential building. Pursuant to the RSL, the landlords executed a plan of cooperative organization to convert the rental units to co-operative ownership. A percentage of the tenants had to agree to join in the plan. The tenants alleged in their petition that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code ch. 51, § YY51-6.0(c)(9) was unconstitutional and that the city officials acted arbitrarily in approving the plan of cooperative organization. The trial court ruled that the constitutional issue could not be determined in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. § 78 proceeding and that the city official's actions were not arbitrary. The appellate division affirmed without opinion. On appeal, the court held that: (1) in an effort to determine the whether a cause for complaint had been stated the trial court should have treated the complaint as a declaratory judgment action and addressed the constitutional issue; (2) New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code ch. 51, § YY51-6.0(c)(9)(a) was constitutional; and (3) the RSL did not limit a more extensive right, but granted the tenants a limited right that they did not have before.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision from the appellate court that dismissed the tenants' petition. The court noted that the tenant could raise a constitutional challenge to the RSL by way of an action for a declaratory judgment.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- conversion to co-operative ownership -- in CPLR article 78 proceeding by tenants in building where plan to convert to co-operative ownership has been presented, Special Term should have considered tenants' claim that section YY51-6.0 of Rent Stabilization Law, under which landlord refused to execute renewal leases, was unconstitutional -- since parties briefed issue, Court of Appeals will consider it without remand therefor to Special Term -- Rent Stabilization Law, which gave tenants limited right to renewal, subject to exception where plan for conversion has been presented, effective upon purchase of apartments by 35% of tenants, is constitutional.

1. In a CPLR article 78 proceeding by tenants in a building where a plan of co-operative organization has been presented to convert it to co-operative ownership, Special Term should have taken cognizance of petitioners' claim that section YY51-6.0 of the Rent Stabilization Law (RSL) (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 51, tit. YY) pursuant to which their landlord refused to execute renewal leases, was unconstitutional. While a proceeding under article 78 is not the proper vehicle to test the constitutionality of legislative enactments, since all necessary parties were before the court, under subdivision (c) of CPLR 103 it should have treated the proceeding as an action for a declaratory judgment to test constitutionality and proceeded accordingly. Since CPLR 3017 (subd. [a]) provides that "[relief] in the alternative or of several different types may be demanded", the general rule, recognized in Matter of Diocese of Rochester v. Planning Bd. (1 N Y 2d 508), that a party cannot, in the same proceeding, rely upon a statute or retain benefits thereunder and attack its constitutionality, is no longer viable.

2. Since the corporation counsel and the other parties concerned with the co-operative plan have briefed the issue of constitutionality, remand to Special Term is not necessary and it is proper for the Court of Appeals to consider it.

3. Prior to enactment of the RSL (Local Laws, 1969, No. 16 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 51, tit. YY), tenants possessed no rights to renewal leases. The enactment of the RSL granted them the limited right to renewal of leases, subject to an exception where a plan for conversion has been presented, effective upon the purchase of apartments by a certain number of tenants. Appellant tenants have no ground for complaint and the provision for conversion to co-operative ownership upon purchase by a minority (35%) of the tenants (§ YY51-6.0) is not unconstitutional. Judgment is directed in favor of respondents declaring that section YY51-6.0 (subd. c, par. [9], cl. [a]) is constitutional.

Schneider v. Wyman

133 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Byrn v. New York City Health & Hospitals Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 194; 286 N.E.2d 887; 335 N.Y.S.2d 390; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1132


May 30, 1972, Argued  
July 7, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, guardian for unborn children, appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), reversing an order of the lower court which enjoined defendant hospital from performing abortions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, guardian of unborn children, sought a declaratory judgment to declare the 1970 abortion liberalization statute unconstitutional. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction against defendant hospital, ordering it to stop performing abortions. The intermediate court vacated the injunction and sustained the statute's validity. On appeal, the court affirmed, concluding that the issue of the litigation, whether unborn children were to be recognized as legal persons with a right to life, was not justiciable because it required only a policy decision. The issue was outside the law unless the legislature provided otherwise. It was the duty of the legislature, not the courts, to provide legal personality for the unborn, or to provide other protections.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed; the court concluded that only the legislature had the power to give legal personality rights to the unborn because only it could make the required policy decision.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- abortion -- statute defining "justifiable abortional act" and permitting abortion under stated circumstances (L. 1970, ch. 127) not unconstitutional -- Constitution does not confer or require legal personality for unborn -- whether law should accord legal personality is policy question to be determined by Legislature, subject to Constitution.

The statute defining a "justifiable abortional act" and permitting abortion under stated circumstances (L. 1970, ch. 127) is not unconstitutional. Children in embryo need not be recognized as legal persons or entities entitled under the State and Federal Constitutions to a right to life. While unborn, and even unconceived, children have been recognized as acquiring certain rights or interests, unborn children have never been recognized as persons in the law in the whole sense. Whether the law should accord legal personality is a policy question which, in most instances, devolves on the Legislature, subject to the Constitution. The Constitution does not confer or require legal personality for the unborn; the Legislature may, or it may do something less, and provide some protection far short of conferring legal personality.

 

134 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gnozzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 134; 286 N.E.2d 706; 335 N.Y.S.2d 257; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1177


May 31, 1972, Argued  
July 6, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v. Gnozzo: Order reversed and case remitted to Erie County Court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.

In People v. Zorn: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which convicted defendant of possession of gambling records. The government appealed, in a second case, from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: The appeal involved two cases. In the first, an eavesdropping warrant was issued for a home telephone and a public telephone. In the application for an eavesdropping warrant, the prosecutor averred that a stockbroker testified to placing bets with an individual, who had prior gambling convictions. The affidavit and the warrant stated that the individual was overheard placing a call on the public telephone to an outside person. The next day another call to the same number included conversations about betting on sports events. Pursuant to the search warrant, the police entered the apartment and found papers indicating that the apartment was used for gambling. Defendant came to the apartment and was arrested. Defendant's motion to suppress was not granted and he was found guilty of possession of gambling records. In the second case, a warrant was issued and a conversation was intercepted relating to gambling. Evidence uncovered during the search led to defendant's arrest. That defendant's motion to suppress evidence was granted. The court reversed the order in the second case and affirmed the order in the first case, finding the interception of communications was authorized.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order in the second case, granting defendant's motion to suppress, and affirmed the order in the first case, which did not grant the motion to suppress and found defendant guilty. The court found the interception of communications was authorized.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- eavesdropping warrants and search warrants -- telephone conversations, intercepted pursuant to eavesdropping order and involving crime related to crime specified in order, may validly and constitutionally be used to support warrants to search persons and premises of parties not named in eavesdropping order; and no amendment of such order is needed -- for commercial gambling activities, eavesdropping order may validly be limited to 30 days and may be unrestricted as to daytime or nighttime -- prosecutor's affidavit showed probable cause to listen to telephone calls made by named convicted gambler on his home telephone and in telephone booth; and, on basis of those intercepted conversations, warrant was properly issued to search different apartment at address which was obtained from telephone company, wherein telephone was located to which said named gambler was making calls; such search properly led to arrest and conviction of different gambler.

1. Telephone conversations which are intercepted pursuant to a proper eavesdropping order, and which involve a crime related to a crime specified in the order, may validly and constitutionally be used to support the issuance of search warrants against the persons and premises of parties to the intercepted conversations who are not named in the eavesdropping order; and no amendment of such eavesdropping order is needed (U. S. Const., 4th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 12; Code Crim. Pro., § 814, subd. 3; § 817, subd. 2, par. [b]; § 819, subds. 2, 4; § 825, subds. 3, 4; CPL 700.05, subd. 3; 700.20, subd. 2, par. [b]; 700.30, subds. 2, 4; 700.65, subds. 3, 4).

2. For the interception of conversations relating to commercial gambling activities, an order which is limited to 30 days and which is not restricted as to daytime or nighttime, is constitutional and valid (Code Crim. Pro., § 819, subd. 7; § 821; CPL 700.30, 700.40; cf. U. S. Code, tit. 18, § 2518, subds. [5], [6]).

3. As to a known, convicted gambler named in an eavesdropping order, the supporting affidavit of the prosecutor showed probable cause to intercept conversations on said gambler's home telephone and on a public telephone in a telephone booth which the gambler was using. On the basis of those intercepted conversations, a warrant was properly issued to search an apartment at a different address, which was obtained from the telephone company, and wherein was located the telephone to which the named gambler was making calls. Pursuant to the search warrant, the police entered the apartment and found papers indicating that it was a wire room for large-scale gambling. While the police were there, a person who was not named in either of the warrants came to the apartment and opened the door with a key. The police arrested him, and he was charged with possession of gambling records. His motions to vacate the eavesdropping order and the search warrant and to suppress the evidence were rightly denied, and he was properly convicted.

 

135 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lasky


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 146; 286 N.E.2d 712; 335 N.Y.S.2d 266; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1178


April 28, 1972, Argued  
July 6, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a denial of defendant's petition for a writ of error coram nobis. Defendant argued that a prior conviction was not a constitutionally valid predicate conviction to support his fourth-felony offender sentence, on the ground that he had not been represented by counsel.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of conspiracy, third-degree burglary, and first-degree grand larceny. The district attorney filed an information charging defendant with fourth-felony offender status. The lower court found that defendant's proof that counsel at a prior conviction did not represent him was not sufficient. Defendant was sentenced as a fourth-felony offender. Defendant filed an earlier appeal requesting resentencing on ground that the burden of proving the unconstitutionality of the prior convictions had been wrongfully placed upon him. The petition was denied. Defendant filed the present petition for a writ of error coram nobis, reasserting his claim that the prior conviction was not a constitutionally valid predicate conviction to support the fourth-felony-offender sentence, on the ground that he was not represented by counsel. The lower court denied the petition, and the appellate court affirmed. The court held that the burden of proof was properly placed upon defendant at the sentencing hearing. The court further held that, on balancing the evidence, the court could only conclude that defendant failed to carry this burden of proof.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the denial of defendant's petition for a writ of error coram nobis. The court held that the burden of proving the unconstitutionality of defendant's prior convictions, used to support his sentence as a fourth-felony offender, was properly placed upon him at the sentencing hearing and that defendant did not sustain this burden of proof.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- right to counsel -- when District Attorney filed information charging defendant with being fourth-felony offender, burden of proof was properly placed upon defendant -- documentary record shows defendant was represented by counsel at Arkansas conviction -- defendant failed to carry burden of proof -- statute ( CPL 400.20, subds. 5, 6, formerly Code Crim. Pro., § 470-a, subds. 6, 7) placing burden of proof on People not applicable.

1. Following defendant's conviction in 1966, the District Attorney filed an information charging the defendant with being a fourth-felony offender. The court, after a hearing, found defendant's proof upon his claim that he was not represented by counsel at an Arkansas conviction in 1960 was not sufficient to repudiate the proof offered by the District Attorney. In the present petition for a writ of error coram nobis defendant again asserts that he had not been represented by counsel. The burden of proof was properly placed upon the defendant at the 1966 sentencing hearing. The documentary record clearly shows that defendant was represented by counsel. The defendant failed to carry his burden of proof.

2. The fact that CPL 400.20 (subds. 5, 6) (formerly Code Crim. Pro., § 470-a, subds. 6, 7) places the burden of proof upon the People in determining whether a defendant should be sentenced as a persistent felony offender does not alter the result inasmuch as the statute did not become effective until September 1, 1967, and is not retroactive in application.

Levine v. Long Island R. Co.

Court of Appeals of New York

Paramount Film Distrib. Corp. v. State


Claim No. 45976


Court of Appeals of New York


30 N.Y.2d 415; 285 N.E.2d 695; 334 N.Y.S.2d 388; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1235


March 20, 1972, Argued  
June 8, 1972, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Remittitur amended by Paramount Film Distrib. Corp. v. State, 31 N.Y.2d 678, 288 N.E.2d 811, 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1113, 336 N.Y.S.2d 911 (1972)
Remittitur amended by Paramount Film Distr. Corp. v. State, 31 N.Y.2d 710, 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1833 (1972)
Rehearing denied by Paramount Film Distr. Corp. v. State, 31 N.Y.2d 709, 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1814, 337 N.Y.S.2d 1029 (1972)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Paramount Film Distributing Corp. v. New York, 414 U.S. 829, 94 S. Ct. 57, 38 L. Ed. 2d 64, 1973 U.S. LEXIS 389 (1973)



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and case remitted to the Court of Claims for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, a motion picture distributor, sought the recovery of motion picture license fees paid to the state between June 1959 and June 1965, when the applicable statutes were nullified. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) entered an order that affirmed a modified judgment in favor of the distributor. The modification consisted of increasing the award.


OVERVIEW: The distributor paid all but a trivial portion of the motion picture license fees without protest and had not otherwise ever resisted the statutory procedure for licensing or the payment of fees. Nevertheless, when the applicable statutes were nullified, the distributor brought suit. The distributor claimed that it was entitled to recover because the payments of the license fees were involuntarily made under duress. The state asserted that the payments of the license fees were voluntary. The court agreed with the state, holding that the distributor's payments were voluntary because they were made without protest or other action to resist the payments or to recover them. Thus, in the absence of protest at the time, the distributor was not entitled to recover the fees it paid just because in a collateral matter on grounds not applicable to it or ever raised by it, the applicable statutes had been declared null. However, because a small percentage of fees were paid under protest between March and June 1965, the matter was remanded so that such fees might be computed and recovery allowed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remitted the case for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


HEADNOTES

Theatres and shows -- motion pictures -- licenses -- motion picture license fees paid without protest were voluntarily made and may not be recovered -- even if payment were coerced, State was not unjustly enriched and recovery is denied.

1. Motion picture license fees paid to the State without protest from June 10, 1959 to June 10, 1965 (except as to a small percentage paid under protest between March and June, 1965) when the statutes were nullified, were voluntarily made by claimant and may not be recovered. The fees were reasonable tariffs for motion picture licensing and the films were actually licensed

2. Even if the payment of the fees were involuntary or coerced, the State was not unjustly enriched. The State received no benefit. The fees defrayed the cost of the licensing program and did not produce revenue. The funds were disbursed long ago and the State did not act tortiously or fraudulently. The parties being equally innocent, recovery may be denied

 

138 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Palla v. Suffolk County Bd. of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


31 N.Y.2d 36; 286 N.E.2d 247; 334 N.Y.S.2d 860; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1240


March 20, 1972, Argued  
June 7, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: In Matter of Palla: Order affirmed, without costs.

In Matter of Bell and Matter of Gorenberg: Order modified, without costs, and the matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In companion cases, petitioner students sought review of decisions of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second and Fourth Judicial Departments (New York), which reversed the trial court decisions granting relief to the students in their proceedings to compel respondent board of elections to permit them to register to vote, and dismissed petitions which sought a declaration that N.Y. Elec. Law § 151 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The students were summarily denied the right to register to vote because they were residenced at their respective colleges or universities. One set of students filed an action to compel the board of elections to permit their registration. Relief was granted by the trial court, then reversed and remanded by the appellate court for proceedings pursuant to the election law. The other set of students filed actions seeking a declaration that N.Y. Elec. Law § 151 was unconstitutional, which was dismissed by the trial court and affirmed by the appellate court. The court affirmed the appellate court in the first case, finding that the fact of residence was a subject of detailed inquiry which should have only been determine after the court has heard proof. With respect to the second case, the court found that those students should have also been afforded the opportunity to have their registrations challenged as statutorily prescribed, and remanded the case involving the second set of students for proceedings de novo in conformance with election law procedures.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court decision which reversed and remanded the trial court decision granting relief to the students in their proceeding to compel the board of elections to permit them to register to vote. The court modified the appellate court decision which dismissed petitions seeking declaratory relief and remanded them to the trial court for challenge proceedings consistent with the election law.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- residence -- students -- in Palla v. Suffolk County Board of Elections, order of Appellate Division determining that issue of bona fide change in residence of students at university could not be determined by questionnaires but required court to hear proof, including swearing of witnesses and recording of testimony, affirmed -- in Matters of Bell and Gorenberg courts treated proceedings as for declaratory relief only -- petitions included demand for judicial review -- matters remanded to Board of Elections for proceedings de novo -- statutory scheme for determining residence of students not violative of due process and right to equal protection -- Election Law ( § 151), which provides procedure for determining residence of student, codifies traditional criteria for determining residence for voting purposes -- determination of residence is factual -- no special burden imposed on students -- classification reasonable.

1. In Matter of Palla v. Suffolk County Bd. of Elections, petitioners, students at a university, were denied registration by the County Board of Elections on the ground that residence in a university dormitory did not qualify them to vote under sections 150 and 151 of the Election Law. Special Term, on the basis of questionnaires, found that residency was established by the petitioners, other than those who indicated recent registration in other districts. The Appellate Division held that the issue of the bona fide change in residence could not be determined on the basis of the questionnaires but required a determination only after the court has heard proof, which contemplates the swearing of witnesses and recording of their testimony, and remanded the matter for further proceedings. The order appealed from in this proceeding is affirmed.

2. The procedures for challenging a prospective registrant for voting on the basis of residence are prescribed by section 171 of the Election Law and contemplate the use of sworn affidavits ("challenge affidavits"), which are calculated to provide necessary details. Where the sworn answers satisfy a majority of the board of the applicant's right to be registered, they shall register the name as a voter; if not, they are required to point out to the applicant the qualifications which he lacks as a voter. In either event, provisions for a form of expedited judicial review of board determinations are set forth in section 331 of the Election Law. The "challenge affidavit" may be augmented by the use of any reliable device. While a line of inquiry such as that initiated by the questionnaires was authorized, the function of such questionnaires would properly be confined to augmenting the "challenge affidavit", not usurping its role. Since the fact of residence is properly a subject of detailed inquiry, the issues of fact raised by the affidavits, as well as the inferences to be drawn, should be determined only after the court has heard proof.

3. In Matter of Bell some of the petitioners had been refused the right to register, and some who had been previously registered were notified that their right to vote would be challenged on Election Day or that their registrations would be voided because of their residence at college dormitories. In Matter of Gorenberg, a class action on behalf of students at the university, declaratory relief was sought and also an order compelling the Board of Elections to register petitioners and those similarly situated. The courts below elected to treat the proceedings as for declaratory relief and, in upholding the statutory scheme, dismissed the petitions. The petitions included a demand for judicial review of an administrative act as well as a prayer for declaratory relief, and, on that basis alone, the board's determinations should have been annulled and petitioners afforded an opportunity to establish residence. The matters should be remanded to the Board of Elections for proceedings de novo.

4. The statutory scheme is not violative of the due process and equal protection clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment, does not violate the Voting Rights Act (U. S. Code, tit. 42, § 1971), is not contrary to the Twenty-Sixth Amendment, and does not abridge the 18-year-old voting right. The statutory scheme represents, at most, a permissible effort to insure that all applicants for the vote actually fulfill the traditional requirements of bona fide residence.

5. Section 151 of the Election Law provides that for "the purpose of registering and voting no person shall be deemed to have gained or lost a residence by reason of his presence or absence * * * while a student of any institution of learning". Residence, in turn, is defined in terms of domicile and is dependent upon the applicant's expressed intent, his conduct and "all attendant surrounding circumstances". The criteria which the Board of Elections may deem relevant in determining an applicant's residence for voting purpose (cf. subd. [c]) are an elaboration and codification of the criteria which have always been matters under the decisional law of the State properly considered in making a determination of one's residence. In its operation and effects, the statute is entirely neutral. Facts supportive of a student's change of residence for voting purposes, must be independent of his presence at the college for educational purposes. The determination is a factual one, evaluated in terms of actual conduct, having in mind the statutory criteria.

6. Students do not bear such a special burden as to make the procedures under section 151 offensive to the equal protection guarantee. The statute by its terms imposes no voter qualifications and withholds no constitutionally secured right. The classification represents no more than a reasonable effort at assuring that applicants for the vote actually fulfill the requirements of bona fide residence. The statute distinguishes between residents and nonresidents -- not students and other citizens.

7. The Board of Elections erred in summarily denying the petitioners' applications because of their student status. In Gorenberg and Bell the determinations should be annulled and the petitioners afforded the opportunity of having their qualifications challenged as prescribed in section 171 of the Election Law.

 

139 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Robin v. Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


30 N.Y.2d 347; 285 N.E.2d 285; 334 N.Y.S.2d 129; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1262


March 23, 1972, Argued  
June 1, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant village sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's determination that a village ordinance restricting abortion services was valid.


OVERVIEW: Appellant village passed an ordinance that provided that abortions could be performed only in hospitals licensed and accredited by the state. Appellee physician performed abortions in his office and sought a judgment declaring the ordinance unconstitutional and invalid. The village sought an injunction against appellee doctor and appellee family planning center where defendant physician had an office. The trial court found that the ordinance was valid, and it enjoined appellees from violating it. The intermediate court reversed holding that the ordinance was outside the scope of the powers of appellant village. The appellate court affirmed holding that the state had reserved to itself regulation of the performance of abortions.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate division finding appellant village's ordinance regulating abortion invalid was affirmed because the state had reserved to itself regulation of the performance of abortions.


HEADNOTES

Abortion -- local regulation of abortion -- ordinance of Village of Hempstead (Code of Ordinance, ch. 7, art. VI, § 7-90) which provided that abortion should be performed only in hospital duly licensed and accredited under New York State Department of Health and having equipment and facilities acceptable to State Hospital Review and Planning Council invalid -- State has pre-empted subject of abortion legislation -- no clear and explicit authority for local action -- no special conditions present in village as opposed to rest of State warranting enactment of local ordinance.

1. An ordinance of the Village of Hempstead (Code of Ordinances, ch. 7, art. VI, § 7-90) provided that, "Notwithstanding any other law, statute, ordinance or other regulation, 'Justifiable abortional acts', as defined in the Penal Law of the State of New York, shall be performed only in a hospital duly licensed and accredited under the New York State Department of Health, and having equipment and facilities acceptable to the State Hospital Review and Planning Council." The State has pre-empted the subject of abortion legislation so as to prohibit additional regulation by local authorities in the same area.

2. A village or other municipality lacks authority to deal with the matter unless it is specifically empowered so to do in terms clear and explicit. No such clear and explicit authority exists in this case.

3. Local health regulations may be enacted in spite of general State regulation (Village Law, § 89, subd. 59) only if there exists a real distinction between the local area and other parts of the State. There are no special conditions concerning the performance of abortions in the Village of Hempstead -- as opposed to the rest of the State -- which warrant enactment of the local ordinance.

4. The Village of Hempstead lacked the power to enact the ordinance.

 

140 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Golden v. Planning Bd. of Ramapo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


30 N.Y.2d 359; 285 N.E.2d 291; 334 N.Y.S.2d 138; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1325; 63 A.L.R.3d 1157; 2 ELR 20296


November 17, 1972, Argued  
May 3, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed, with costs, and the case remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town boards challenged a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a special term's decision that denied appellee applicants' application for preliminary approval of a residential subdivision plant because of their failure to secure a special permit as required by Ramapo, N.Y., Zoning Ordinance § 46-13.1.


OVERVIEW: The applicants argued that the primary purpose of the zoning ordinance was to control or regulate population growth within the town. In reversing and remitting to the special term for further proceedings, the court held that the ordinance involved proper zoning techniques, exercised for legitimate zoning purposes. According to the court, the ordinance advanced legitimate zoning purposes by assuring that each new home built in the town would have at least a minimum of public services in the categories regulated by the ordinance. Thus, the court found that the challenged ordinance was designed to promote the welfare of the town. The court determined that the existing physical and financial resources of the town were inadequate to furnish the essential services and facilities that a substantial increase in population required. Therefore, a rational basis for phased growth existed. Thus, the court found that the ordinance was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remitted to the special term for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- zoning ordinance which prohibits subdivision approval except where stated facilities or services are available, not unconstitutional -- enabling legislation (Town Law, §§ 261, 263) sufficiently broad -- purpose of ordinance is orderly growth, not exclusion -- subdivision rights are conditioned, not denied -- diminution in value of land not confiscation -- other appropriate uses permitted -- justiciable controversy presented.

1. Section 46-13.1 of the Town zoning ordinance prohibits subdivision approval except where the residential developer has secured, prior to the application for plat approval, a special permit or a variance. The standards for the issuance of special permits require the availability to the proposed subdivision plat of five facilities or services: (a) public sanitary sewers or approved substitutes; (b) drainage facilities; (c) improved public parks or recreation facilities, including public schools; (d) State, county or town roads -- major, secondary or collector; and (e) firehouses. No special permit may issue unless the proposed residential development has accumulated 15 development points, to be computed on a sliding scale of values assigned to the specified improvements. The purpose is to phase residential development to the Town's ability to provide the above facilities or services. The board may issue special permits vesting a present right to proceed with residential development in such year as the development meets the required point minimum, but in no event later than the final year of the 18-year capital plan. A prospective developer may advance the date of subdivision approval by agreeing to provide the necessary improvements. Meanwhile, applications are authorized for a reduction of the assessed valuation. The effect of these integrated efforts in land use planning and development is to provide an over-all program of orderly growth and adequate facilities through a sequential development policy commensurate with progressing availability and capacity of public facilities. The ordinance is constitutional.

2. In enacting the challenged amendments, the Town Board has sought to control subdivision in all residential districts, pending provision of essential services and facilities. Though zoning may not be invoked to further the general police powers of a municipality ( Westwood Forest Estates v. Village of South Nyack, 23 N Y 2d 424), the challenged amendments are within the municipality's delegated authority to determine the lines along which development shall proceed, though it diverts that growth from its natural course. Considered in terms of the enabling legislation, the amendments constitute proper zoning techniques, exercised for legitimate zoning purposes (Town Law, §§ 261, 263).

3. The Planning Board has the right to refuse approval of subdivision plats absent improvements specified in Town Law ( § 277), and the fact that it is the Town and not the subdividing owner who is required to provide improvements does not transform the scheme into an absolute prohibition. Denial of subdivision plat approval invariably amounts to a prohibition against subdivision, albeit a conditional one (Real Property Law, §§ 334-a, 335). Whether it is the municipality or the developer who provides the improvements, the objective is the same; and, in either case, subdivision rights are conditioned, not denied.

4. The power to zone under current law is vested in local municipalities and the issues presented framed in terms of the parties' due process rights. The general public interest requires, however, that those rights not be adjudicated independently of the rights of others "in search of a more comfortable place to live." Measured under this standard, the ordinance nevertheless represents a first practical step, achieved without forsaking paramount social goals.

5. Land use and development regulations are designed to determine the course of growth within a particular town or district. Where those restrictions upon the beneficial use and enjoyment of land are necessary to promote the ultimate good of the community, and are within the bounds of reason, they will be sustained. Nevertheless, zoning only directs growth; it cannot be used to further local efforts at exclusion.

6. The timing devices presently at issue have their purpose in preventing premature subdivision absent essential municipal services and to insure continuous development commensurate with the Town's obligation to provide such services. Unlike proscribed exclusionary tactics, they do not freeze population at present levels, but seek to maximize growth and the efficient utilization of land.

7. These restrictions, though substantial in nature and duration, are not absolute; and the restricted parcels may be put to profitable use within a reasonable time. The amendments contemplate a definite term and in the interim the Town is committed to the construction and installation of necessary improvements.

8. The burden of holding restricted property is an ordinary incident of police power regulation and the fact that the restriction may result in a depreciation in market value does not render the underlying scheme unconstitutional, absent a showing that the measure is unreasonable in terms of its necessity or the diminution in value is tantamount to a confiscation. Diminution is a relative concept and, though its magnitude is an indicia of a taking, it does not, of itself, constitute a taking.

9. Assuming that the restrictions here will remain in force for the life of the program, up to 18 years in certain areas, they do not amount to a confiscation. In the interim, the landowner is not deprived of either the best use of his land or other appropriate uses, still permitted in the various residential districts, including the construction of a single family residence.

10. The challenged amendments propose restrictions of a certain duration and founded upon estimate determined by fact. The inference is permissible that within a reasonable time the subject property will be put to the desired use at an appreciated value. Taken as a whole, then, the proposed restraints, mitigated by the prospect of appreciated value and interim reductions in assessed value, are within the limits of necessity and should be sustained.

11. The prescription for securing a special permit under the ordinance is mandatory, and were the scheme held unconstitutional, the ordinance could admit of no constitutionally permissible construction so as to require initial administrative relief to determine whether injury had occurred. The attack by the subdividing owners is directed against the ordinance in its entirety, and the thrust of the petition and complaint is that the amendments operate to destroy the marketability of the subject parcels for residential use and thus constitutes a present invasion of the property rights of the complaining owners. The alleged harm is thus immediate and is sufficient to raise a justicable issue.

 

141 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Finger Lakes Racing Ass'n v. N.Y. State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Comm'n


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


30 N.Y.2d 207; 282 N.E.2d 592; 331 N.Y.S.2d 625; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1394


February 7, 1972, Argued  
March 23, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff racing associations appealed a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York) finding for defendant New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Commission and sustaining the constitutionality of defendant and the New York City Off-Track Betting Corporation Law, 1970 N.Y. Laws, ch. 143, 144, 145. The court found that the legislation was constitutional under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 9.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff racing associations challenged the constitutionality of defendant New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Commission and the New York City Off-Track Betting Corporation Law, 1970 N.Y. Laws, ch. 143, 144, 145. Plaintiffs claimed that their property and financial interests in the gambling and horse-racing business were adversely affected by the off-track betting system. The court affirmed the lower court's decision finding that both defendant and the statute were constitutional pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. I, § 9, noting that a state was empowered to collect revenue and that the statute fairly compensated racetrack owners for the use of their facilities. The part of the net revenues, which went to the participating municipalities was so closely tied to the interrelated financial dependence of the local governments on the state that the fiscal assistance through defendant was revenue for the support of the state government.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, finding that the statute and defendant New York State Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Commission were lawful. The court noted that the State of New York was authorized to collect reasonable revenue and found that the statute in question fairly compensated racetrack owners for the use of their facilities.


HEADNOTES

Horse racing -- off-track pari-mutuel betting -- Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law (§ 69-c), which imposes tax on off-track pari-mutuel bets, provides "reasonable revenue" for support of government within language of State Constitution (art. I, § 9) -- revenues for support of municipal governments are for support of "government" within constitutional language -- Governor's certificate complied with constitutional provision for shortening time to permit consideration of bills (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 14) -- off-track betting statutes do not deprive plaintiffs of property without due process of law.

1. Chapter 143 of the Laws of 1970 established the Off-Track Pari-Mutuel Betting Commission and chapter 144 created the New York City Off-Track Betting Corporation, and both were authorized to provide for off-track pari-mutuel betting. From an absolute constitutional prohibition on gambling (art. I, § 9), a specific exception was made for "pari-mutuel betting on horse races as may be prescribed by the legislature and from which the state shall derive a reasonable revenue for the support of government". The Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law (§ 69-c) imposes a tax of one half of 1% on all off-track pari-mutuel bets. After operating expenses of the public benefit corporation conducting the system have been paid, 80% of the net revenues, up to $ 200 million, go to the participating municipality, and 20% to the State. If they exceed $ 200 million, the State and the participating municipality each receives half (§ 69-c, subd. 4). This distribution is a "reasonable revenue" for the support of government within the constitutional language. ( Saratoga Harness Racing Assn. v. Agriculture & N. Y. State Horse Breeding Development Fund, 22 N Y 2d 119, decisive.)

2. Revenues which go to the support of municipal governments are for the support of "government" within the constitutional language. Fiscal assistance through a State agency to the political subdivisions of the State must be regarded as revenue for the support of State government.

3. Article III ( § 14) of the State Constitution postpones the enactment of bills for at least three days after copies are in final printed form on legislative desks. That time lapse may be cut down if the Governor certifies to the Legislature "the facts which in his opinion necessitate an immediate vote thereon". In view of the imminence of adjournment, the Governor's certificate of April 19, 1970 complied with the constitutional requirement. The request by the leaders of both houses to the Governor for a certificate shortening the time to permit consideration of the bill before adjournment was to make legislative action possible. This is a compliance in terms and in spirit with article III (§ 14).

4. The off-track betting statutes do not deprive plaintiffs of property without due process of law. A gambling enterprise is prohibited by article I ( § 9) of the State Constitution and operation of the gambling part of plaintiffs' business depends on legislative permission. This permission may be conditioned and the conditions altered from time to time according to the legislative view of fairness. The Legislature should be able to reapportion the profit results of the gambling business, including the allocation of a greater share for public benefit.

 

142 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Oliver v. Postel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


30 N.Y.2d 171; 282 N.E.2d 306; 331 N.Y.S.2d 407; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1408; 1 Media L. Rep. 2399


January 3, 1972, Argued  
March 22, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Ordered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant newspapermen sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York), which dismissed their petition that sought to direct respondent judge to open the courtroom in a criminal prosecution to the public and press.


OVERVIEW: The judge closed his courtroom to the newspapermen upon a motion by the defendant due to news articles that revealed prior criminal conduct of the defendant. The newspapermen challenged the dismissal of their petition and argued that the judge's order violated the First Amendment's guarantees of freedom of speech and of the press, and the Sixth Amendment guarantee of a public trial. The court determined that the judge's order closing the courtroom was aimed specifically at the news media and was intended to punish the newspapermen for what the judge characterized as "contumacious conduct" in disregarding his prior admonitions. The court held that the judge's threat of contempt and his order closing the trial because of the newspapermen's conduct had a deterrent effect on free discussion by the press. The court concluded that the judge's order was an unwarranted effort to punish and censor the press. The court held that the questions on appeal became moot when the trial ended and the requested relief would serve no purpose. The court ordered that the lower court judgment be modified to reflect that the petition was dismissed as moot.


OUTCOME: The court ordered the judgment modified to reflect that the newspapermen's petition was dismissed as moot.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- public trial -- constitutional law -- freedom of press -- because of disclosures in public press, and editorials critical of trial court for threatening contempt on newsmen, trial court granted motion of defense to exclude public and press from trial; newsmen were targets of order and have standing to challenge order of exclusion -- order had effect of denying freedom of press; no showing that order was needed to protect integrity of trial -- acquittal rendered questions academic but questions presented are of sufficient importance and interest to justify entertaining appeal -- order punished and censored press and would otherwise be reversed.

1. In articles about the defendant in a criminal action, newspapers reported that he had a criminal record and was reputed to have underworld connections. The trial judge, respondent herein, stated that the articles were unfair and, after polling the jury and finding that no juror had read the articles, denied the motion by the defense for a mistrial but warned the news media that he would hold any reporter in contempt who reported anything other than what traspired in the courtroom. When, thereafter, articles and editorials were published in newspapers critical of respondent's threats of contempt, respondent directed that the courtroom be closed to the press and public. Since petitioners were the direct targets of respondent's order, and their ability to comment upon the trial as professional journalists was thereby impaired, they have the requisite personal stake in the outcome of the controversy to give them standing to challenge the validity of that order.

2. The contempt power may not be utilized to impose punishment for the publication of out-of-court statements relating to a pending court proceeding, except where such statements are shown to present a clear and present danger or a serious and imminent threat to the administration of justice. No such showing appears in this record.

3. While respondent did not hold any reporters in contempt, his threat of contempt, followed by his condemnation of the subsequent newspaper articles and editorials as "contumacious conduct" and his order closing the trial because of such conduct had a deterrent effect on free discussion by the press.

4. Moreover, there was no support for respondent's conclusion that defendant could obtain a fair trial only if the courtroom was closed to the public and press.

5. Although termination of the trial rendered the appeal academic and moot, the questions presented are of sufficient importance and interest to justify entertaining it.

6. The conclusion that respondent's order was an unwarranted effort to punish and censor the press would ordinarily lead to a reversal. However, since the defendant has been acquitted and the questions presented became academic, the order is modified to the extent of dismissing the petition but only for that reason.

Kranker v. Levitt

146 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Dickens v. Ernesto


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


30 N.Y.2d 61; 281 N.E.2d 153; 330 N.Y.S.2d 346; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1497


January 10, 1972, Argued  
February 16, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adoptive parents sought review of a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment that denied the adoptive parents' N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 petition for a determination that appellee department's denial of their application for adoption on the ground that they did not have a religious affiliation violated their constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: The adoptive parents' application for adoption was denied by the department because they lacked a religious affiliation. The adoptive parents brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding against the department for a determination that the religious affiliation requirements were unconstitutional. The trial court found no constitutional violation but ordered the department to process the adoptive parents' application. The judgment was affirmed and the adoptive parents sought further review. The court affirmed the judgment and held that: (1) the religious affiliation requirements of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 116, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 373, N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 113, conformed to N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 32, which required placement, when practicable, with persons of the same religious persuasion as the child; (2) the trial court had discretion to approve persons of a different faith in exceptional situations; and (3) the statutes, in their direction that religious "matching" be consistent with the best interests of the child, was neutral as to religion and did not violate the adoptive parents' First Amendment right to the "free exercise of religion" or deny them equal protection of the laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that requirements that children be adopted by persons of the same religion as the child were constitutional and directed the department to process the adoptive parents' application for adoption, without costs.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- free exercise of religion -- adoption -- article VI (§ 32) of New York Constitution and statutes adopted in conformity therewith, requiring that child be placed for adoption "when practicable" and "consistent with the best interests of the child" with persons of same religious persuasion as child, are not unconstitutional -- legislation does not constitute establishment of religion and does not deny petitioners, whom the lower courts permitted to apply for adoption, right to free exercise of religion or equal protection of laws.

1. Article VI (§ 32) of New York's Constitution requires that a child shall be placed for adoption, when practicable, with persons of the same religious persuasion as the child. The statutes, requiring religious conformity of child and adoptive parents "when practicable" ( Family Ct. Act, § 116, subd. [g]; Social Services Law § 373, subd. 7; see, also, Domestic Relations Law, § 113), were broadened to provide that such religious matching should be "consistent with the best interests of the child" (L. 1970, ch. 494). Under the statutes, religion is one of many factors in the placement of a child for adoption, and placement in conformity with the religious wishes of the parents of the child, though desirable, is not mandatory. The challenged legislation places primary emphasis on the temporal best interests of the child. These religious affiliation requirements of the statutes enacted in conformity with article VI ( § 32) of the State Constitution are valid under the United States Constitution.

2. Under the criteria which have developed to determine when there is a violation of the Establishment Clause, the constitutional provision and the statutes do not constitute the estabilshment of religion. The matching provisions fulfill a "secular legislative purpose" and reflect and preserve a "benevolent neutrality" toward religion. They do not have the primary effect of advancing or inhibiting religion or of fostering an "excessive government entanglement" with church interests.

3. Subdivision (e) of section 116 of the Family Court Act which defines the words "when practicable" does not support petitioners' claim of unconstitutionality. In applying subdivision (e) in the light of the provisions of subdivision (g), the court retains discretion to determine whether a particular placement is in the best interests of the child.

4. There is no basis for the claim of the petitioners, to whom the courts below granted permission to file an application as adoptive parents, that the challenged legislation denies them the right to the free exercise of religion or creates an arbitrary classification which denies them equal protection of the laws.

Eastman Kodak Co. v. State Tax Comm'n


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

New York v. Wyman

149 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York
