150 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gold v. Lomenzo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


29 N.Y.2d 468; 280 N.E.2d 640; 329 N.Y.S.2d 805; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1533


November 18, 1971, Argued  
February 9, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner real estate broker challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed his N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to vacate respondent secretary of state's order suspending the real estate broker's license. The real estate broker challenged the wording of N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 441-c as unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: The real estate broker's license was suspended based on charges filed by clients who alleged that the real estate broker had exacted finders' fees that were excessive. The secretary of state found that the real estate broker had demonstrated untrustworthiness within the meaning of N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 441-c. The real estate broker initiated a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding to vacate the suspension and to challenge the constitutionality of N.Y. Real Prop. Law § 441-c as vague and indefinite. The appellate court dismissed the proceeding as academic because the real estate broker's appeal of his suspension was superceded by a subsequent order of revocation of his license. The court held that it was error for the appellate court to dismiss the real estate broker's appeal as academic where there were questions presented that were of general interest, substantial public importance, and likely to arise with frequency. The court held that § 441-c was not unconstitutionally vague, that there was substantial evidence to reasonably conclude that the real estate broker demonstrated untrustworthiness, and that the secretary of state properly disciplined him by suspending his license.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing the real estate broker's appeal was modified to the extent of deleting the condition imposed by the secretary of state that limited fees the real estate broker could charge in future transactions.


HEADNOTES

Brokers -- real estate brokers -- although CPLR article 78 proceeding sought review of suspension of petitioner's real estate broker's license only despite later revocation of his license, both orders are intertwined and, because of public importance, will both be reviewed -- proceeding is treated as action for declaratory judgment in order to determine issue of constitutionality -- section 441-c of Real Property Law is constitutional and word "untrustworthiness" therein is not vague --  [**2] petitioner was properly found to have demonstrated untrustworthiness for charging excess brokerage fees on apartment rentals -- imposition of condition that petitioner return excess fees as part of punishment on finding of untrustworthy conduct was valid exercise of respondents' power -- as to fourth client who forfeited, because of clause in lease, $ 311.85 representing security, rent and commission although she never took apartment, since respondents had approved such clause, it was improper to penalize petitioner for using clause and to compel full reimbursement; partial reimbursement of excess commission should have been ordered -- imposition of condition that future fees be limited to one month's rent was arbitrary and confiscatory.

1. A proceeding under CPLR article 78 was commenced to review the earlier suspension of petitioner real estate broker's license but not the revocation of petitioner's license which had been made later and 18 days before this proceeding's commencement. The revocation and suspension orders were intertwined to such a degree so as to preclude adoption of a conclusion that the revocation order rendered the questions raised in this article 78 proceeding [**3] academic. In any event, a matter, although settled as between the parties, will not be considered academic when the underlying questions are of general interest, substantial public importance and likely to arise with frequency. The questions presented in this appeal, particularly those which relate to the propriety of the conditions which respondents imposed in the suspension order, fall into this category and, it was error for the Appellate Division to dismiss the appeal.

2. Article 78 proceedings are inappropriate vehicles to test constitutionality and the proper remedy is an action for a declaratory judgment. Since all necessary parties are before the court, the article 78 proceeding may be treated as an action for a declaratory judgment and the issue determined.

3. Section 441-c of the Real Property Law is not unconstitutional. The words "demonstrated untrustworthiness" are not vague. The term is sufficiently certain to real estate brokers to apprise them of the scope of permissible activities and it is the standards of that calling which serve as the guide for their behavior.

4. Petitioner was properly found to have demonstrated untrustworthiness for his deviations from [**4] these professional norms. Evidence introduced at the hearing indicated that petitioner's fees were far in excess of what the local real estate board considered usual and he was unable to explain what special services were rendered to earn these fees. Additionally, it was revealed that petitioner had no established formula or basis for the computation of commissions. Although neither the real estate board nor respondents had authority to fix fees, the amount of the fees and their relevance to actual services were matters which were properly considered by respondents in evaluating the complaints filed against petitioner. It was therefore proper to discipline petitioner by suspending his license.

5. Where a broker is disciplined because he demonstrated untrustworthy conduct by charging excessive fees which bear no relation to legitimate services, respondents may legitimately condition the suspension on the return of those excessive fees. The reimbursement ordered in the case of three clients who complained was a valid exercise of respondents' powers, and petitioner has failed to effectively demonstrate any error in the manner in which they were imposed or in their amount.

6. In [**5] the case of a fourth client who complained, the client never took an apartment because it was unavailable on the date promised but nevertheless was forced to forfeit $ 311.85 representing the security, rent and commission because a clause in the lease provided that no refunds would be made if the apartment were available within 30 days of the date promised. Since respondents had specifically approved this 30-day provision a few months previously, it was improper to penalize petitioner for using this clause and to compel reimbursement of the full sum paid. However, in view of the finding that the commission exceeded the guidelines established by the Real Estate Board by some $ 86.98 and does not reflect unusual services rendered, a refund in that amount should have been ordered in the same manner as in the other transactions.

7. The imposition of an additional condition that in the future fees be limited to one month's rent was arbitrary. Inasmuch as there is no statute authorizing the fixing of fees, brokers are entitled to commissions for legitimate services. To arbitrarily limit future fees was confiscatory and placed petitioner in an uncompetitive position in the market place [**6] as it would deprive him of compensation in situations where his services exceeded the norm.

 

151 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fhagen v. Miller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


29 N.Y.2d 348; 278 N.E.2d 615; 328 N.Y.S.2d 393; 1972 N.Y. LEXIS 1581


October 19, 1971, Argued  
January 6, 1972, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former mental patients challenged the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed the action for a declaratory judgment brought by the patients, in favor of defendants, state and Commissioner of Mental Hygiene.


OVERVIEW: The patients filed an action against defendants, which sought to have N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 72, 78, declared unconstitutional under the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. The trial court rejected the patients' arguments, decided that the provisions were constitutional, and dismissed the complaint. The appellate court modified the judgment by striking the paragraph that dismissed the complaint and substituting a provision that declared the sections in question valid. The patients appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court's ruling. The court held that N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law § 78 was constitutional. Section 78 required an examination by a staff physician, immediate notification of the patient's rights, and that any demand for release was considered as a habeas corpus relief. Section 72 afforded the same rights as § 78 and required that three physicians find the patient afflicted with mental disease to such an extent that his welfare or the welfare of others required admission.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court, which modified the trial court's ruling and found the statute unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- admission to mental hospital -- section 78 of Mental Hygiene Law, which provides for involuntary admission to hospital of person alleged to be in need of immediate observation,  [**2] care or treatment for mental illness, and section 72, which provides for admission of one alleged to be mentally ill only after certificate of two examining physicians accompanied by application from relative, friend or specified individual, do not constitute denial of due process -- ample opportunity afforded to litigate question of mental illness and proceedings for confinement.

1. Section 78 of the Mental Hygiene Law, which provides for the involuntary admission to an approved hospital, for no longer than 30 days (shortened by regulation [14 NYCRR 62.2] to 15 days) of any person who is "alleged to be in need of immediate observation, care or treatment for mental illness" does not constitute a denial of due process. Where immediate action is necessary for the protection of society and for the welfare of an allegedly mentally ill person, a person is not unconstitutionally deprived of his liberty so long as he is afforded an opportunity within a reasonable period to litigate the question of his mental illness and the proceedings by which he was confined. That opportunity is amply afforded by the statute. One need not be violent or dangerous to be constitutionally confined [**3] for a short period prior to notice and an opportunity to be heard.

2. Section 72 of the Mental Hygiene Law, which provides for admission of a person "alleged to be mentally ill and suitable for care and treatment" but also provides that admission can be had only on a certificate of two examining physicians accompanied by an application, made 10 days in advance of admission, from a relative, friend or specified official is not unconstitutional. The procedure under that section is such that a mentally ill person, so committed and hospitalized, may not be said to have been deprived of his liberty without due process of law.

Kelly v. Catherwood

153 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Samuel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


29 N.Y.2d 252; 277 N.E.2d 381; 327 N.Y.S.2d 321; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 967


October 19, 1971, Argued  
November 17, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant drivers were charged by respondent state with leaving the scene of an accident without making the requisite report under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 600, in three separate cases. The Appellate Term of the Supreme Court (New York) affirmed. The drivers appealed.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 600 required a motor vehicle operator involved in an accident to remain at the scene, identify himself, and report the accident to the police. The drivers were convicted of the misdemeanor violation of § 600 and each attacked the constitutionality of the statute on the ground that the statute invaded his federal and state privilege against self incrimination. On appeal the court held that: (1) the purpose of § 600 was to regulate a lawful activity in the interest of public safety, (2) the self-reporting requirement was constitutional if its tendency to incriminate was only incidental and minimal, (3) leaving the scene of an accident was a true and substantial independent wrong meriting treatment as a separate criminal offense, (4) the self-reporting requirement under § 600 was a valid exercise of the state's regulatory power and thus was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the drivers' convictions for misdemeanor violations of the state's vehicle accident reporting law.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- leaving scene of accident -- constitutional law -- section 600 of Vehicle and Traffic Law which requires motor vehicle operator involved in accident, whether culpable or not, to remain at scene of accident, identify himself and report accident to police is valid -- duties imposed by statute are reasonable exercises of police power -- statute is constitutional where purpose is regulation of lawful activity and incidental effect is occasionally to inculpate.

1. Section 600 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law which requires a motor vehicle operator involved in an accident, whether culpable or not, to remain at the scene of the accident, identify himself, and report the accident [**3] to the police is valid. The duties imposed by it are reasonable exercises of police power by the State to regulate activities directly relevant to public safety.

2. The rationale in People v. Rosenheimer (209 N. Y. 115), in which the validity of the predecessor statute was declared in 1913, that the operation of a motor vehicle was a privilege, and that acceptance of the privilege constituted a waiver of constitutional rights, including that of the privilege against self incrimination, is no longer acceptable.

3. If, as is the case with section 600 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, its purpose is the regulation of lawful activity to protect the public from significant harm and the incidental effect is occasionally to inculpate, the statute is within constitutional limitations.

 

154 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Corning v. Donohue


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


29 N.Y.2d 209; 275 N.E.2d 579; 325 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1002


October 7, 1971, Argued  
October 21, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual sought review of a decision of Appellate Division of Supreme Court in Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order declaring ch. 462 of the Laws of 1970 inapplicable to City of Albany, declaring subdiv. 9 of § 2553 of Education Law unconstitutional, and restraining respondent board of elections from accepting petitions to nominate candidates to the Board of Education and from placing them on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: The issue in this case was whether chapter 462 of the Laws of 1970, which amended article 52 of the Education Law under New York law, was applicable to the city of Albany after July 1, 1971. The court found that consideration of the legislative history confirmed that the law was applicable to the city of Albany. The court found that the legislature demonstrated that its intent was not to divide the cities on the basis of population but, rather, to separate out article 52's six enumerated cities from the remaining cities of the state in order to accord them special treatment under the Education Law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate division.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- appointed or elected  [**2] Boards of Education -- Education Law (art. 52) applied to six named cities, including Albany -- article 51 applied to school districts of cities having less than 125,000 inhabitants -- chapter 462 of Laws of 1970 provided that first election of members of Board of Education for Albany should take place in November, 1971 -- chapter 492 of Laws of 1970, effective three days thereafter, provided that, where city had less than 125,000 inhabitants in 1970, its school district should be governed by article 52 until July 1, 1971 -- although Albany's population dropped below 125,000 article 52 remained applicable -- chapter 462 of Laws of 1970 not unconstitutional.

1. Section 2550 of the Education Law, contained in article 52 of the Education Law, declares that "This article shall apply to the school districts of the following cities only: New York, Buffalo, Rochester, Syracuse, Yonkers and Albany." Article 51 applies to all other school districts of cities which in 1950 had a population of less than 125,000 inhabitants and to those school districts of cities incorporated thereafter. By chapter 462 of the Laws of 1970, section 2553 of the Education Law, which is contained in article 52 [**3] of the Education Law, was amended to make the Albany Board of Education elective, subject to a permissive referendum to be held at the general election in November of 1970. The referendum was held and the voters approved the elective board. Chapter 462 also provided that the first election of members of the board was to take place at the general election in November, 1971. Chapter 492 of the Laws of 1970 became effective three days after chapter 462. It provided that, in the case of a city which had a population of 125,000 inhabitants or more by the 1960 census and less than 125,000 by the 1970 census, the school district of that city should be governed by the provisions of article 52 until July 1, 1971. Albany's population, according to the 1970 census, dropped below 125,000 inhabitants. Chapter 462 of the Laws of 1970 is applicable to the City of Albany after July 1, 1971. The Legislature has indicated its intent that Albany be governed by the provisions of article 52 of the Education Law, regardless of any change in its population. It is unreasonable to suppose that chapter 492, which became effective three days after chapter 462, repealed chapter 462. Two classes of cities [**4] are provided for, one class consisting of six cities including Albany, and the other consisting of all the other cities. The Legislature demonstrated its intent to accord special treatment under the Education Law to the six enumerated cities.

2. Chapter 462 is not unconstitutional. It does not violate section 17 of article III of the State Constitution.

3. The question whether chapter 462 violates section 4 of article VIII of the Constitution (dealing with a city's taxing and debt contracting powers) is not presented since no issue relating to Albany's taxing and debt contracting powers is involved.

156 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Spence-Chapin Adoption Service v. Polk


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


29 N.Y.2d 196; 274 N.E.2d 431; 324 N.Y.S.2d 937; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1074


September 7, 1971, Argued  
September 23, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a child custody action appellants, foster parents and an adoption service, sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a trial court's order dismissing writs of habeas corpus filed by appellees, a mother and the New York Department of Social Services (Department). The appellate court sustained the writs and directed the return of the child.


OVERVIEW: The foster parents contended that they were entitled to custody of the child because they could provide better care for her. The court disagreed and affirmed the appellate court's decision, holding that the appellate court correctly determined it lacked power, absent abandonment of the child, statutory surrender outstanding, or the established unfitness of the mother, to deprive the mother of custody. The court held that since none of these factors was present the natural mother was entitled to obtain the custody of her child, and the child should have been returned to her mother. The court reasoned that the burden rested, not upon the mother to show that the child's welfare would have been advanced by being returned to her, but rather upon the foster parents and the adoption service to prove that the mother was unfit to have her child and that the latter's well-being required separation from her mother. The court determined that the foster parents were merely a temporary parent substitute and were not entitled to assert any further rights with regard to the child.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court was affirmed, without costs.


HEADNOTES

Parent and child -- custody -- foster care -- natural mother has right to custody of child, in absence of abandonment or outstanding formal surrender of child by her, or demonstrated unfitness -- couple with whom agency placed child for foster care, not adoption, were properly directed to return her to agency for return to mother.

1. A natural mother has the right to the custody of her child, in the absence of abandonment or an outstanding formal surrender of the child by her, or demonstrated unfitness. Accordingly,  [**2] in a case where the natural mother of a child gave her to the New York City Commissioner of Social Services for temporary care, not for adoption, and the agency to whom he delegated the custody of the child placed her with a couple for compensated foster care, to be returned on demand, the latter were properly directed to return her to the agency for return of custody to the natural mother.

2. Although the mother had executed a statutory written surrender of custody and authorized the adoption of the child, the child was never placed for adoption and, when the mother demanded her return, the Commissioner, joined by the agency, consented to such return, thus nullifying the surrender, as if it had never been.

3. The fact that the mother may have made inadequate plans for the child's upbringing does not demonstrate unfitness, and there has been no serious effort to assert abandonment of the child by her.

 

157 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Parrino v. Lindsay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


29 N.Y.2d 30; 272 N.E.2d 67; 323 N.Y.S.2d 689; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1097


June 10, 1971, Argued  
July 7, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, landlord and a membership corporation, appealed from an order of the New York County Supreme Court, which found certain rent control laws for the elderly were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: In response to a housing shortage, New York City issued certain local laws governing the amount of rent that could be charged the elderly poor. The landlord and a membership corporation brought suit seeking to strike the legislation. They argued that the legislation resulting in a taking of property without compensation and that it was violative of the equal protection laws. The trial court disagreed and upheld the legislation. The court affirmed. The exemptions from the elderly poor did not render an impermissible taking of property or a denial of due process. Additionally, the legislation did not deprive plaintiffs of the equal protection of the laws. The legislation had a substantial relation to a legitimate public purpose, namely, the prevention of sever hardship to aging needy citizens resulting from the shortage of low and moderately priced housing accommodations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that found certain rent control laws for the elderly were constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- rent regulation -- New York City's Local Law No. 31 of 1970 (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-5.0, subd. n), which exempts from rent increases provided by New York City's Local Law No. 30 of 1970 (§ Y51-5.0, subd. a, par. [2]) any tenant 62 years of age or older, not recipient of public assistance, with limited income, and which applies from August 1, 1970 to January 1, 1972, is valid exercise of police power -- exemption does not amount to taking of property without compensation -- legislation does not reduce rent but merely prohibits certain increases -- no denial of equal protection of law.

1. New York City's Local Law No. 30 of 1970 provided [**2] for general increases in maximum rents (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y51-5.0, subd. a, par. [2]). Local Law No. 31 of 1970 (§ Y51-5.0, subd. n) exempts from such rent increases any tenant 62 years of age or older, not the recipient of public assistance, whose total annual income -- when added to that of other members of the household -- does not exceed $ 4,500, after taxes, and who pays more than one third of such income for rent. Special Term, noting that it "only affects rent increases granted on or after Aug. 1, 1970" and "is due to expire on Jan. 1, 1972", properly upheld the legislation as a valid exercise of the police power.

2. Plaintiffs are in error in arguing that the exemption amounts to a taking of property without compensation in that it "effects a reduction in the rent of certain tenants at the expense of their landlords". Local Law No. 31 calls for no reduction; it merely provides that, for a limited period, elderly tenants are to be exempt from certain rent increases. They will be required to pay the same rent as before, established pursuant to the pre-1970 legislation which has been upheld as valid.

3. Local Law No. 31 does not deprive the [**3] plaintiffs of equal protection of the laws. The classification has a reasonable basis; it has a substantial relation to a legitimate public purpose -- the prevention of severe hardship to aging needy citizens resulting from the shortage of low and moderately priced housing accommodations.

 

158 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Feinlowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


29 N.Y.2d 176; 272 N.E.2d 561; 324 N.Y.S.2d 62; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1104


April 21, 1971, Argued  
July 7, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was granted permission to appeal from a judgment of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court for the Ninth and Tenth Judicial Districts in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of a city court convicting defendant of bookmaking and possession of bookmaking records. The issue was whether certain recordings of defendant's voice as a result of a wiretap pursuant to court order were admissible.


OVERVIEW: The central issue was whether recordings of defendant's voice obtained as a result of a court-ordered wiretap were properly admitted at trial. The police secured a court order in 1966 authorizing them to intercept and record conversations over a telephone line they suspected defendant was using for bookmaking. A tape recording of his telephone conversation was introduced over his objection at trial. Defendant argued on appeal that the evidence obtained by wiretapping was constitutionally inadmissible. He also urged that the order authorizing the telephone interception violated § 605 of the Federal Communications Act. The court affirmed, holding that the seizure was not unconstitutional by 1966 standards and thus the recordings were properly allowed into evidence. There was probable cause for the wiretap, and the affidavit in support of the court order specifically described defendant and his suspected gambling activities, including the specific location to be observed. Any deviation from constitutional standards announced after 1966 was held to be harmless in light of the overwhelming evidence against defendant. Neither defendant's constitutional or statutory rights were violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for bookmaking and possession of bookmaking records.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- wiretapping -- tape recordings of conversations on public telephone made on June 15, 1966 were properly admitted on defendant's later trial which resulted in conviction for bookmaking, despite invalidation of rule of Olmstead v. United States (277 U.S. 438) by Katz v. United States (389 U.S. 347) -- wiretap was admissible in State criminal proceeding, despite Lee v. Florida (392 U.S. 378), by amendment of section 605 of Communications Act of 1934 by Omnibus Crime Control and Safe Streets Act of 1968 -- seizures here were made in substantial compliance with provisions of Safe Streets Act.

1. Tape recordings of conversations on a public telephone wherein defendant took several bets, made on June 15, 1966, although [**2] defendant was convicted of bookmaking and possession of bookmaking records on July 31, 1969, were properly admitted in evidence. At the time the tape recordings of the conversations were made the rule of Olmstead v. United States (277 U.S. 438) was applicable holding that a wiretap, conducted without a physical intrusion into defendant's premises, was not an unlawful search and seizure within the meaning of the Fourth Amendment. At the time of the trial, Katz v. United States (389 U.S. 347) invalidated the Olmstead rule and prohibited the use of such evidence. Since the evidence obtained by the wiretap was admissible when the wiretap was made, the recordings were properly allowed in evidence in the later trial.

2. Section 605 of the Communications Act of 1934 prohibits all tapping of telephone conversations. Evidence seized in violation of section 605 has been held inadmissible in Federal court but admissible in a State criminal proceeding. In Lee v. Florida (392 U.S. 378), decided on June 17, 1968, evidence seized in violation of section 605 was held inadmissible in State criminal proceedings. However, on June 19, 1968, Congress enacted the Omnibus [**3] Crime Control and Safe Streets Act of 1968 wherein it amended section 605 and authorized wiretapping. Under such amendment, the fruits of court authorized wiretaps are admissible in State courts.

3. In seizures made before the effective date of the Safe Streets Act, substantial compliance with its provisions is all that is required to bring the evidence within the newly-enacted exception in section 605. Substantial compliance was present here. The wiretap was obtained under judicial supervision, in full compliance with section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, with probable cause for the seizure clearly indicated. There is no valid reason to exclude such evidence especially since the police acted in full compliance with both constitutional law and the statutory law of our State as it existed at that time.

 

159 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Transp. Authority v. County of Nassau


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


28 N.Y.2d 385; 271 N.E.2d 213; 322 N.Y.S.2d 228; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1244


April 22, 1971, Submitted  
May 26, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3222(1), entered an order declaring that plaintiff Metropolitan Transportation Authority (MTA) acted within the scope of its authority under N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1277 in determining and certifying the costs of operating and maintaining the stations in the county.


OVERVIEW: The MTA determined and certified to the county the costs for operating, maintaining, and using the railroad stations in the county. The MTA brought an action against the county. The appellate division reviewed the submission of controversy upon an agreed statement of facts, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3222(1). The appellate division entered an order in favor of the MTA, finding its acted within the scope of its authority under N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 1277, finding the statute constitutional, and making an award to the MTA, declaring the county liable for liability insurance and expenses, and the county not entitled to credit or offset for liens or additional revenues received by the railroad. On the county's appeal, the court affirmed the appellate division. The court determined that the statute did not violated the constitution because the MTA did not have taxing authority and was not a super-government, that the legislature provided only limited discretion to the MTA in fixing costs to be charged the county, and that the statute did not violate Home Rule because it had sufficiently state wide impact, N.Y. Const. art. IXX, § 2(b)(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that the MTA acted within the scope of its authority and the statutes creating the MTA were constitutionally valid.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- Metropolitan Transportation Authority -- statute (Public Authorities Law, art. 5, tit. 11, § 1260 et seq. [L. 1965, ch. 324]), which created Metropolitan Transportation Authority, not unconstitutional -- Authority does not have power to tax -- no constitutional requirement that members be elected rather than appointed -- statutory standards sufficient -- Authority not authorized to disburse revenues in violation of Constitution (art. VII, § 7) -- legislation sufficiently "general" (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 2, subd. [b], par. [2]).

1. The statute (Public Authorities Law, art. 5, tit. 11, § 1260 et seq. [L. 1965, ch. 324]) which created the Metropolitan Transportation Authority (MTA) is not unconstitutional.

2. The statute did not create the MTA as a "super-local-government" and give it power to tax in violation of section 1 of article XVI, and section 3 of article VIII, of the State Constitution. The MTA does not have the power to tax.

3. There is no constitutional requirement that the members of the Authority be elected rather than appointed.

4. The statutory standards under which the Authority operates are as detailed as is reasonably practicable.

5. The MTA has not been authorized to disburse its revenues in violation of the Constitution (art. VII, § 7), since these moneys are not a fund under the management of the State contemplated by the Constitution.

6. The legislation ( Public Authorities Law, § 1277), which allows the MTA to charge the costs of station operation to the county, transcends the concerns of Nassau County alone and affects a sizable portion of the State as a whole. As such, it is sufficiently "general" within the sense of the constitutional provision (art. IX, § 2, subd. [b], par. [2]).

 

160 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Overhill Bldg. Co. v. Delany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


28 N.Y.2d 449; 271 N.E.2d 537; 322 N.Y.S.2d 696; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1253


April 12, 1971, Argued  
May 26, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board challenged an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's judgment for respondent owner in its action under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 to annul the board's zoning decision and obtain a permit to modify a building.


OVERVIEW: The owner's building complied with the zoning ordinance but was deficient in its provision of off-street parking and constituted a nonconforming use. The other properties in the area were also nonconforming. The owner applied for a permit to remodel garage space into office space, which would have had the effect of further decreasing compliance with the parking requirement. The board denied a variance and the owner brought a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 action against the board to challenge its decision. The trial court granted the requested relief and the board sought review. The court held that: (1) because the building was in compliance and the parking was the issue, the request should be treated as an area variance; (2) an area variance should be granted unless the board demonstrated a public health, safety or welfare need; (3) the public interest in decreasing traffic congestion was an ample public safety justification; (4) the owner then had the burden of showing that its hardship deprived it of any reasonable use of the property and amounted to a taking of the property; and (5) mere financial loss was not hardship.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted the owner the requested relief from the decision of the board. The court remitted the matter for further proceedings to dismiss the owner's petition without prejudice to any action for a declaratory judgment.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- applications for variances from zoning regulations which prescribe number of off-street parking spaces required for buildings are governed by rules for area variances -- Board of Appeals properly denied variance to petitioner for purpose of converting off-street parking spaces into office space; such determination promoted public safety by decreasing traffic congestion -- constitutionality of zoning ordinance cannot be determined in article 78 proceeding -- petition dismissed without prejudice to commencement of action for declaratory judgment.

1. Applications for variances from zoning regulations which prescribe the number of off-street parking spaces required for buildings are governed by the rules relating to area variances. Petitioner is seeking to convert 1,850 square feet of garage space to office space to be added to its office and apartment building, which is a permitted use. While the change is not strictly one of area, the variance is to be treated as an area variance.

2. Appellant Board of Appeals denied a variance to petitioner which would have created more income producing office space and would have removed off-street parking spaces below the number for which petitioner has a nonconforming use, although, under the zoning ordinance, the addition of the office space required more off-street parking spaces. Such denial was not an abuse of discretion. The board reasonably determined that further increases in the amount of square feet devoted to business use would be likely to result in traffic problems and that it was necessary to promote public safety by enacting and applying the ordinance. The courts below erroneously substituted their judgment for that of the board. The board's determination did not deprive petitioner of any use of its property since it remains a valid apartment and office use. That petitioner suffers a financial loss by denial of the variance does not constitute a hardship within the rule established by Matter of Fulling v. Palumbo (21 N Y 2d 30).

3. In this proceeding ( CPLR art. 78) the constitutionality of the zoning ordinance cannot be determined. Petitioner's remedy is an action for a declaratory judgment. Although CPLR 103 (subd. [c]) gives the courts the power to treat an article 78 proceeding as an action for declaratory judgment, it cannot be done here because the village trustees must be parties to the proceeding and they have not been made parties.

4. The petition is dismissed without prejudice to the commencement of an action for a declaratory judgment.

Hidley v. Rockefeller

There is no connection between the loss of jobs and the form of the budget bills. There is no showing that any positions would be more secure had the budgetary and legislative processes taken the form that plaintiffs assert are constitutionally mandated.

164 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Oakley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


28 N.Y.2d 309; 270 N.E.2d 318; 321 N.Y.S.2d 596; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1352


April 6, 1971, Argued  
April 21, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review after the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) affirmed a jury verdict that convicted defendant of robbery and related crimes.


OVERVIEW: The police exhibited defendant to the complainant, who identified him as the culprit. Defendant argued that because the "show-up" exhibition was suggestive and, therefore, inadmissible under the rules laid down in a series of federal cases, the grand jury identification was "inadmissible" and that the people failed to present a prima facie case to the grand jury. The court held that evidence that was inadmissible because it was incompetent (for example, hearsay) was different from evidence competent prima facie, rendered inadmissible only by extrinsic, subsequent proof. For purposes of grand jury presentations, the evidence was competent until nullified. An indictment's proper purpose was to bring a defendant to trial upon a prima facie case, which, if unexplained, would warrant a conviction, N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 251. The identification testimony before the grand jury satisfied that purpose. Moreover, undoing the indictment would only result in resubmission to the grand jury. It would also ignore the significance of other evidence in the case, which corroborated complainant's identification, that made the victim's identification of less than the usual importance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- indictment -- identification -- indictment not rendered infirm because identification testimony before Grand Jury, competent prima facie, was subsequently held to be based upon inadmissibly suggestive exhibition of defendant alone -- with respect to issue raised by defendant as to People's failure to call certain witness, no showing of prejudice to defendant.

1. An indictment is not rendered infirm because identification testimony before the Grand Jury, which was competent prima facie, was subsequently held to be based upon an inadmissibly suggestive exhibition of defendant alone.

2. With respect to an issue raised by defendant as to the People's failure to call a certain witness, there was nothing to show that the witness could give any relevant evidence and on no view of the matter was there any showing of prejudice to defendant.

167 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Murphy v. Erie County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


28 N.Y.2d 80; 268 N.E.2d 771; 320 N.Y.S.2d 29; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1462


January 11, 1971, Argued  
March 3, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York) affirmed an order and judgment of the trial court that granted the motion of defendants, county and company, to dismiss plaintiff taxpayers' amended complaint challenging the validity of a stadium project. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The county entered into a contract with a company whereby the company agreed to operate a new proposed domed stadium as lessee in return for a percentage of the revenues. The taxpayers alleged that the contract violated provisions of the state constitution and effected a waste of county funds and sought to enjoin the county from permitting the stadium to be operated by someone other than county employees and to declare the contract null and void. The county and company moved to dismiss the taxpayers' complaint based on res judicata by another taxpayer's previous action. The trial court granted the dismissal, and the appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The taxpayers appealed on constitutional grounds. The court held that res judicata applied only to the matters actually litigated in the prior suit. The prior suit only raised questions of competitive bidding on the contract and res judicata did not prevent the trial court from passing upon the merits. The court ruled that the lease provided to the company by the county was in furtherance of a public and not a private purpose, and was not in violation of the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's order affirming the trial court's dismissal of the taxpayers' complaint was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Counties -- contracts -- validity -- contract executed by county, pursuant to act of State Legislature (L. 1968, ch. 252) permitting incurring of indebtedness in connection with building of stadium, was warranted by legislative act -- contract is in furtherance of public and not private purpose -- no loan or gift of property (N. Y. Const., art. VIII, § 1) -- in third taxpayers' suit regarding stadium, res judicata applies only to matters actually litigated in prior suit.

1. Pursuant to an act of the State Legislature empowering Erie County to enter into contracts and incur indebtedness in connection with the building of a stadium (L. 1968, ch. 252), and a resolution of the County Legislature, an agreement was signed by the County Executive and one Kenford providing that Kenford would operate the stadium as lessee and would pay a stated sum to the county over a 40-year term. If the parties could not agree upon a lease within a stated time, then, it was agreed, they would execute a 20-year management agreement whereby Kenford would operate the stadium in return for a percentage of the revenues. The lease or management contract was warranted by the act authorizing the county to build the stadium.

2. By giving control over the stadium to Kenford, for either 40 years under the lease or 20 years under the management contract, the county did not convert the stadium into a private use for Kenford's benefit. The purposes of the stadium are to "furnish to, or foster, or promote among, or provide for the benefit of, the people of the county of Erie, recreation, entertainment, amusement, education, enlightenment, cultural enrichment" (L. 1968, ch. 252, § 2). That the county may not itself be using the stadium is irrelevant to these purposes. The county's residents will be obtaining the full benefit for which the stadium is intended. The lease is in furtherance of a public and not a private purpose.

3. The fact that the bids submitted exceeded the amount authorized by the county does not render the litigation moot. Whether the county is absolved from any obligation to Kenford and whether its legislature was justified in rescinding the contract by unilateral action are questions which require a factual determination.

4. The lease here under attack may not be construed as a "loan" or "gift" of county property "in aid of any * * * private corporation or association, or private undertaking," as prohibited by article VIII ( § 1) of the State Constitution.

5. This is the third taxpayers' suit challenging the stadium project. Upon considerations of policy in these taxpayers' actions, res judicata applies only as to the matters actually litigated in the prior suit.

 

168 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ditniak


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


28 N.Y.2d 74; 268 N.E.2d 768; 320 N.Y.S.2d 25; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1500


January 13, 1971, Argued  
February 25, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from an order of the Ulster County Court (New York), which dismissed eight separate informations in the form of Uniform Traffic Tickets that had charged defendants with violations of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 21, § 103.1.


OVERVIEW: Each defendant was a driver of a charter bus and was charged in a separate information with violating § 103.1 by driving a charter bus in a closed service area between Albany and New York City during a summer weekend. Defendants contended that closing those portions of the road violated the equal protection clause. The trial court dismissed the charges and the state sought review. On appeal, the court reversed the dismissal, holding that there was no constitutional violation because there was a reasonable basis for the exclusion of the charter buses from the service areas. The thruway authority had the right to exclude vehicles from an already crowded service area, and the choice of exclusion for only charter buses was rationally related to the fact that they brought a large crowd of people all at one time. There was valid authority and reasoning for the particular exclusion and accordingly, the charges should not have been dismissed. The matter was remanded for further proceedings upon reinstatement of the informations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's dismissal of the informations charging the charter bus drivers with violation of the rule which excluded them from particular service areas during busy summer weekends. The court directed reinstatement of the informations and remanded for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- Thruway -- charter buses -- Thruway Authority in closing service areas between Albany and New York City to charter buses on summer weekends did not violate equal protection clauses of Federal and State Constitutions -- general regulations of Thruway Authority on file with Secretary of State sufficient -- further filing not required.

1. The actions of the Thruway Authority in closing service areas between Albany and New York City to charter buses on summer weekends do not violate the equal protection clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions.

2. Each defendant, a driver of a charter bus, was charged in a separate information with violating section 103.1 of the Thruway Regulations ( 21 NYCRR 103.1), which provides, in part, that "No person shall fail, neglect or refuse to comply with any * * * sign * * * erected or displayed by the Thruway Authority on the Thruway system". The signs in question announced that the service areas between Albany and New York City would be closed to charter buses from midnight Friday to midnight Sunday during the heavily traveled summer months. There is no violation of equal protection where the Authority, due to the overcrowding of facilities, restricted the use of those areas in order to protect the users of the facilities from injury and inconvenience. The charter bus companies were admittedly forewarned so as to allow them to make alternative plans.

3. Moreover, the Thruway Authority has an affirmative duty to take action to alleviate dangerous conditions that it could foresee. The action taken is both reasonable and consistent with its responsibilities.

4. There are on file with the Secretary of State pursuant to the requirements of section 8 of article IV of the New York State Constitution regulations allowing the Thruway Authority to close any part of the Thruway system to the public if dangerous conditions evolve and prohibiting any person from refusing to obey any sign or device erected by the Thruway Authority. These parent or general regulations constitute sufficient authority and a further filing is not required.

171 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Menechino v. Warden, Green Haven State Prison


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 376; 267 N.E.2d 238; 318 N.Y.S.2d 449; 1971 N.Y. LEXIS 1606


November 9, 1970, Argued  
January 13, 1971, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Supreme Court, Dutchess County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator parolee appealed an order from the Supreme Court in Dutchess County (New York), which denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus. The parolee argued that his due process rights were violated because he did not have the benefit of counsel at his parole revocation hearing.


OVERVIEW: The issue presented on appeal was whether the parolee was constitutionally entitled, under the United States Constitution and the Constitution of New York State, to the assistance of counsel in his parole revocation hearing. Although most of the courts which passed upon the matter held that there was no such right under the Due Process Clause of the Fifth or Fourteenth Amendment, the court preferred the contrary view, expressed by a few courts, as well as by the commentators, that the right to the assistance of an attorney at the hearing was constitutionally mandated. Revocation of parole was a decision that deprived an individual of his liberty; as such, the decision turned on factual determinations. Therefore, it was necessity for the parolee to have the aid of counsel in marshaling the facts and introducing evidence of mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: Accordingly, the order, which denied the parolee's petition for writ of habeas corpus, was reversed and the case was remitted to the Board of Parole for the purpose of conducting a hearing at which the parolee would be entitled to the assistance of counsel and permitted to call witnesses.


HEADNOTES

Parole -- revocation of parole -- right to counsel -- parolee has constitutional right to be represented by lawyer and is entitled to introduce testimony in parole revocation hearings.

1. The demands of due process, under both the United States Constitution and the Constitution of the State of New York, require that a parolee be represented by a lawyer in parole revocation hearings.

2. Although the United States Supreme Court has not considered whether a parolee has such a constitutional right in a parole revocation hearing, it has held that counsel must be afforded a defendant at a proceeding to revoke probation. The differences between the "deferred sentencing" procedure involved in two Supreme Court cases, probation revocation and parole revocation are not such as would militate against the need for a lawyer where revocation of parole results in the deprivation of liberty.

3. The presence of an attorney and the receipt of testimony offered by the parolee need not prolong the hearing beyond the time necessary for the Board of Parole to ascertain the facts upon which it is to make its determination.

4. The failure of relator to request the assistance of a lawyer at his hearing did not constitute a waiver of his right to counsel. In the absence of competent legal assistance, he may not be precluded from arguing that he is entitled to a lawyer because he did not know enough to ask for one.

 

172 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Irwin v. Board of Regents


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 292; 265 N.E.2d 752; 317 N.Y.S.2d 332; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 899


October 8, 1970, Argued  
December 10, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner certified public accountant (CPA) sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), reversing the trial court's judgment that had directed recission and cancellation of an order from the Commissioner of Education that had revoked petitioner's CPA license. Petitioner contended that he was denied a fair hearing because he was denied certain subpoenas duces tecum.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner certified public accountant (CPA) had been convicted of furnishing a gratuity to a federal employee. The Department of Education subsequently charged him with having been convicted of a crime and with being guilty of unprofessional conduct. Petitioner was found guilty of both charges and respondent university board of regents ordered the Commissioner of Education to revoke petitioner's license to practice as a CPA. The trial court directed recission and cancellation of the order revoking petitioner's certificate and the appellate court reversed. On appeal, the court affirmed the order. Petitioner contended that he was denied a fair hearing because the committee hearing the charges against him denied his request for the issuance of certain subpoenas duces tecum. The court found that the failure on the part of the subcommittee to issue the subpoena duces tecum was not an abuse of discretion. A subpoena duces tecum could not have supported petitioner's claim that he was deprived of the opportunity to show that his conviction was not a crime with the meaning of the Education Law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the appellate court that had reversed the trial court's recission and cancellation of an order of the Commissioner of Education which revoked petitioner certified public accountant's (CPA) license to practice as a CPA. It was not an abuse of discretion for the subcommittee, before which the hearing was held, to deny petitioner's request for the issuance of certain subpoenas duces tecum.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- certified public accountants -- petitioner, certified public accountant, was not denied fair hearing on charges of having been convicted of crime and unprofessional conduct, of which he was held guilty resulting in revocation of license -- since hearing committee was given specific authority by Education Law ( § 7406, subd. 3) to issue subpoenas, CPLR 2307 dealing with issuance of subpoenas, was not limit on committee's power -- under language of section 7406, petitioner did not have unqualified right to have subpoenas issued, but matter rested in discretion of hearing committee -- first reason advanced for issuance of subpoena could not be supported and denial was not abuse of discretion -- second reason, that regulation under which charges were brought was not properly filed, is also without merit.

1. Following the conviction of petitioner, a certified public accountant, in Federal court for furnishing a gratuity to a Federal employee, he was charged by the Department of Education with having been convicted of a crime and with unprofessional conduct, and after a hearing held at which he was found guilty of both charges, respondent ordered the revocation of his license to practice as a certified public accountant. Petitioner was not denied a fair hearing because his request for the issuance of certain subpoenas duces tecum was denied by the hearing committee.

2. CPLR 2307 (subd. [a]) which deals with the issuance of subpoenas by the court, is applicable only to those subpoenas issued pursuant to CPLR 2302 (subd. [a]) providing for the issuance of subpoenas without court order by "any member of a board, commission or committee authorized by law to hear, try or determine a matter" not granted specific power to issue subpoenas. Since the hearing committee here was given specific authority to issue subpoenas by section 7406 (subd. 3) of the Education Law, CPLR 2307 was not a limit on said committee's power.

3. Section 7406 (subd. 3) of the Education Law provides that at any hearing in disciplinary proceedings, respondent shall have "the right to produce witnesses * * * and to have subpoenas issued by the committee. The complainant likewise shall have similar power to produce witnesses and to have any necessary subpoenas issued by the committee." Under this language petitioner did not have the unqualified right to have subpoenas issued on his behalf. The issuance of subpoenas upon the request of respondent or complainant is a matter within the discretion of the hearing committee.

4. Petitioner sought the subpoena to show that his conviction under the Federal statute was not a crime within the meaning of the Education Law but only a crime of gratuity. Section 7406 (subd. 1, par. [c]) requires only a conviction in a court of competent jurisdiction of any crime with no distinction between types of crimes. Since the issuance of the subpoena could not support petitioner's claim, the denial was not an abuse of discretion.

5. As a second reason for seeking the subpoena, petitioner sought to establish that the regulation under which this charge was brought was not properly filed and published. Contrary to such claim, the rule defining unprofessional conduct, having been adopted by the Commissioner of Education and approved by the Board of Regents, was filed in the office of the Secretary of State and was published in 8 NYCRR 70.5. Since such reason is without merit, the hearing committee's denial was likewise proper.

 

173 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Oriental Boulevard Co. v. Heller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 212; 265 N.E.2d 72; 316 N.Y.S.2d 226; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 994; 2 ERC (BNA) 1037


September 21, 1970, Argued  
November 12, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff apartment house owners brought suit against defendant municipal officials contending that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code §§ 892-1.0 - 897-2.0, an ordinance regulating the use of fuel burners and refuse incinerators, was unconstitutional. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the officials, and the owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: Apartment house owners claimed that a municipal ordinance requiring upgraded pollution controls was unconstitutional because state law preempted local pollution control legislation. They also argued that it was impossible to comply with the ordinance's deadlines, that the cost of compliance was prohibitory, that penalties under the ordinance were confiscatory, and that no rational basis existed for the ordinance. The officials defended the ordinance as a reasonable exercise of the municipality's police power. The court held that the ordinance was constitutional because the government could attack massive problems such as air pollution piecemeal and the choice of reasonable courses of action was within the domain of legislative and executive discretion. State law was not preemptive because N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 10, 14 recognized local government's role in controlling pollution. The owners' arguments that the ordinance would not achieve its goals and was too costly did not affect the constitutionality of the legislative choice made in the ordinance. Unless it was shown that compliance was in fact impossible, the provisions for cumulative penalties were not confiscatory.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate division.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- reduction of air pollutants -- municipality has power to regulate use of fuel burners and refuse incinerators by multiple dwellings to control emission of air pollutants -- pollution by such equipment is not of trivial effect -- wisdom of measures required by municipal ordinance was within legislative discretion -- cumulative penalties provided by ordinance are valid -- plaintiffs have not shown they are aggrieved by time requirements of ordinance -- outlay required for correction is not so disproportionate as to be unconstitutional -- State has not pre-empted subject matter -- under statute, provisions for summary sealing of equipment are not unconstitutional; hearing and notice are provided.

1. A municipality has power by local ordinance to regulate the use of fuel burners and refuse incinerators by multiple dwellings to control the emission of air pollutants.

2. Pollution caused by incinerators and oil burning equipment is not of trivial effect. Even if there is a 2% daily contribution of pollutants by private apartment houses, this contribution aggregates over 186 tons per day. Moreover, government is and must be entitled to attack massive problems piecemeal, and to select those most susceptible areas which permit of the least destructive effect on the economy.

3. The wisdom and practicality of the rigorous measures required by the ordinance were within the domain of legislative and executive discretion. So long as there is reasonable basis in available information, and rationality in chosen courses of conduct to alleviate an accepted evil, there is no constitutional infirmity.

4. The cumulative penalties provided in the ordinance constitute a valid means of control. Absent a showing of impossibility of compliance with the requirements of the ordinance the cumulative penalties are not confiscatory.

5. Because plaintiffs do not contend that they, rather than all owners of multiple dwellings, found timely performance impossible because of a shortage of pollution control engineers, they fail to show that they are aggrieved. Statutes will not be struck down unless plaintiffs are actually aggrieved.

6. The outlay of sums ranging from $ 3,500 to $ 19,000 to upgrade fuel burners and incinerators, considering the serious health hazard represented by air pollution, is not disproportionate to the capital investment or the benefits to be obtained. There is no unconstitutionality involved and the balance to be struck is a legislative and not a judicial obligation.

7. The Environmental Conservation Law (L. 1970, ch. 140) has not pre-empted the subject and explicitly recognizes that local governments are intended to function in the air pollution area.

8. The ordinance authorizes summary sealing in the case of specified classes of fuel burners and incinerators for which permits have not been obtained and whose operation has thus become unlawful. A hearing for the affected owner, even after the summary sealing, obtainable by judicial proceedings of a summary or plenary character is sufficient to insure constitutionality so long as the health hazard to be controlled is sufficiently widespread and grave and immediate action is desirable. As to other equipment, there must be notice and hearing before the offending equipment may be sealed on the ground that it is emitting noxious substances.

 

174 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 198; 265 N.E.2d 66; 316 N.Y.S.2d 217; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1025


October 7, 1970, Argued  
November 11, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Criminal Court of the City of New York, Kings County (New York), entered judgment convicting defendant of menacing in violation of N.Y. Penal Law, § 120.15, possession of a dangerous weapon in violation of § 265.05, and unlawful mutilation of the American flag in violation of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 136(d).On review, the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court, Second Judicial Department, affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On the evening of August 3, 1968, a policeman observed defendant affixing an American flag to a vacant building. As a crowd of about 40 to 50 people looked on, defendant proceeded to saturate the flag with lighter fluid and then ignited it, Upon being informed by the policeman that he was under arrest, defendant took a knife from his trouser pocket, warned him to stay away, and then lunged at the officer. On appeal of his subsequent convictions and the affirmance of those convictions by the appellate term, defendant contended N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 136(d) was unconstitutional both on its face and as applied. The court affirmed because the record unequivocally established the sole basis for defendant's conviction was his act of burning the flag, not his words.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that in part convicted defendant of unlawful mutilation of the American flag.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- defilement of flag -- defendant, who uttered protest words as he burned flag, was not convicted of defiling flag based both on his words and deed, which is constitutionally impermissible, but only on his act of burning flag.

Subdivision d of section 136 of the General Business Law has been held constitutional insofar as it renders criminal the act of any person who publicly mutilates, defaces or defiles the flag. It has also been held that a defendant could not be convicted of defiling the flag for both his words and his deed. Even though defendant, when he burned the flag, stated that "I am going to burn Johnson, Humphrey and Wallace just as I am going to burn this flag", defendant was not convicted for his words. The information charged only the commission of an act, that of defiling the flag, and made no reference to defendant's words, and the entire record establishes unequivocally that the sole basis for defendant's conviction was his act of burning the flag.
