A. E. Nettleton Co. v. Diamond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 182; 264 N.E.2d 118; 315 N.Y.S.2d 625; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1039; 1 ERC (BNA) 1700; 44 A.L.R.3d 994


September 23, 1970, Argued  
October 15, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, without costs, and case remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, several officials of the State of New York (state), sought review of the judgment of the Supreme Court, Onondaga County (New York), which declared N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 358-a to be contrary to the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. Appellee manufacturers (industry) had brought suit seeking to have that law and others declared unconstitutional and requesting an injunction enjoining their enforcement.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that the Harris Act was a laudable complement to the Federal Endangered Species Conservation Act of 1969, but that the Mason Law, N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 358-a, was violative of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, on the ground that it was an unreasonable exercise of the state police power and deprived the industry of property without due process of law. The court disagreed and reversed the trial court's judgment, finding that the Mason Law was a reasonable exercise of the police power and applied to skins, hides, or products which arrived in the United States on or before August 31, 1970. The court also ruled that the statute did not deprive the industry of property without due process of law. Further, the court found no conflict between the federal statute and the Mason Law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the matter was remitted for the entry of a judgment declaring the Mason Law constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- use of hides of alligators, spotted cats and other animals -- section 358-a of Agriculture and Markets Law prohibiting sale or offer for sale after September 1, 1970 of skins or bodies or products made therefrom of certain named endangered species of animals is constitutional -- section does not apply to skins, hides or products which arrived in the United States of America before August 31, 1970 -- pre-emption under the Supremacy Clause is constitutional issue cognizable in direct appeal under CPLR 5601 (subd. [b], par. 2).

1. Section 358-a of the Agriculture and Markets Law prohibits the sale or offer for sale after September 1, 1970 of the skin or body of stated wild animals including alligators, crocodiles and caiman skins used by plaintiff in its shoe manufacturing business, and spotted cats and other endangered animals used by intervenors in their respective businesses, and provides for the forfeiture of goods in violation of the statute. The statute is constitutional. The 1969 Federal Endangered Species Conservation Act has not pre-empted the field and the State law is neither a violation of the Supremacy Clause nor the Commerce Clause of the Constitution. The law constitutes a valid exercise of the police power ( People v. Bunis, 9 N Y 2d 1, distinguished) and does not constitute a denial of due process.

2. Section 358-a does not apply to skins, hides, or products therefrom which arrived in the United States of America on or before August 31, 1970 and, accordingly, is not confiscatory.

3. Pre-emption under the Supremacy Clause is a constitutional issue cognizable in a direct appeal pursuant to CPLR 5601 (subd. [b], par. 2). ( Swift & Co. v. Wickham, 382 U.S. 111, distinguished.)

 

176 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gilligan v. Procaccino


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 162; 263 N.E.2d 385; 314 N.Y.S.2d 985; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1081


May 26, 1970, Argued  
October 7, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: In Matter of Gilligan: Order reversed, without costs, and petition granted.

In Matter of Mariconda: Order reversed, without costs, and judgment of Special Term reinstated.

In Matter of Serra: Order reversed, without costs, and judgment of Special Term reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner law secretaries brought three actions against respondent city and judicial officials, seeking salary increases. The trial courts (New York) in two proceedings entered judgments in favor of the secretaries. The other trial court entered judgment dismissing the petition. On review, the appellate division, third department, entered orders affirming the dismissal and reversing the other two judgments. The secretaries appealed.


OVERVIEW: On April 8, 1964, the judicial officials sent a letter to the city officials, stating the judicial officials had unanimously agreed to withhold in whole or in part from certain of the nonjudicial personnel the salary increases which otherwise would have been granted to the particular individuals. In response, the secretaries, who were non-lawyers, brought their three actions, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, claiming increased salaries were unlawfully withheld from them. In two of the proceedings, the trial courts agreed with the secretaries and entered judgment accordingly. In the third proceeding, however, the trial court agreed with the city and judicial officials and dismissed the petition. On review, the appellate division held all three petitions should have been dismissed. On further review, the court reversed the appellate division and granted the secretaries' petitions because the judicial officials lacked the authority to withhold the salary increases from the secretaries.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the appellate division and granted the secretaries' petitions.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- salaries of nonjudicial personnel -- Justices of Appellate Division in First and Second Departments were not "appropriate appointing authority" who, by laws of 1961 and 1962, could withhold increased salaries provided for in such laws, to petitioners, nonlawyer secretaries to Justices of Supreme Court; individual Justices were "appropriate appointing authority"; petitioners are entitled to increased salaries.

The Justices of the Appellate Division in the First and Second Departments were not "the appropriate appointing authority" who, under chapter 492 of the Laws of 1961 and chapter 640 of the Laws of 1962, could withhold the increased salaries provided for in such chapters to petitioners, nonlawyer "Law Secretaries to Justices" in such Departments. Under section 157 of the Judiciary Law, the individual Justices have the power to appoint "Law Secretaries to Justices" of the Supreme Court and the general administrative powers of the Appellate Divisions (Judiciary Law, §§ 214, 216) must give way to the specific power of appointment residing in the individual Justices of the Supreme Court. Moreover, section 222 of the Judiciary Law provides that wherever, under existing law, a Judge or Justice is empowered to appoint personal assistants, that power is to continue, after the court reorganization in 1962 "notwithstanding the provisions of section two hundred fourteen of this chapter [Judiciary Law]". Petitioners are entitled to receive the increases.

 

177 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Michael A.C.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 79; 261 N.E.2d 620; 313 N.Y.S.2d 695; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1115


May 25, 1970, Argued  
July 2, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v. Michael A. C. (Anonymous): Order affirmed.

In People v. Jerome C. (Anonymous): Order reversed and judgment of Nassau County Court reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of two decisions by the Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, which reversed the trial court's adjudication of two defendants as youthful offenders and ordered a new trial in each case.


OVERVIEW: The trial court adjudicated the two defendants to be youthful offenders. The intermediate appellate court reversed the adjudications based on the United States Supreme Court's decision in Duncan v. Louisiana, 391 U.S. 145 (1968). The Duncan court held that N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. §§ 913-g(3) and 913-h, which require a youngster between the ages of 16 and 19 to consent to a trial without a jury in order to render him eligible for youthful offender treatment, were unconstitutional. In affirming the intermediate appellate court's judgment as to one defendant, the court held that §§ 913-g(3) and 913-h were unconstitutional insofar as the sections compelled a defendant to consent to a trial without a jury in order to render him eligible for youthful offender treatment. As to the other defendant, however, the court held that the defendant's adjudication as a youthful offender antedated the Duncan decision, which had prospective effect only, by approximately two months. Thus, the court ordered reinstatement of the trial court's adjudication of the defendant as a youthful offender.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the intermediate appellate court as to one defendant. As to the other defendant, however, the court reversed the decision of the intermediate appellate court and ordered the reinstatement of the defendant's adjudication as a youthful offender.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- youthful offenders -- jury trial -- youthful offender, exposed to possibility of four-year reformatory sentence of imprisonment, is entitled to trial by jury -- State may not demand his consent to trial without jury as condition to affording him benefits of youthful offender treatment -- statutes compelling defendant to consent to trial without jury to render him eligible for youthful offender treatment are unconstitutional -- decision in Duncan v. Louisiana (391 U.S. 145) receives prospective application only -- consequently, one defendant whose trial antedated that decision was not entitled to jury trial.

1. The Sixth Amendment as applied to the States through the Fourteenth Amendment requires that defendants charged with "serious crimes" be afforded the right to a trial by jury ( Duncan v. Louisiana, 391 U.S. 145). Where the possible penalty exceeds six months' imprisonment, the offense is deemed sufficiently "serious" to require the grant of a jury trial ( Baldwin v. New York, 399 U.S. 66, 69). A youthful offender, such as one of the defendants herein who was indicted for criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the second degree, a class D felony (Penal Law, former § 220.15), and is exposed to the possibility of a four-year reformatory sentence of imprisonment (Penal Law, art. 75; Code Crim. Pro., § 913-m, subd. 1, par. [d]), is entitled to a trial by jury (cf. In re Winship, 397 U.S. 358).

2. Although any defendant, including a youthful offender, is free to consent to a trial without a jury, a State may not demand that such consent be given as an absolute precondition to affording him the benefits of youthful offender treatment.

3. Sections 913-g and 913-h of the Code of Criminal Procedure, insofar as they compel a defendant to consent to a trial without a jury to render him eligible for youthful offender treatment, are unconstitutional.

4. The trial of one of the defendants antedated, by some two months, the Supreme Court's decision in Duncan v. Louisiana (391 U.S. 145), although he was adjudged a youthful offender subsequent to that decision. In DeStefano v. Woods (392 U.S. 631), the court held that the Duncan decision should receive only prospective application. Consequently, this defendant was not entitled to a jury trial.

5. The in-court identification of the latter defendant was not influenced or affected by the lineup of which he complains. If there was any error or impropriety in the procedure adopted, it may be disregarded under section 542 of the Code of Criminal Procedure.

Simonson v. Cahn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 1; 261 N.E.2d 246; 313 N.Y.S.2d 97; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1151


May 11, 1970, Argued  
July 1, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, District Attorney and Judges of the County of Nassau, challenged an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), granted in favor of respondent foreman of grand jury on the constitutional issue of whether defendant, charged with a felony, could waive indictment by a grand jury and be tried on an information of the district attorney.


OVERVIEW: Informations were filed against defendant alleging misdemeanor possession of a narcotic drug in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.05 and of the felony possession of a loaded weapon in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.05(2). Although defendant was to go before the grand jury on the felony charge, the district attorney filed another information against him charging both offenses. At defendant's arraignment with his counsel present he was advised of his right to proceed before the grand jury and waived it for a prosecution under the felony information. The foreman brought his petition under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 that raised the constitutional claim that defendant could not be prosecuted for a felony without an indictment by the grand jury. The court affirmed the order of the appellate division restraining the district attorney from prosecuting and restraining the judges from taking jurisdiction of the criminal matter until after the grand jury returned the indictment. The appellate division correctly agreed with the foreman's contention that the district court lacked jurisdiction based on the explicit grand jury requirement of N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, which could not be waived.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate division contested by appellants and entered for the foreman on the constitutional issue of whether a defendant facing felony charges could waive the indictment by grand jury and be tried on the district attorney's information. The court did not award costs.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- waiver of indictment -- provision in State Constitution (art. I, § 6) that no person shall be held to answer for capital or otherwise infamous crime unless on indictment of grand jury may not be waived.

The provision in the State Constitution (art. I, § 6) that "No person shall be held to answer for a capital or otherwise infamous crime * * * unless on indictment of a grand jury" may not be waived.

8200 Realty Corp. v. Lindsay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 124; 261 N.E.2d 647; 313 N.Y.S.2d 733; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1161


June 3, 1970, Argued  
July 1, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and case remitted to Supreme Court, New York County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlords filed an action against defendant municipality seeking a declaration that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § YY 51 was unconstitutional. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) granted the landlords' motion for summary judgment, and the municipality appealed.


OVERVIEW: The municipality enacted New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § YY 51 to control rising rental charges that were causing hardship to tenants living in the municipality. The landlords claimed that the rent control provisions were unconstitutional because they resulted in an unlawful delegation of legislative authority. They also claimed that the statute violated the Equal Protection Clause and that it exceeded the delegation of power by the state to the municipality for the enactment of rent control legislation because it vested the administration of controls on rents and evictions in a group other than a city agency. The court held that the statute was constitutional. The court ruled that (1) the statute closely circumscribed and regulated the powers of the real estate industry association in setting limits on rental charges and delegated no legislative power to the association; and (2) the statute did not violate the Equal Protection Clause because its classification scheme had a rational basis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the landlords and entered a declaratory judgment upholding the constitutionality of the administrative code provision.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- Rent Stabilization Law of 1969 of City of New York providing for rent control over post-1947 apartments is valid.

The New York City Rent Stabilization Law of 1969 (Local Laws, 1969, No. 16 of City of New York; Administrative Code of City of New York, tit. YY, ch. 51) which extends rent control to post-1947 housing constitutes a valid exercise of the legislative authority of the city. There has been no unlawful delegation of power to a Real Estate Industry Stabilization Association. The overall supervision of the regulatory process is vested in the Housing and Development Administration which is expressly authorized to enact rules and regulations for the implementation of the statute, to approve the code of a Real Estate Industry Stabilization Association and to discipline an association. The right of any member aggrieved by action of an association to review before the Conciliation and Appeals Board, whose members are appointed by the Mayor, shows that no judicial power has been given the association. While the funds to pay the salaries of the board and the expenses of the office are provided by the association, it does not involve an invalid delegation of the taxing power. Since there is a rational basis for the difference in regulatory treatment between pre-1947 and post-1947 housing, there has been no denial of equal protection of the laws.

 

180 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sweeny


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 138; 261 N.E.2d 655; 313 N.Y.S.2d 744; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1162


May 12, 1970, Argued  
July 1, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, individuals who participated in scheme to purchase insurance company, appealed judgment of Appellate Division of Supreme Court in First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed defendants' convictions for violating N.Y. Ins. Law § 78, subd. 2, and one defendant's conviction for forgery and criminal misconduct of officer of corporation.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, individuals who participated in scheme to purchase insurance company, appealed appellate court judgment which affirmed defendants' convictions for pledge of insurance company assets in violation of N.Y. Ins. Law § 78, subd. 2, and one defendant's conviction for forgery and criminal misconduct of officer of corporation. Defendants purchased insurance company's stock by using proceeds from sale of insurance company's government bonds. Indictment charged defendants with allegedly concealing this transaction by issuance of, and manipulations with, certificates of deposit. Court reversed convictions. After purchasing insurance company's stock, defendants arranged complex offsetting loans, and deposits of loan proceeds were made to procure certificates of deposit to secure such loans. However, insurance company never owned these certificates. N.Y. Ins. Law § 78, subd. 2, required that assets pledged be those of insurer. Convictions for forgery and misconduct of corporate officer were reversed because they were based on certificates being insurance company's assets.


OUTCOME: After finding defendants concealed fraudulent purchase of insurance company's stock by arranging offsetting loans and deposited loan proceeds to procure certificates of deposit to secure such loans, court found insurance company never owned these certificates. New York Insurance Law was not violated because defendants had not pledged insurer's assets; therefore, judgment reversed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- assets of insurance company -- proof showed that certificates of deposit in name of insurance company were not assets of said company; hence proof, as limited by indictment and by theory of trial, failed to show that defendants pledged assets of insurance company (Insurance Law, § 78, subd. 2) or falsely certified that assets were unencumbered (former Penal Law, § 665, subd. 3, and § 889), or conspired to commit those crimes; judgments of conviction reversed and indictment dismissed.

1. The first-named defendant Begole (who died before trial) purchased all the stock of a domestic casualty insurance company from its sole owner, in exchange for a bank's check for $ 5,000,000 payable to said seller; and said purchaser elected himself and a codefendant as directors of the insurance company and authorized the insurance company to sell its government bonds and to use the proceeds to buy, for the insurance company, a bank's certificate of deposit; but instead, he and his codefendants gave the bonds to the bank, which sold them; and thus the bank was paid for its $ 5,000,000 check, and thus said deceased defendant used the insurance company's own assets to pay for his purchase of its stock.

2. The defendants concealed this misappropriation of the insurance company's bonds, by means of several circular or "wash" transactions among themselves and among different financial corporations. These transactions produced a series of offsetting book entries. In these camouflage transactions, no assets of the insurance company were passed or were moved, but several bank certificates of deposit were issued in the name of the insurance company, and some of those nominally owned certificates of deposit were pledged. The original misappropriation seems to have become time-barred from prosecution, and it has not been shown that the subsequent concealment of the misappropriation further eroded the company's assets; and in any event, that is not for what the defendants were indicted.

3. The defendants were indicted and convicted of pledging securities of the insurance company for a loan for which the insurance company did not receive the proceeds (Insurance Law, § 78, subd. 2), and of forgery in the third degree and misconduct by a corporate officer, consisting of false statements in the company's books and in its reports to the Superintendent of Insurance, to the effect that certificates of deposit owned by the company were not encumbered or pledged (former Penal Law, § 889; § 665, subd. 3), and of conspiring to commit those crimes. Section 78 (subd. 2) of the Insurance Law requires that the assets pledged be those of the insurance company; and the judgments of conviction of the other crimes concededly depend also on "assets". But the proof, as limited by the indictment and theory of the trial, showed that those certificates were never "assets" of the insurance company. Accordingly, the judgments of conviction should be reversed and the indictment dismissed.

 

181 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Chestnut


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 481; 260 N.E.2d 501; 311 N.Y.S.2d 853; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1225


April 22, 1970, Argued  
June 4, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) judgments affirming their convictions for criminal contempt.


OVERVIEW: After being told that immunity had been granted, appellants refused to answer questions during a grand jury investigation and were charged with and convicted of criminal contempt. Appellants sought review of the convictions, which were affirmed. On appeal from the judgment affirming the convictions, appellants argued that the grand jury was illegally constituted, arguing that regardless of motivation or intent, the state had an affirmative duty to provide a jury consisting of a cross-section of the community and that its failure deprived appellants of their constitutional rights. The court affirmed the judgment. The court held that defendants willfully refused to testify before a grand jury after being offered complete immunity. Furthermore, it held that the grand jury was lawfully and statutorily constituted. Since defendants could only be convicted on one count of contempt regardless of the number of questions on a particular subject, the court modified the judgment.


OUTCOME: Court affirmed judgment affirming appellants' convictions for criminal contempt, because appellants willfully refused to testify before a grand jury after immunity had been offered to them; and since appellants could only be convicted of one count of contempt, court modified judgment.


HEADNOTES

Grand Jury -- selection of jurors -- Grand Jury was lawfully constituted body, chosen without regard to race or other discriminatory factors -- defendants properly convicted of criminal contempt (former Penal Law, § 600, subd. 6) -- exclusion of welfare recipients, persons adjudged guilty of adultery and persons under age of 35 not lacking in reasonable justification -- defendants should have been found guilty of single contempt -- judgment modified accordingly -- defendants having been granted immunity, could be required to testify whether or not they were "targets" of investigation.

1. In order to sustain a claim of an illegally constituted jury, intentional and systematic discrimination must be proved and a showing of mathematical disparity, without more, is insufficient. The record establishes that the County Clerk tried to fulfill the statutory duty to select grand jurors who were "intelligent; of sound mind and good character [and] well informed" (Judiciary Law, § 596, subd. 6). By choosing jurors on the basis of these factors, rather than race or ethnic background, his action was consistent with the policies embodied in the Equal Protection Clause. The Grand Jury was a lawfully constituted body, chosen without regard to race or other discriminatory factors. Defendants, who refused to answer questions during an investigation by the Grand Jury after they had been granted immunity, were properly convicted of criminal contempt (former Penal Law, § 600, subd. 6).

2. While the County Clerk's office did intentionally exclude welfare recipients, persons adjudged guilty of adultery and persons under the age of 35, it may not be said that any of these criteria is so totally lacking in reasonable justification as to be constitutionally invalid.

3. All but one of the defendants was convicted on multiple counts of contempt, even though each of them made it clear, at the outset, that he would not answer any questions concerning the alleged conspiracy, and concurrent sentences were imposed. The court should have found the defendants guilty of but a single contempt, sentenced each of them for that crime and dismissed all the other counts. This result may be accomplished, without the necessity of remanding for resentencing, by modifying the judgment.

4. Where, as here, there has been compliance with the immunity statute, defendants, whether or not they were "targets" of the investigation as they claim, could be required to testify and be subjected to prosecution for contempt should they willfully refuse to do so.

 

182 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


300 West 154th Street Realty Co. v. Department of Bldgs.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 538; 260 N.E.2d 534; 311 N.Y.S.2d 899; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1249


May 11, 1970, Argued  
May 28, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, by remitting matter to Supreme Court, New York County, for a hearing in accordance with the opinion herein, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order that denied the landlord's petition to declare as unconstitutional the Emergency Repair Program of the New York City Department of Health, New York City, N.Y., Rules of the City of New York, tit. 564, §§ 15.0 to 31.0.


OVERVIEW: The nuisance, a blocked toilet, was located in an apartment occupied and controlled by a welfare recipient. She asserted that she contacted the landlord's office but was refused repairs. An employee of respondent city telephoned the landlord, who stated that it knew nothing of the stoppage until it received a bill for repair of the toilet that he was required to pay. On appeal, the court modified the order that denied a declaration of the statute as unconstitutional, and it remanded for a hearing. There were contested issues of fact that involved notice to the landlord by both the tenant and the city. Further, an order to abate and forced payment could not be enforced with respect to a nuisance not created by the landlord other than one that he must have or should have known, because of actual control, or operation, unless the city made reasonable efforts to first notify the landlord, beyond a notice by general publication. Although entitled to notice and an initial opportunity to effect necessary repairs, the landlord was not entitled to a judicial determination of liability before his tenants were asked to make payments due from them as rent directly to the city.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order and remitted the matter to the trial court for a hearing in accordance with the opinion.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- abatement of nuisance -- Board of Health of City of New York published resolution declaring buildings without effective sewage disposal facilities to be nuisance and ordered that adequate sewage disposal facilities be provided, throughout city -- board empowered by Administrative Code of City of New York (§ 564-21.0, subd. c) to order abatement of nuisance by general newspaper publication -- issue of fact presented as to notice assertedly given landlord by tenant and Department of Buildings -- order to abate may not be enforced with respect to nuisance not created by landlord unless reasonable efforts made to notify landlord -- proceeding remanded -- landlord not entitled to judicial determination of liability before his tenants are asked to make payments of rent directly to department.

1. The Administrative Code of the City of New York provides that a landlord be given personal service of an order to abate a nuisance, or, if necessary, service by mail or posting of the order on the offending premises (§ 564-21.0, subds. a, b). The Board of Health is empowered, however, to declare that a nuisance is so widespread that the regular provisions for notice are not feasible, and, by general newspaper publication, without specification of names and addresses, the board may order that "any such nuisance which may exist upon or in any real or personal property or place located within the area or areas specified in such order" be abated (§ 564-21.0, subd. c). The Board of Health published a resolution declaring buildings without effective sewage disposal facilities a nuisance, and ordered that adequate sewage disposal facilities be provided, throughout the city. The pleadings reveal contested issues of fact involving the notice assertedly given the landlord by both the tenant and Department of Buildings. An order to abate may not be enforced with respect to a nuisance not created by the landlord other than one of which he must have or should have known because of actual control, or operation, unless the department makes reasonable efforts to notify the landlord, beyond a notice by general publication made before creation of the nuisance in question. The proceeding should be remanded for a resolution of the factual issue.

2. Although the landlord is entitled to notice and an initial opportunity to effect necessary repairs, he is not entitled to a judicial determination of liability before his tenants are asked to make payments due from them as rent directly to the department.

184 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Old Farm Road, Inc. v. New Castle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 462; 259 N.E.2d 920; 311 N.Y.S.2d 500; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1284


April 8, 1970, Submitted  
May 14, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed an order of the trial court, granting a motion by defendants to dismiss the amended complaint to challenge the constitutionality of a zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The issue was the constitutionality of certain parts of the town's zoning ordinance. The provisions challenged created a board of architectural review and required that each application for a permit to construct a new building, or to make alterations or additions of specified nature and cost, would be submitted to the board. Without first applying for a permit, plaintiff brought an action for declaratory judgment stating that the ordinance provided no standards for the guidance of the board, exceeded the permissible bounds of the police power, and was too vague and ambiguous to be enforced. In granting defendants' motion to dismiss, the trial court gave effect to defendants' argument that plaintiff's failure to apply for a building permit rendered its constitutional attack premature. The court affirmed, concluding that plaintiff should, indeed, be confined to its administrative remedy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- application to declare zoning ordinance unconstitutional premature where no application had been made for building permit -- plaintiff confined to administrative remedy in first instance.

A town zoning ordinance creates a Board of Architectural Review and requires that each application for a permit to construct a new building, or to make alterations or additions of specified nature and cost, shall be submitted to the board. Authority is conferred upon the board to disapprove any such application upon finding that the structure "would, if erected or altered be so detrimental to the desirability, property values or development of the surrounding areas as to provide one or more of the harmful effects set forth in Section 1 [of the ordinance] by reason of * * * excessive similarity * * * excessive dissimilarity * * * or * * * inappropriateness in relation to the established character of other structures in the immediate area or neighboring areas". Without first applying for a permit, plaintiff brought this action for judgment declaring the ordinance unconstitutional. The causes of action are asserted prematurely where, as here, no application has been made for a building permit. Plaintiff should be confined to its administrative remedy in the first instance. Aesthetic considerations are not unlawful per se and unless and, until a building permit for a specific structure shall have been denied, even the existence of any present damage cannot be demonstrated. Additionally, a decision rendered prior to the denial of a building permit would have to be reached without such help as might be obtained from knowledge of the board's practical construction of the ordinance and of the standards actually applied.

 

185 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mt. St. Mary's Hospital v. Catherwood


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 493; 260 N.E.2d 508; 311 N.Y.S.2d 863; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1286; 74 L.R.R.M. 2897


January 15, 1970, Argued  
May 14, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital challenged an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which sustained the validity of the compulsory arbitration features of N.Y. Lab. Law § 716 (1969) in its declaratory judgment action against respondent labor union representatives (representatives).


OVERVIEW: The hospital and the representatives failed to reach a collective bargaining agreement, and the dispute was submitted to compulsory arbitration pursuant to N.Y. Lab. Law § 716 (1969). The hospital did not submit to arbitration because review of the arbitrator's decision under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 75, the only mode of review expressly provided by N.Y. Lab. Law § 716 (1969), was inadequate to provide the hospital due process of law under the Fourteenth Amendment and N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. The lower court sustained the statute. The court affirmed and found that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7511 permitted review if the arbitrator acted beyond his authority. Such review was sufficient to afford the parties due process in the context of compulsory arbitration as mandated by N.Y. Lab. Law § 716 (1969). The court held that compulsory arbitration and the limited review under § 716 was constitutionally justified and sufficient because the hospital was affected with a profound public interest directly related to the health and lives in the community and was dependent on governmental subsidies, reimbursements, and exemptions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order sustaining the validity of the state's compulsory arbitration statute.


HEADNOTES

Arbitration -- compulsory arbitration -- section 716 of Labor Law provides for compulsory arbitration for disputes in labor contract negotiations with private voluntary and nonprofitmaking hospitals -- application to confirm, modify, correct or vacate arbitration award must be made in accord with CPLR article 75 governing arbitration generally -- article 75, given broad interpretation encompasses standard of review sufficient to afford parties due process -- section 716 of Labor Law is accordingly valid.

1. Section 716 of the Labor Law provides for the compulsory arbitration of disputes in labor contract negotiations with private voluntary or nonprofitmaking hospitals. An application to confirm, modify, correct or vacate an arbitration award must be made in accord with CPLR article 75, the article governing arbitration generally. Because article 75, if given a broad rather than a narrow interpretation, encompasses a standard of review sufficient to afford the parties due process in the context of compulsory arbitration as mandated by section 716 of the Labor Law, the statute is valid.

2. CPLR 7511 (subd. [b]) provides in part: "1. The award shall be vacated on the application of a party * * * if the court finds that the rights of that party were prejudiced by: * * * (iii) an arbitrator, or agency or person making the award exceeded his power or so imperfectly executed it that a final and definite award upon the subject matter submitted was not made". Because of due process limitations affecting compulsory arbitration, the arbitrator is circumscribed by the data or evidence to support the award made by him. His power derives from the statute which mandates upon the parties the arbitration or the agreement for arbitration. The arbitrator must be limited by the same constitutional requirements which limit the statute conferring power on him. The scheme of section 716 is to provide for the resolution of contract negotiation disputes about conditions in the industry on the basis of evidence, hearings, and a record made. This device for arbitration is a substitute for a determination of the dispute by an administrative or regulatory agency. As a substitute device, its objective may not be accomplished under lower constitutional standards than would be required of an administrative or regulatory agency. On this view, CPLR 7511 (subd. [b]), in authorizing review of whether the arbitrator has exceeded his power, by necessary logical extension and without distortion of its literal terms, includes review in the case of compulsory arbitration (but only in such case) of whether the award is supported by evidence or other basis in reason, as may be appropriate, and appearing in the record. Such a review is all that the plaintiff hospital is entitled to obtain.

3. A dispute arising from a failure to reach a collective bargaining agreement is akin to a regulatory or quasi-legislative resolution of the conditions of employment. In such event the test must partake more of the elements associated with the validation of an exercise of legislative power under the standards expressed in the statute since the 1969 amendment (§ 716, subd. 7; L. 1969, ch. 526, § 3). The award may not be arbitrary or capricious, and this indirect exercise of the police power by the arbitrators must be justified by the public interest and reasonable conditions to effect that interest. The arbitrators are not empowered to adopt any plan for the conditions of employment simply to satisfy or meet the wishes either of employer or employees. Given a reasonable basis for the award, the court's power to review, under any theory or by any procedure, ceases. On this interpretation, constitutional limitations are satisfied by the statute for the arbitrators may do no more than the Legislature could, and the review power is limited to the same power that exists to review legislative or quasi-legislative acts.

4. Section 716 has devised a unique procedure for the admixture of administrative, regulatory, and quasi-judicial functions. In that sense, the procedure is sui generis and the fact that article 75 is by itself appropriate only to consensual arbitration should neither require the statute to be condemned as invalid nor, on the other hand, require translation of the concepts of consensual arbitration to compulsory arbitration as if there were no difference between the two. The Legislature had the power to weld the two to the extent that they are compatible and the language of the statutes and the constitutional limitations permit. Giving the "excess of power" clause in section 7511 of CPLR its ordinary meaning, it is sufficiently broad to cover the constitutional limitations. So interpreted, section 716 is valid.

5. The statute is not a violation of equal protection of the law because it applies only to nonprofitmaking facilities.

Thomson Industries, Inc. v. Port Washington North


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


27 N.Y.2d 537; 261 N.E.2d 260; 313 N.Y.S.2d 117; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1317


April 15, 1970, Argued  
May 14, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs, in a memorandum.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) that affirmed with modifications a trial court judgment declaring that defendant's building zone ordinance prohibited a proposed use of plaintiff's property as a helicopter land site, and that this prohibition was valid and not unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court affirmed with modifications a judgment of the trial court declaring that defendant's building zone ordinance prohibited a proposed use of plaintiff's property as a helicopter landing site, and that this prohibition was valid and not unconstitutional. The appellate court modified the trial court judgment by adjudging that N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 249 prohibited the use of plaintiff's property as a helicopter landing site, unless authorization was granted by defendant's governing body in accordance with this section. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court decision. The court found that the zoning ordinance prohibited a "heliport" as a use in the industrial "A" district in which plaintiff's property was located. The court found that the appellate court properly held that, as fairly construed, the ordinance prohibited the activities which plaintiff's operations would necessarily entail. The court found that the ordinance was in derogation of common-law property rights, and therefore must be strictly construed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order of the appellate court that affirmed with modifications a trial court judgment declaring that defendant's building zone ordinance prohibited a proposed use of plaintiff's property as a helicopter landing site and that this prohibition was valid and not unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- helicopter landing site -- Special Term declared that section of village building zone ordinance prohibiting use of plaintiff's property as helicopter landing site was valid and not unconstitutional and dismissed complaint insofar as it sought declaration as to effect of section 249 of General Business Law upon helicopter operations -- Appellate Division modified by further adjudging that section 249 of General  [***4] Business Law prohibited use of property as helicopter landing site, unless authorization was granted by local governing body in accordance with said section -- order of Appellate Division affirmed -- strictly construed, term "heliport" in building zone ordinance is not reasonably applicable to plaintiff's limited, occasional, purely business-connected use of small portion of parking lot for landing and takeoff of its own helicopter -- accordingly, order is affirmed on ground that plaintiff's operation comes within definitions contained in section 240 of General Business Law and requirements of section 249 of said law -- affirmance without prejudice to whatever action plaintiff may be advised to take in light of amended section 249.

Northern Operating Corp. v. Ramapo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 404; 259 N.E.2d 723; 311 N.Y.S.2d 286; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1320


March 5, 1970, Submitted  
May 13, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs in all courts, and case remitted to Supreme Court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant town challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which granted plaintiff developer's motion for summary judgment and directed judgment declaring the town's amended zoning ordinance was invalid for failure to follow statutory requirements. In granting the developer's motion, the trial court reversed a previous lower court decision denying the motion.


OVERVIEW: The developer sought reapproval of two subdivision plans from the town's planning board, but the board denied reapproval based on a finding that the developer's purported use of the land was in conflict with an amended zoning ordinance. The developer alleged that the ordinance in question was invalid because the board failed to promptly enter a copy of the amended ordinance in the minutes. The trial court agreed, and granted the developer's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that in amending the ordinance, the town acted in accordance with the requirements of the governing statute, N.Y. Town Law § 264. The court noted undisputed facts that the minutes were written and officially filed in the proper public office available for inspection in accordance with § 264. The court also noted that while § 264 required that a zoning ordinance be entered into the minutes of the town board, the "minutes" referred to was not necessarily the "ordinance book," and held that the town clerk's failure to promptly put the ordinance into the ordinance book as required by N.Y. Town Law § 30 was personal neglect which did not invalidate the board's public action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order granting summary judgment to the developer on the first cause of action seeking declaratory judgment that the amended ordinance was invalid. The court entered judgment for the town on that cause, with costs. The court also remanded the matter to the lower court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- ordinances -- validity of enactment -- amendment of town's zoning ordinance complied with requirements of Town Law ( § 264) including entry in minutes, even though not entered in "ordinance book", so that defendants are entitled to judgment declaring ordinance valid -- order affirming denial of summary judgment to plaintiff and defendants is nonfinal and not reviewable.

1. An ordinance amending a town's Comprehensive Zoning and Building Ordinance was passed by a majority vote of its Town Board with a written copy before it for consideration. A copy of the ordinance was duly and timely published in several newspapers circulating in the town and was duly posted. It was entered in the minutes of the board meeting by the clerk and kept by her in her official public office, available for inspection by the public. It was not, however, physically incorporated in the "ordinance book" of the town until after October 23, 1967. By every reasonable standard the enactment of the ordinance meets the requirements of section 264 of the Town Law. The neglect of a Town Clerk promptly to put ordinances adopted by the board in the "ordinance book" as required by section 30 of the Town Law ought to be deemed a personal neglect by the clerk insufficient to invalidate the board's action unless there is some resulting uncertainty to someone's detriment. Defendants are entitled to judgment declaring the ordinance valid.

2. Those parts of the order of the Appellate Division affirming the denial of summary judgment to plaintiff and defendants are nonfinal and not reviewable on this appeal by defendants.

191 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Todaro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 325; 258 N.E.2d 711; 310 N.Y.S.2d 303; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1370


March 4, 1970, Argued  
April 16, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified by reversing so much thereof as convicted the defendant of harassment and by dismissing the charge of harassment, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court that found defendant guilty of disorderly conduct in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(3),(6) and harassment in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.25 on the complaint of an arresting officer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of disorderly conduct in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.20(3),(6) and harassment in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 240.25 upon the complaint of the arresting officer who arrested defendant for loitering, which was affirmed on appeal. Defendant sought further judicial review. The court reviewed the record and concluded that there was sufficient evidence that defendant refused to obey an order by the arresting officer to move and stop loitering, which supported the disorderly conduct conviction. The court also noted that the refusal to obey such an order could have been justified only where the circumstances showed conclusively that the officer's direction was purely arbitrary and not calculated to promote public order, which was not the case under the facts. However, the court reversed and dismissed the charge of harassment because it was only based on comments by defendant directed against the officer upon defendant's arrest. The court held that defendant's mere comments only expressed his annoyance at being arrested and did not establish his intent to annoy or alarm the officer, or that he threatened to strike, shove, or kick the officer.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for disorderly conduct was affirmed because there was no evidence that an arresting officer arbitrarily ordered defendant to stop loitering. Defendant's conviction for harassment was reversed and the charge was dismissed because defendant's comments did not indicate defendant's intent to threaten, strike, or otherwise physically harm the arresting officer.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disorderly conduct -- harassment -- in prosecution for disorderly conduct (Penal Law, § 240.20, subds. 3, 6), proof of accomplished fact of public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm not required -- trial court could find beyond reasonable doubt that defendant, using abusive and obscene language in response to police officer's requests to move on and refusing to move on, was guilty of disorderly conduct -- attack on constitutionality of statute without merit -- not necessary that complaint charging harassment refer specifically to any subdivision -- evidence that defendant stated to police officer "I'll get you for this" did not suffice to establish guilt of harassment (Penal Law, § 240.25, subd. 1) -- complaint dismissed as to harassment.

1. Defendant was convicted of disorderly conduct on the complaint of a police officer that he congregated with others in a public place, used abusive and obscene language, and refused to comply with the police officer's order to move on after several warnings. The Appellate Term, in affirming the judgment of conviction, was correct in concluding that proof of the accomplished fact of public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm was not required. Proof only from which the risk of it, recklessly created, might be inferred was required (Penal Law, § 240.20, subd. 6). The trial court could have found beyond reasonable doubt that defendant was aware of and consciously disregarded a substantial and unjustifiable risk that public inconvenience, annoyance or alarm might result from his use of abusive and obscene language in response to the officer's repeated requests to move on and his refusal to comply with such requests.

2. Defendant's attack on the constitutionality of the statute is without merit, both as to a challenge of the statute on its face and as applied to the facts.

3. A complaint charging harassment is sufficient if the evidence establishes a violation of any one of the subdivisions of the harassment statute. It is not necessary that the complaint refer specifically to any one of the subdivisions.

4. The evidence on the harassment charge was insufficient. The statute provides that "A person is guilty of harassment when, with intent to harass, annoy or alarm another person: 1. He strikes, shoves, kicks or otherwise subjects him to physical contact, or attempts or threatens to do the same" (Penal Law, § 240.25, subd. 1). The only evidence bearing upon the charge was the police officer's testimony that the appellant stated: "I'll get you for this". This statement did not suffice to establish beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant "with intent to harass, annoy or harm" the police officer, attempted or threatened to "strike, shove, kick or otherwise subject him to physical contact". The complaint should be dismissed to the extent of the charge of harassment.

 

192 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Scott


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 286; 258 N.E.2d 206; 309 N.Y.S.2d 919; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1383


January 19, 1970, Argued  
April 15, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant landlord appealed the judgment of the County Court of Broome County (New York), which convicted him of violating the town code section prohibiting open storage of one or more inoperable vehicles. On appeal, the landlord argued that the ordinance was invalid insofar as it directed him, as an owner who by lease had relinquished the right to enter upon the property, to remove property placed upon it by the tenant.


OVERVIEW: A landlord leased part of his property to his son, who stored a number of inoperable vehicles on the land. After the lease was signed, the town passed an ordinance that prohibited residents from keeping inoperable vehicles on their land. The landlord was convicted of violating the ordinance. On appeal, the landlord argued that he had no right of re-entry on the leased property and that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The court reversed the landlord's conviction and held that the landlord had no right of re-entry on the land, so he could not be held responsible because he had no control over the property at the time he was served. The court also held that the ordinance was an arbitrary exercise of police power and violated due process protections. The court found that the ordinance was too harsh because it did not even allow for the storage of one car on a resident's premises, and the word "inoperable" in the ordinance was too vague for residents to know how to conform their behavior to the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the landlord's conviction and dismissed the information.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- ordinance prohibiting storage of vehicles not in operating condition -- town ordinance which prohibits storage, on private property, of motor vehicles not in operating condition invalid insofar as it directs defendant owner who, by lease, relinquished right to enter upon private property, to remove motor vehicles on leased premises -- landlord had no right of entry for such purpose -- defendant could be responsible only if he had right of entry.

Defendant, owner of a 30-acre tract of land in the Town of Vestal, leased 20 acres to his son for an indefinite term which could be terminated by a 60-day notice given by either party. On the leased premises, the son stored approximately 100 disabled motor vehicles. Subsequent to the execution of the lease, an ordinance was enacted by the town which provided that: "Open storage of one (1) or more motor vehicles which are not in operating condition for legal use on the public highways shall not be permitted on private property within the Town of Vestal." Written notice was given to defendant owner to remove these vehicles and, upon his failure to comply, he was prosecuted for a violation of this ordinance. The ordinance is invalid insofar as it directs defendant, as an owner who, by lease, has relinquished the right to enter upon the property, to remove property placed upon it by the tenant. Defendant may be held responsible only if he had a right of entry at the time that notice was served upon him. That right must be found in the lease or in a statute. It may not be found in the ordinance either directly or impliedly, for the Legislature has not authorized that kind of interference with the landlord-tenant relationship. In the absence of a reserved right of entry, the landlord has no common-law right to enter upon the demised premises. Defendant has reserved such a right only for nonpayment of rent.

195 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Second Report of November, 1968 Grand Jury


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 200; 257 N.E.2d 859; 309 N.Y.S.2d 297; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1459; 63 A.L.R.3d 571


December 8, 1969, Argued  
March 5, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 253-a which authorized a grand jury to submit a report to the court concerning non-criminal misconduct by public officers. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York) affirmed trial court orders denying motions for inspections of grand jury minutes.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the employees were entitled to have their constitutional question passed upon by the court of appeals as of right. The court found that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 517-a could not have been invoked to interfere with the appeal on that question even if the proceeding was deemed criminal in nature. On the merits of the appeal, the court found that a reversal was required where the court had denied employees' motions for inspection of the minutes of the grand jury upon which its report was based. The court found that the trial court's denial of the motion, premised on the ground that it lacked authority to grant such an application limited the accused employees to a bare unsupported and unsubstantiated list of charges and allegations against them, which would have served to deprive them of that opportunity to be heard which is guaranteed to them by the Due Process Clause of the state constitution, N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6. The court, however, refused to find N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 253-a unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The orders appealed from were reversed and the matter remitted to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Grand Jury -- report -- State Constitution does not warrant invalidation of section 253-a of Code of Criminal Procedure which authorizes grand jury to submit report to court concerning noncriminal misconduct or neglect in office by public officer or employee -- court did not lack authority to grant motions for inspection of minutes of grand jury upon which its report was based -- appeal properly before Court of Appeals whether proceeding under section 253-a is regarded as criminal or civil -- Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 517-a) does not prevent challenge to constitutionality of procedure authorized by section 253-a.

1. Section 253-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure authorizes the grand jury to submit a report to the court concerning noncriminal misconduct or neglect in office by a public officer or employee. Although the State Constitution does not itself vest the grand jury with power to make a report charging non-criminal misconduct, it does not prohibit the enactment of legislation authorizing such a report. Nothing in section 6 of article I or section 1 of article III of the Constitution warrants invalidation of this statute.

2. The demands of due process and a regard for fundamental fairness dictated that the evidence before the Grand Jury relating to the appellants should have been made available to them not only before they went forward with their appeals but before they were required to file their answers. Unless the grand jury minutes contain matter that must be kept confidential or unless disclosure of those minutes would be inimical to the public interests, the motions to inspect the minutes should have been granted.

3. The appeal, taken both by permission (Code Crim. Pro., § 520) and as of right ( CPLR 5601, subd. [b]), is properly before the Court of Appeals, whether the proceeding under section 253-a be regarded as criminal or civil.

4. While section 517-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure recites that the Appellate Division in such a proceeding "shall be the sole court having jurisdiction of * * * an appeal" and that its order "shall not be subject to [further] review" (subd. 7), that provision was intended merely to limit further review and consideration as to the sufficiency of the evidence supporting the grand jury's report and not to preclude and prevent a challenge to the constitutionality of the procedure authorized by section 253-a.

Metropolitan Transp. Authority v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 817; 257 N.E.2d 898; 309 N.Y.S.2d 352; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1541


December 10, 1969, Argued  
February 19, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified in accordance with the memorandum herein, and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- railroads -- passenger stations -- controversy submitted to Appellate Division upon agreed statement of facts involved amounts due to Metropolitan Transportation Authority (MTA) from City of New York (City) pursuant to section 1277 of Public Authorities Law, which provided that total cost of "operation, maintenance and use of each passenger station" of railroad within City of New York was to be borne by city -- Appellate Division adjudged that Pennsylvania, Jamaica and Flatbush Avenue stations of Long Island Railroad were passenger stations within meaning of said statute, specified costs which it found to be part of total cost of operation, maintenance and use of passenger stations within meaning of statute and costs which it found to be not part of said total cost, and allowed deduction, from total cost, of net revenues realized from concessions and rentals and revenues from redcap tariffs -- order of Appellate Division modified by providing that costs incurred at Jamaica station in sale of tickets by mail and costs incurred in operation of system-wide lost and found offices at Pennsylvania and Jamaica stations be apportioned among all stations, and by providing that, in determining total cost, revenues from concessions and rentals and from redcap tariffs shall not be deducted, and, as so modified, order affirmed. 

 

197 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Radich


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 114; 257 N.E.2d 30; 308 N.Y.S.2d 846; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1558


December 1, 1969, Argued  
February 18, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) that convicted him of violating the state's flag desecration statute, former N.Y. Penal Law § 1425(16)(d), now N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 136(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, as proprietor of an art gallery, publicly displayed sculptures that prominently incorporated the American flag in their expression of protest against the Vietnam War. He was convicted of violating the state's flag desecration statute, and the intermediate appellate court affirmed that judgment. On appeal the court affirmed, holding that the statute did not violate defendant's First Amendment right of free speech. Whether or not defendant thought so, the acts portrayed in the sculptures dishonored the flag, and the trial court could have properly found that implicit in the invitation to view the sculptures was the opportunity to join in the protest, or in counter-protest, with the consequent potential of public disorder. The court agreed with the trial court's conclusion that the legitimate interest of public peace that the statute was designed to protect was threatened by the violation of which defendant was convicted. Although defendant may have held a sincere ideological viewpoint, he was required to find ways to express it that did not violate a valid law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's order sustaining defendant's conviction for violating New York's flag desecration statute.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- defilement of flag -- proprietor of art gallery who displayed construction, comparable to sculptures, made out of American flag for purpose of protesting Vietnam war was properly found guilty of defiling American flag in violation of former Penal Law ( § 1425, subd. 16, par. d); statute does not impinge on defendant's right of free speech under First Amendment -- statute is not unconstitutionally vague -- intent not essential to conviction for violation of malum prohibitum statute.

1. Defendant, proprietor of an art gallery who publicly displayed and exposed for sale, certain constructions, comparable to sculptures, which had been fashioned, as protests against the Vietnam war, out of the American flag, was properly found guilty of violating paragraph d of subdivision 16 of section 1425 of the former Penal Law, which proscribes the public defilement of the American flag by act. The statute here involved does not impinge on defendant's right of free speech under the First Amendment. Defendant may express his ideas but may not break a valid law to do so. The flag desecration statute is aimed at keeping the public peace. The public peace was threatened by defendant's violations.

2. The statute is not unconstitutionally vague.

3. Whatever defendant's intent in exhibiting the constructions, intent is not essential to a conviction of violating a statute which is malum prohibitum.

Vergari v. Marcus


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 764; 257 N.E.2d 652; 309 N.Y.S.2d 204; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1566


January 14, 1970, Argued  
February 18, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified in accordance with the memorandum herein and, as so modified, affirmed, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- constitutional right to jury trial in forfeiture proceeding -- pursuant to section 3353 of Public Health Law, providing for seizure and forfeiture of vehicles, vessels or aircraft unlawfully used to conceal, convey or transport narcotics, District Attorney instituted proceeding for judgment declaring forfeiture of respondent's automobile -- after nonjury trial, Special Term granted District Attorney's petition -- Appellate Division reversed, dismissed proceeding and directed return of automobile to respondent, concluding that statute was unconstitutional insofar as it authorized imposition of forfeiture without jury trial -- order of Appellate Division modified and case remitted for trial by jury -- cases involving judicial proceedings for forfeiture or penalties were tried by jury before adoption of 1846 State Constitution and, therefore, section 2 of article I of State Constitution guarantees right to jury trial in present proceeding -- instant statute does not prohibit trial by jury nor detail any of proceedings to be followed -- hence it was incumbent on court to provide appropriate proceedings, including jury trial mandated by Constitution. 

 

199 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rosemond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 101; 257 N.E.2d 23; 308 N.Y.S.2d 836; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1573


December 12, 1969, Argued  
January 22, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court of New York in the Second Judicial Department, which affirmed a judgment of the trial court convicting defendant, on his plea of guilty, of attempted burglary in the third degree. Defendant's motion to suppress the evidence because of an unlawful search was denied.


OVERVIEW: A policeman saw defendant and his companion enter and later leave an apartment building carrying a bag. Upon being questioned, defendant told police that he did not know what was in the bag. He said that he got it around the block and took police to the apartment where the lock to the door was broken. Defendant was arrested and indicted for attempted burglary in the third degree. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the evidence because of an unlawful search. The appellate division affirmed this judgment. On appeal, the court held that there was no violation of defendant's Fourth Amendment rights or N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 180-a. There existed reasonable grounds to arrest defendant, at least for larceny, from the moment defendant made the highly suggestive answer that he did not know what was in the bag he had just carried out of the house. The police only asked questions until the answers themselves gave probable cause for a search and detention. The professional experience of the police dictated, to a large extent, what was unusual enough to warrant inquiry.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and the judgment of the lower court denying the motion to suppress evidence.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- attempted burglary -- police inquiry -- policeman observed defendant and another enter apartment building, empty handed, and when they came out carrying suitcase and shopping bag, officer made inquiries -- as result of questioning and investigation, he found that material in bags belonged to tenant in apartment -- Code of Criminal Procedure (§ 180-a), which authorizes police officer to stop citizen and question him where he has reasonably grounded suspicion, does not limit previously existing broader common-law basis for police inquiry -- inquiry here was lawful -- no violation of defendant's constitutional rights.

While on patrol a policeman observed defendant and another man enter an apartment building empty handed. A short time later, he saw the two men coming out of the building carrying a suitcase and a shopping bag. The officer asked the men what was in the package and, as a result of his questioning and subsequent investigation, he found that the material in the bags belonged to a tenant in one of the apartments. Defendant and his companion were arrested and indicted. Section 180-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure which authorizes a police officer to stop a citizen and demand of him certain specific explanatory information where there is a reasonably grounded suspicion that the citizen is or was committing or will commit a felony or certain serious misdemeanors does not prescribe the full scope of police activity. It should not be read to narrow down the normal duty of police to find out by suitable inquiry what is happening in the public streets. The right of inquiry upon grounds less compelling than those which would justify an arrest and less compelling than those stated in the statute was a previously existing common-law basis for police inquiry. This right continues and is not impaired by the provisions of the statute. The inquiry in this case itself was lawful and the answers to the inquiry afforded sufficient ground to look further. There was no violation of defendant's constitutional rights.

 

200 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gleason v. Gleason


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


26 N.Y.2d 28; 256 N.E.2d 513; 308 N.Y.S.2d 347; 1970 N.Y. LEXIS 1596


November 25, 1969, Argued  
January 21, 1970, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) reversed the decision of the trial court denying appellant husband's motion to dismiss the complaint and granting respondent wife's cross motion. In the second action, the trial court granted respondent wife's motion to dismiss the complaint. The husbands in the first and second action appealed.


OVERVIEW: The two actions were brought about because the couples had been living apart for more than two years. The appellate court had held in both cases that N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 170(5) did not apply to pre-existing judgments of separation. The court held that the legislature intended that § 170(5) should be applied retroactively in the sense of encompassing pre-1966 decrees and that it did not offend due process, the equal protection of the law, or any other constitutional provision. The court held that the operative fact was that the parties had actually lived apart for two years after September 1, 1966, pursuant to separation decrees. The court held that equal protection rights were not violated by making § 170(5) prospective because the differentiation between a party to a pre-1966 separation decree and a party to a pre-1966 separation agreement rested upon some rational consideration which was not arbitrary. The court also noted that a marriage was not a contract for the purposes of the Contracts Clause of the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed both of the decisions of the appellate court.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- divorce -- retroactive application of statute -- section 170 (subd. [5]) of Domestic Relation Law, which provides as grounds for granting divorce that husband and wife have lived apart for two years after grant of separation judgment or decree intended to apply retroactively -- immaterial whether party is "innocent" or "guilty" -- no vested rights of defendant wife adversely affected and no denial of due process -- no denial of equal protection of laws where Legislature distinguished between decrees and agreements -- no unconstitutional impairment of contract rights.

1. Section 170 (subd. [5]) of the Domestic Relations Law provides, as grounds for granting a divorce, that a husband and wife have lived apart for a period of two years (subsequent to September 1, 1966, L. 1966, ch. 254, § 15) after the granting of a separation judgment or decree (L. 1966, ch. 254, eff. Sept. 1, 1967). The provisions of subdivision (5) are available where the decree was rendered prior to the enactment of the new statute. The Legislature intended that subdivision (5) of section 170 of the Domestic Relations Law should be applied, retroactively, in the sense of encompassing pre-1966 decrees.

2. It makes no difference whether it is the "innocent" or "guilty" party who seeks to convert the judicial separation into a final divorce. The Legislature intended to abandon the traditional fault approach to divorce and permit the termination of marriages even where both parties are at fault -- except in cases of adultery (Domestic Relations Law, § 171).

3. Marital rights have always been treated as inchoate or contingent and may be taken away by legislation before they vest. A wife's prospective right of inheritance is inchoate, expectant and does not become vested until the death of her husband. A spouse has no vested rights in the marital status or in social security or pension benefits. The State, having the power directly to limit or abolish rights of succession to the property of a living person, may do so indirectly by providing a new ground for divorce. Since no vested rights of the defendants have been adversely affected, there has been no denial of due process.

4. The Legislature, by making subdivision (5) retroactive and subdivision (6), which involves separation agreements, prospective, did not unreasonably differentiate between a party to a pre-1966 separation decree and a party to a pre-1966 separation agreement. There is no denial of equal protection of the laws since the differences between separation decrees and separation agreements justify the distinction which has been made.

5. The statute did not unconstitutionally impair contract rights. Marriage is not a contract within the meaning of the provision of the Federal Constitution which prohibits the impairment by the States of the obligation of contracts.
